# Rust Essential Training

> If you know even a little bit about programming languages, you know there are a plethora of options to choose from. If your priority is a language focused on reliability, speed, memory, safety, and parallelism, then Rust may be the one for you. In this course, instructor Barron Stone covers the key components of Rust, starting with basic programming concepts including variables, data types, functi

### Introduction

#### Learn Rust programming
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] What do you look for in a programming language?
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=3)** There are a ton of languages out there to choose from, all optimized for different types of applications.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=9)** If performance and reliability are at the top of your list of priorities, then Rust is a great language to learn.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=17)** It enables you to write efficient programs that run blazingly fast, and it's structured to be memory-safe and prevent common bugs, so you can be confident in your code.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=26)** Those features, along with great tools, documentation and a welcoming community have made Rust a language that developers love.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=35)** It's perfect for low-level systems programming but Rust is also being used to write everything from web apps and network services to embedded programs.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=44)** I'm Barron Stone, and I'm here to get you up and running with Rust.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/learn-rust-programming?u=76281980&t=48)** We've got a lot to cover, so let's get to it.

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] This course is intended for beginners.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=2)** So you do not need to have any prior experience with Rust.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=6)** We'll be starting from the very beginning.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=9)** It will be helpful to have some amount of experience programming with other languages.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=14)** But even that's not a strict requirement.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=17)** If you're a seasoned programmer, you'll probably find yourself in familiar territory during the first half of this course as we cover common concepts like variables, functions, conditional expressions, and loops.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=29)** To see how they're implemented in Rust.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=31)** In the second half of the course, we'll get in to some of the more unique concepts that make Rust different from other languages.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=39)** Like ownership and the borrow checker.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=42)** Now, Rust does have a reputation as being a difficult language to learn.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=47)** And I'll admit it's not the easiest language.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=50)** But is not necessarily hard.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=53)** It really comes down to having the right mindset.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=56)** Learning the languages syntax is just a small part of the journey.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=60)** The bigger piece is learning to think properly in terms of Rust.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=64)** Because of it's differences compared to other languages.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=67)** To help build that understanding, I've included several challenge problems throughout this course.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=72)** And I encourage you to attempt them for practice along the way.

#### What is Rust?
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] Before we dive headfirst into writing Rust code, let's take a step back to understand what makes Rust special and why developers would want to use it.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=10)** Rust is a relatively young language making its first appearance in 2010 as a Mozilla research project.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=18)** It's an open source systems programming language whose syntax looks similar to C plus plus.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=24)** Rust builds on decades of experience and lessons learned from programmers using C plus plus and other related languages to create a modern programming language that's designed for performance and reliability or safety especially for concurrent programming.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=40)** Like C plus plus, Rust is a compiled language meaning it uses a program called a compiler to take the source code you write and turn it into machine code that can run natively on the system.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=52)** That means Rust programs can be incredibly fast and efficient with performance similar to C plus plus, and in some cases even better.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=61)** Rust is also a statically and strongly typed language.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=64)** Statically typed means the data type for every variable must be known at compile time and cannot be dynamically changed later and strongly typed means there are restrictions on converting between data types which are designed to help prevent writing incorrect programs.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=80)** Those are all things Rust has in common with C plus plus the main way Rust differs is how it manages memory.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=88)** In C plus plus, invalid memory access is a notoriously common source of bugs.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=93)** Rust solves that problem by using an ownership model that allows the compiler to check all memory accesses to guarantee that they're valid that makes Rust program's memory safe by default.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=104)** It's not possible to accidentally corrupt your data in memory.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=108)** Also, since those checks are performed at compile time, it doesn't impact runtime performance like other types of dynamic memory management such as garbage collection.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=119)** The one downside to Rust's ownership model is that it's different than what most programmers are used to and therefore requires a different way of thinking which we'll cover in this course.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=130)** With Rust promising such great performance and reliability, you might be wondering if it's going to replace C plus plus and maybe someday but it's not going to happen overnight.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=140)** Applications today are built on decades worth of C plus plus code and libraries so Rust has a long way to catch up to replace all that legacy code.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=149)** That said, major tech companies are actively supporting Rust development as sponsors and they're choosing Rust for some of their newer projects because its guarantee of memory safety is a huge advantage.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=161)** Rust adoption is increasing and the language has been steadily climbing in popularity.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=167)** In addition to the guiding principles of performance and reliability, Rust is also focused on enabling productivity by having a robust compiler that provides useful messages and hints, support for common development tools and great documentation.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=182)** You can find a lot of that documentation like going to the learn page on [Rustlearn.org](https://Rustlearn.org).
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=188)** At the top of this page, you'll find three fantastic resources for learning Rust intended for different styles of learners.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=196)** If you prefer books, you can read the Rust Programming Language also known simply as The Book which is a go-to resource for getting started with Rust.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=205)** If you prefer to learn by looking at example code then Rust by example has you covered.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=211)** As you go through this course if you find yourself struggling with certain concepts, I recommend using these resources as alternative explanations and examples.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=221)** Further down the page, you'll find the core Rust documentation which will certainly come in handy as you work your way through this course and below that are several other guides on specific application domains as well as more advanced concepts and Rust.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=238)** If those resources don't answer your questions, you can also ask around on the Rust community forum or the active Rust sub reddit.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/what-is-rust?u=76281980&t=246)** There's a great community behind Rust and it keeps on growing.

#### Rust versions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=0)** - Before we install Rust and start using it, I want to make a quick comment about the versions of Rust you'll see used in this course.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=8)** Rust is an actively developed language and a new version is usually released every six weeks or so.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=15)** You can learn about the main features in each version, by going to the blog on [rust-lang.org](https://rust-lang.org) and reading the announcement posts there.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=22)** Fortunately each new version of Rust aims to be backwards compatible with previous versions.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=29)** New versions may introduce features and enhancements to make the programming experience better, but there shouldn't be any significant issues using a newer version of the Rust compiler to follow along with the examples here.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=41)** So for the majority of this course, you'll see me using Rust version 1.48.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-versions?u=76281980&t=47)** And when new features are released, that impact parts of this course, I'll update the corresponding video to explicitly let you know what's changed, and which version of Rust introduced the update.

### 1. Write Your First Program

#### Installing Rust on Windows
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The recommended way to install Rust is through a command line tool named Rustup, which is used to manage Rust versions and its associated tools.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=9)** To get Rustup for Windows, go to the install page on [rust-lang.org](https://rust-lang.org), download rustup-init.exe.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=21)** I'll switch to my downloads to find that file and then run it.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=26)** Using Rust on Windows requires the Visual Studio C++ build tools to be installed on your system before installing Rustup.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=35)** If you see a warning message like this one, that means they're not installed.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=39)** And since I'm getting that message here, there's another step I'll need to take before continuing to install Rustup.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=45)** So I'll close this installer window, switch back to my web browser, and I'll search for Visual Studio download.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=57)** I'll go to this first link for a downloads page, scroll down to the section that says "Tools for Visual Studio", and then at the bottom of that section, download the build tools for Visual Studio.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=77)** I'll switch back to my downloads folder to run that installer, follow the instructions, and then select this option for C++ build tools.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=95)** I'll click install.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=96)** And it'll take a little while for the installer to run.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=104)** After the installer finishes, it'll prompt you to restart your system.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=109)** After rebooting, I'll return to my downloads folder to run rustup-init.exe again.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=116)** Notice that this time you do not see the warning about needing to install build tools.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=122)** I'll press 1 and then Enter to proceed with the installation.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=130)** And if everything is successful, you'll see this message that Rust was installed.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=135)** And pressing Enter again will close the console.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=139)** To verify that Rust was installed correctly, I'll open a new command prompt by pressing the Windows key and the R key, typing "CMD", and then pressing Enter.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=150)** Now I'll type the command "rustc --version" and press Enter.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=158)** "rustc" is the Rust compiler, and I can see here that version 1.48 is installed.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=164)** If a newer version of Rust comes out later and I want to update my installation, I can simply use the command "rustup update", and that will make sure I have the latest version of the tool chain.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=177)** When you install Rust, it includes documentation that can be accessed offline using the command "rustup doc".
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=186)** This will open up a browser window navigated to the documentation web pages stored locally on your computer.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=193)** Of course, you can also access the most current version of those documents online on [rust-lang.org](https://rust-lang.org).
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=200)** To write code throughout this course, I'll be using the Visual Studio Code editor, but feel free to use any other code editor if you have a different preference.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=210)** If you want to use VS Code to follow along with me, you can download the installer for a variety of operating systems here on code.[visualstudio.com](https://visualstudio.com).
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=220)** After installing VS Code, you can optionally install an extension to add support for features like autocomplete that can make writing code with Rust easier.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=230)** To do that, go to the extension sidebar and search the marketplace for Rust.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=237)** I'll select this first extension, named Rust, produced by the Rust Programming Language, and then I'll click the install button.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-windows?u=76281980&t=246)** That's all there is to it, and now we're good to go.

#### Installing Rust on Linux and macOS
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] If you're using an operating system other than Windows such as Linux or macOS you can follow the link on the install page of [rust-lang.org](https://rust-lang.org) or other installation methods.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=14)** Scroll down to the section on other ways to install rustup, select this shell command, copy it, switch over to a terminal, paste it, and then press Enter to run it.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=32)** This will download and run a shell script which will then download and install the correct version of rustup for your system.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=39)** I'll select option one to proceed with the installation.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=46)** And everything worked correctly, so I get this message that Rust is installed, great.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=51)** Now we also get this message that Cargo's bend directory needs to be included in our path environment variable.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=58)** That should get done automatically the next time you log out and log back into the computer, but we can also configure it for the current shell using the command source $HOME/.cargo/env.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=76)** Now, if I type rustc -- version and press Enter I see that version 1.48 of the Rust compiler was installed.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/installing-rust-on-linux-and-macos?u=76281980&t=86)** If a newer version of the Rust compiler comes out later and I want to update my installation I can simply use the command rustup update and that will make sure I have the latest version of the tool chain, finally, to access the documentation I can use the rustup doc command which will open up a browser window and navigate to the documentation pages that were installed locally on this computer.

>

> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/anatomy-of-a-rust-program?u=76281980&t=111)** We'll cover how to define and use other functions in more detail later in this course but for now notice that the parentheses after main are empty indicating the main function does not accept any inputs.

> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/anatomy-of-a-rust-program?u=76281980&t=124)** Now, for the real meat of our program inside the main function we'll type four spaces println!

>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/anatomy-of-a-rust-program?u=76281980&t=168)** We can write this code with the print statement indented or not using indentation to organize the code helps make it easier to read as a human programmer but to the Rust compiler, that spacing doesn't matter.

#### Adding comments
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're writing code, you're not just writing it for the compiler, You also need to be able to understand your code, as do other programmers that might inherit it later on.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=10)** So it can be useful to include notes for yourself and those other programmers to explain things like what a particular section of code does.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=18)** Or why you made certain design decisions.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=21)** And you can do that by embedding comments in the source code.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=25)** The syntax for comments in Rust is similar to languages like Java and C++.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=30)** To insert a single line comment, we write two forward slashes followed by our message.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=36)** Let's label this program as my hello world program.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=42)** The compiler will ignore everything to the right of the double slashes until the end of the line.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=48)** Code editors with syntax coloring often use green by default to indicate comments as shown here.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=53)** Though if you prefer something else, most code editors will allow you to configure the colors used for syntax highlighting.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=61)** Since the compiler will ignore everything to the right of the double slashes, we can also leave single line comments at the end of lines of code.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=70)** Let's leave a note that this line prints.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=75)** If we want to leave comments that span multiple lines, we can type a forward slash, followed by an asterisk.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=82)** When the compiler sees that pattern it will ignore everything that comes after it until it encounters a second special pattern indicating the end of the comment.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=91)** Notice that the code editor has colored the rest of the program after line three green, indicating it's a comment.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=99)** Let's write a multiple line comment and then end it with an asterisk, followed by a forward slash.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=111)** Notice that the editor changed the color of the print line macro, because it's no longer part of a comment.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=117)** Now to show that the compiler will ignore all of these comments, I'll save the file, compile it by typing rustc main.rs under the terminal, and then run the executable.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=132)** That gives us the normal hello world output because the comments didn't affect anything.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=138)** I'd like to end with one final comment regarding comments.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=141)** And that is not to over comment your code.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=144)** I've seen programmers get carried away and feel like they need to leave comments explaining what every single line of code does, which is almost always unnecessary.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=153)** For example, the comment on line six saying that "this line prints" doesn't tell me anything that's not already obvious, looking at that line of code.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=162)** Which is obviously a print macro.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=165)** Excessive comments like that don't help.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=167)** They just clutter up the page and we don't need that.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/adding-comments?u=76281980&t=172)** Try to limit comments to your thoughts and other information that's not readily apparent from just looking at the code.

#### Building programs using Cargo
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Rust programmers don't usually invoke the rest compiler directly like we did in the previous hello world example by calling the Rustc command.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=10)** Doing so worked fine in that case because hello world was a simple program with only one source file and no external dependencies.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=18)** So we only needed to pass Rustc the one main file as input.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=23)** But as your programs grow in complexity and span tens, or even hundreds of files with references to multiple external libraries, trying to build that with a Rustc command gets complicated.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=36)** That's why most programmers build their projects using the Rust package manager named Cargo.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=42)** Cargo exists to help you manage dependencies and build your programs in a repeatable manner.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=48)** It does things like download and build the external libraries your program needs, and it handles calling Rustc with all the correct parameters for your program.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=58)** The developers of Rust realized that having a good package manager was essential for modern programmers.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=64)** So they made the decision to include Cargo as part of the standard Rust distribution.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=69)** That means when you installed Rustup, you also got Cargo.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=74)** You can check which version of Cargo you have installed from a terminal by typing cargo --version.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=82)** I see here, I have version 1.48 installed.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=85)** To create a new project with Cargo, use the command cargo new followed by a name for the project.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=92)** I'll call this hello_world.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=96)** Cargo will create a new directory named hello world and populate it with the project files.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=103)** I'll open that up in VS code's file explorer, by going to file, open folder, navigating to the desktop where I created it, using select folder and then expanding it.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=118)** Within the top level project directory, we can see that Cargo has generated two files.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=123)** If you're using git for version control, Cargo will initialize a new git repository for your project, along with a .getignore file, which tells git which files or folders to ignore.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=135)** We won't worry about that for this course.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=137)** The other file is Cargo.toml, which is a configuration file for this project.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=144)** T O M L stands for Tom's Obvious Minimal Language.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=148)** It's a text-based format for configuration files that's designed to be minimalist and easy to read, hence the word obvious in its name.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=158)** Looking at our Cargo.toml file, the first line is a section header for information to configure a package and it's followed by four lines of information, including the package name, version, author, and edition.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=173)** To populate the author field, Cargo will look for information from git to detect the author name and email address.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=181)** That fourth edition field refers to the edition of Rust you're using.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=185)** The Rust language is under continuous development, releasing updates several times per year.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=191)** The changes from one version to the next are generally small, but over time, all of those changes add up.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=197)** So every two or three years or so, the Rust team produces a new Rust edition representing the state of Rust at that time with updated documentation and tools.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=209)** The first edition of Rust arrived in May of 2015 with the release of Rust version 1.0.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=215)** As the initial default edition, it focused on stability.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=220)** In December of 2018, the release of Rust version 1.31 established the next edition 2018, which was themed around productivity.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=230)** And most recently, in October of 2021, Rust version 1.56 gave us a new 2021 edition.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=238)** Code written for older editions will always be compatible with newer edition compilers, but newer additions can include code features that don't exist in older versions of the compiler.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=249)** Since I originally recorded this course in late 2020 using Rust version 1.48, the newest edition at that time was 2018.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=258)** So that's what you'll see used throughout most of this course.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=261)** This edition field in the Cargo.toml file lets us specify up to which edition we need feature compatibility for our program.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=270)** The last line in this file is a section header for dependencies on external code libraries.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=276)** Right now our default Cargo project doesn't have any dependencies, but later on in this course, we'll see how to modify Cargo.toml to include external libraries referred to as crates.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=290)** In addition to the .gitignore and Cargo.toml files, this new project also contains a subdirectory named SRC, which is where Cargo expects you to put all of your source code files.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=303)** If I expand that directory, we can see that it contains a single file named main.rs.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=309)** And if I open it, it contains the code for hello world.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=314)** This is the default code that Cargo uses to populate the main source code file when creating a new project, because it serves as a minimal amount of code that can be successfully compiled and run.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=325)** So those are the basic parts of a new Cargo project.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=329)** To build and run this project, we'll need to navigate into the project directory.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=335)** I'll expand my terminal, type cd hello world, to change the working directory.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=342)** And then I'll use the command Cargo run.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=347)** That command will compile and build all parts of the program and then run it.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=351)** And we can see the output from the default hello world code in the terminal.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=356)** Notice that doing that also created a few additional things within our project directory.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=361)** This Cargo.lock file is generated by Cargo.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=364)** It contains exact information about the project dependencies, leave it alone and do not manually edit it.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=373)** The target folder contains the files that were generated from building the program.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=378)** By default, Cargo will build the program in debug mode, which includes additional information for debugging.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=385)** So the result goes into this directory named debug.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=389)** We can see that the debug directory contains the executable that was generated, along with a bunch of other debug information.
>
> **[6:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=396)** After you're done developing and debugging your program, and you're ready to build a final version of it for release, you can do that with the command cargo build
>
> **[6:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=409)** - -release.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=412)** That builds the program with optimizations and the resulting output will go into the target release directory.
>
> **[7:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=420)** We've only covered the basics of Cargo that we'll need for this course, but Cargo has a lot more advanced capabilities to help you manage your projects.
>
> **[7:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/building-programs-using-cargo-14410861?u=76281980&t=428)** If you want to dive deeper into Cargo, I recommend exploring the Cargo book, which is available online as part of the ecosystem of Rust documentation.

### 2. Primitive Data Types

>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=187)** By default variables in Rust are immutable which means they can not be changed or mutated.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=193)** Once you bind a value to a variable name, it's set.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=197)** You cannot change it to a different value later.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=200)** This may seem counterintuitive because the term variable kind of implies something that can change.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=207)** And in many other common programming languages this default restriction doesn't exist.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=212)** If you need to change the value of a variable in Rust, you have to explicitly declare that variable as being mutable, using the key word mut.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=221)** This is one example of how Rust is designed with safety and reliability in mind.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=226)** By requiring you to make it clear that yes, you intend that variable to be mutable.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=232)** The compiler is able to protect against situations where one part of your code might declare a variable expecting it to stay constant.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=240)** And then another part of code that you develop later on makes changes to that variable.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=245)** That's a common source of software bugs that can be difficult to track down.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=249)** And Rust does its part to prevent that by requiring variables to be explicitly labeled as mutable.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=255)** So let's modify this program by declaring the variable X as mutable.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=261)** I'll save that change, build and run again.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=266)** And now it works, printing two messages one from when X was 10, and then the second after we changed it to a value of 20.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=274)** For this example, I named the variable X for simplicity but Rust allows you to use much more than simple single letter variable names.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=283)** Variable names in Rust, can be composed of a combination of letters, numeric digits, and underscore characters.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=290)** However, the name cannot begin with a number only a letter or an underscore.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=296)** Names in Rust are case sensitive so it does differentiate between upper and lower case letters.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=302)** And finally, you cannot use the same name as reserved keywords, such as let or mut.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=308)** If you try to name a variable let that will confuse the compiler and you'll get an error.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=313)** Now, in addition to those rules which the Rust compiler will enforce, the Rust community has also established naming conventions as guidelines, for how to structure the names for different types of items within your program.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=326)** For example, local variables should be named using what's known as, snake case.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=332)** Using all lowercase letters with underscores to separate words as needed.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/declaring-variables?u=76281980&t=336)** The compiler does not enforce these naming conventions but I strongly encourage you to follow them to keep your code consistent with other Rust programs.

#### Integer data types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Computers don't think the same way you and I do as humans.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=4)** Down at their lowest level, computer store data using units of information called bits where a single bit can only have one of two possible values, one or zero.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=16)** And they use sequences of these binary bits to represent the information we care about.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=21)** So, how do we bridge the gap between the way we understand information as humans and the way a computer deals with information as ones and zeros.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=31)** For example, if a computer uses this sequence of bits to represent something, what does it mean?
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=38)** If interpreted to be a number this particular sequence of bits could represent 82.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=43)** But that same sequence of bits could also be interpreted in a different way as a character to represent the letter 'R'.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=52)** This is why we associate each piece of data in our program with a specific Data Type, which tells the computer, what we as the programmer intent that data to mean.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=62)** The data type tells the computer how that data should be stored and interpreted as bits, as well as how the computer should process that data, and which operations can be used on it.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=74)** Going back to our example, if we say this sequence of bits represents a number stored in the computer's memory, then multiplying it by two is an operation that would make sense.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=84)** 82 times 2 is 164.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=87)** But if that sequence of bits was intended to represent the letter 'R' instead, then trying to multiply that letter by two, does not make sense.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=96)** That's an invalid operation.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=99)** Rust is what's known as a Statically Typed Language, which means every variable in your program must have a known data type for the program to compile.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=108)** The rest compiler uses that information to strictly enforce type roles to make sure you're always using data of different types in valid ways.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=117)** A few other common statically typed programming languages include Java, C, C++ and Go.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=123)** So if you have experience using those languages this concept should be familiar.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=129)** There were four basic data types and rust that can be used to represent individual scalar values.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=135)** We'll look at the Integer data types in this video, which provide different ways to store numeric values, and we'll cover Floating point, Boolean and Character data types in later videos.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=147)** When it comes to storing Integers, there are several different data types to choose from, characterized by the number of bits they use to store the value, And whether or not it's Signed or Unsigned.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=159)** Unsigned integers can only represent positive values, Whereas signed integers can be either positive or negative.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=168)** As an example, let's consider how we could use 8-Bits to represent an integer.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=174)** A sequence of 8-bits can be in one of two to the eight or 256 possible combinations.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=180)** So that's the number of unique values it can represent.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=184)** As an unsigned 8-bit integer abbreviated as u8, that would allow us to represent any integer from zero to 255.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=194)** If we want to also be able to represent negative values, then we would need to use a signed integer abbreviated as i8, which shifts down the range of possible values it can represent to between negative 128 and positive 127.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=211)** Increasing the size of our integer to 16-Bits, increases the range of possible values to over 65,000 and growing the size even more to 32-Bits, allows us to represent over 4 billion integer values.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=226)** Rust includes support for signed and unsigned integers With the bit length shown here , ranging from 8-bits to 128-bits from when you need to store really, really big numbers.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=239)** By default, when you initialize a new integer in rust, it will be stored as assigned 32-bit integer or i32 unless you specifically tell the compiler to use a different type.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=251)** So the variable X that we've declared here online two, will by default be stored as a signed integer.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=258)** That means it can be positive or negative.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=263)** If I compile and run that, it works.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=267)** And we see that X is negative 10.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=270)** Now, if we want rust to use a different data type for this variable, we can specify that by adding a colon after the variable name, followed by the datatype.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=281)** Let's make this an unsigned 8-bit integer.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=285)** If I run the program again, now we get a compiler error, saying that the unsigned values cannot be negated.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=294)** This makes sense.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=295)** Rust is preventing us from trying to store a negative value as an unsigned integer.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=300)** So let's get rid of that negative sign, and let's change the value of X from 10 to a thousand.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=310)** I'll save and run the program again.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=314)** And now we get a different compiler error.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=317)** This time the note says something about overflowing literals because we're trying to store 1000 in an unsigned 8-bit integer whose maximum possible value is 255.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=328)** So, let's drop that value to 255, compile it one more time, and now it works.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=339)** But, we're not done causing problems here quite yet.
>
> **[5:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=342)** Let's see what happens if we use an operation to increase the value of X beyond it's valid range as a u8.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=349)** To do that, we'll change X to be mutable, by adding the mute keyword, and then insert a line after initializing it to 255 to increase the value of X by one.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=363)** When I use cargo to run this program, it successfully compiles and begins to run.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=370)** But when the program reaches line three, it panics and aborts, giving us this error message that we attempted to add with an overflow because we tried to increase the value of X from 255 to 256, which goes beyond its valid 8-bit range.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=387)** The reason why I'm showing you all these different errors, is to demonstrate the many ways rust is designed to protect you.
>
> **[6:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=393)** Other languages like C++ allow you to perform some of these actions unchecked, which can lead to invalid data errors.
>
> **[6:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/integer-data-types?u=76281980&t=401)** Rust is designed to catch those problems with its compiler or at runtime as needed to prevent those bugs and make sure your program operates as expected.

#### Floating-point data types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - The integer data types are not intended to store fractional values.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=5)** If you need to store a number with decimal places, you should use a floating-point type.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=10)** Rust supports two floating point data types.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=13)** A 32 bit version, and a 64 bit version.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=17)** You may hear these referred to as single precision and double precision respectively.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=22)** Floating-point values in rust are stored in several parts including a fractional value and an exponential value that's used to scale it.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=31)** The largest possible 64 bit floating-point value is 1.7 times 10 to the power of 308.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=38)** So that exponent allows us to represent a huge range up to some really, really big numbers.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=45)** However, even though that number is huge in magnitude, the fractional component has a finite amount of precision.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=53)** So we're limited in the number of significant digits or decimal places we can represent.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=59)** As an example, let's consider how PI would be stored as a floating point value.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=64)** PI is an irrational number.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=66)** With an infinitely long sequence of decimal values.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=69)** If we store PI as a 32 bit floating point value the fractional component reaches the limits of precision those 32 bits can represent.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=78)** Using a larger 64 bit floating-point value gives us more precision and more decimal places.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=84)** But again, eventually it reaches a limit.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=88)** If you look closely at those two values, you may notice that at a certain point both of the floating-point numbers start to differ from our original PI value.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=98)** That has to do with how the fractional values are represented in a computer using binary bits as they approached the limit of precision.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=106)** 32 bit floating point numbers can only really represent six to nine significant decimal digits of precision.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=113)** And 64 bit numbers can only do 15 to 17 significant digits.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=118)** It can be important to keep that limitation on precision in mind when working with floating-point data, because the value that actually gets stored may not be exactly what you intended.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=129)** And in certain applications that can potentially cause problems.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=134)** The simplest way to initialize a floating-point variable in Rust is to give it a decimal value.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=140)** In the code currently shown here, X will be initialized as a 32 bit signed integer because we've assigned it the integer value of 10.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=149)** By simply including a decimal point on that value Rust will now store the value of X as a floating-point value.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=156)** And by default, it uses a 64 bit float unless we specify otherwise.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=162)** If I save and run this program, the output we get looks the same as if X was an integer.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=170)** Because the default formatting for the print macro doesn't show the decimal places if there's zero.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=176)** But under the hood, this value is being stored as a floating-point.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=182)** To help visualize that, let's give it a more interesting fractional component.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=190)** I'll save and run that.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=194)** And now we see the decimal places included in the print macro's output.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=199)** Notice that the value shown here is shorter with fewer decimal places than the value we use to initialize it in the code.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=206)** We reached the limit of significant digits this 64 bit float value can represent.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=211)** Now, just as with integers, we can tell Rust to use a specific type of floating-point value to store X.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=218)** So rather than using the default 64 bits, let's use a 32 bit float instead.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=227)** When I run the program with that modification, notice that now the output displays even fewer decimal places because the 32 bit floating-point value has fewer digits of precision than a 64 bit float.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=241)** Having so many different ways to represent numbers with different types of integers and floating-point values can be a bit overwhelming at first.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=249)** So, how do you know which data types you should use.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=253)** As a rule of thumb, when you need to represent a number that has a fractional component or decimal places floating-point is your only option.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=261)** And the default 64 bit floating-point data type is generally the way to go.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=266)** It provides the most precision and range.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=269)** And in modern computers processing 64 bit floats is about as fast as dealing with 32 bit float values.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=276)** Using a smaller 32 bit float value might make sense in certain embedded applications where memory is limited.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=283)** When it comes to integers, it's important to consider the maximum possible value a specific variable might have.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=290)** Positive or negative.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=292)** The default 32 bit signed integer provides a generous range for most general purpose use cases.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=298)** Between around plus or minus 2 billion.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=301)** But, if a variable could potentially need to represent a value outside those bounds, you'll need to use a different data type with a larger range.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=309)** In other situations, if memory usage is a concern you might want to consider using one of the smaller integer data types to conserve and use less memory.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=319)** Finally, if you're interfacing with other existing code like functions from external libraries, then the decision on data type might already be made up for you.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/floating-point-data-types?u=76281980&t=329)** If those functions expect you to provide inputs to them as certain types.

#### Arithmetic operations

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Rust provides the standard set of arithmetic operations you can expect from any programming language, including addition, subtraction, multiplication, division and the modulo operator.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=12)** The code shown here demonstrates the addition operator which we've already used in a previous video.

>

>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=112)** Running this program results in c being one, which is the remainder of 10 divided by three.
