>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=154)** One thing to notice is that it eventually reaches the end of the precision, or number of digits, that a 64 bit float can represent.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=162)** Which is why the last digit is oddly rounded to five.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=166)** So, we've seen how the output from division can differ depending on if we're dividing integers or floating point values, let's see what happens if we try to mix the two types.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=176)** I'll delete the decimal from line two.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=181)** So, a will be stored as an integer while b is still a float.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=185)** When I try to run this program, it results in a compiler error.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=190)** Which says that there's no implementation for dividing an integer by a float.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=195)** The arithmetic operators in Rust do not allow you to mix data types like this because it leaves ambiguity.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=202)** It's not clear which type should be used to represent the result of the division operation.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=207)** To divide these two variables, we'll need to explicitly convert a into the same data type as b through a process called casting.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=216)** The syntax for that tells Rust to process a as an f64.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=224)** When we run that code, a will be casted into a 64 bit float.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=230)** So, since a and b are both floats, the resulting c will also be a float, and we get the output with 3.33 repeating.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=240)** One thing to keep in mind when casting variables is that, depending on the data types you're converting between, information can sometimes be lost.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=249)** Casting from an integer to a float will usually be fine, because the float is just adding the ability to store decimal values.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=257)** But, when you cast a float to an integer, since the integer cannot represent those decimal places, fractional parts of the number will get cut off.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=265)** Rust uses truncation instead of rounding so 3.9 would be cast to three.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=272)** Even casting between different types of integers can lead to strange behavior.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=277)** For example, casting 300 as a u8 results in the value 44.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=283)** That's because 300 is too big to store in a u8 whose maximum value is 255.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=289)** So, the extra rolls over to leave us with 44.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=294)** Casting a negative integer value into an unsigned type will also produce strange results.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=300)** It may not seem like it looking at these odd results, but Rust has well defined behavior when casting between integral types.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=308)** So, as far as Rust is concerned, these are all valid behaviors and the compiler doesn't do anything to stop you.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=315)** By requiring you to explicitly cast data types using the "as" keyword, Rust is putting trust in you as the programmer to know what you're doing.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=323)** So, always consider the effect casting might have on your data.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=328)** One last topic I want to cover regarding arithmetic operators is their order of precedence.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=333)** When you build complex statements containing multiple operations, multiplication, division and modulo operations will be evaluated before addition and subtraction.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=346)** To demonstrate that, I'll add one at the end of the statement on line four.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=351)** And when I run this program, we get the output that c is 4.3.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=357)** That's because the division operator executes first.
>
> **[6:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=361)** 10 divided by three is 3.3.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=363)** And then one gets added to that.
>
> **[6:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=365)** If we want the addition to occur first, we can use parenthesis to control the order of evaluation.
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=373)** The expression inside of the parenthesis will be evaluated first on it's own, and then its result will be used to evaluate the rest of the expression.
>
> **[6:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arithmetic-operations?u=76281980&t=382)** So, when I run the program now, it will first add b plus one which gives us four, and then after that, it will divide a, which is 10 by four resulting in the final value of c being 2.5.

#### Bitwise operations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Remember that under the hood, a computer uses sequences of binary bits, or ones and zeros to represent information.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=8)** We can define values in Rust using a binary notation with the prefix 0b, followed by a sequence of ones and zeros, as shown here on line two.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=19)** Written out like this, this kind of sequence can be visually hard to interpret.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=23)** So Rust allows us to insert optional underscore characters to visually break it up into chunks so it's easier to read.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=31)** Rust will store this value as an integer.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=33)** And by default, it will use the signed 32-bit integer data type.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=38)** However, this sequence is only eight bits long, so we could store the value in a smaller integer data type, such as a u8 if we needed to conserve memory.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=49)** Let's do that by adding the suffix u8 to the end of the literal to specify its data type as an unsigned eight-bit integer.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=59)** Now, since this is being stored as an integer, when we use the print line macro on line three to display its value, Rust will format it like any other integer.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=69)** Rust doesn't care that we initially defined it using binary notation.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=74)** If we want to display its value as a sequence of bits, we can include additional formatting in the curly braces.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=81)** I'll copy and paste that print line statement and then add some formatting.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=92)** The colon in the curly braces indicates we want to use special formatting.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=96)** The lowercase b tells it to display the values as binary bits, or ones and zeros.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=102)** Eight is the number of bits to display.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=105)** And the zero in front of that tells it to show any leading zeros on the front of the number rather than hide them which is the default behavior.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=113)** When I run this program, the first line of output shows that this particular sequence of bits corresponds to the integer value 245 when displayed using decimal notation.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=127)** And then the second line shows it represented as binary bits.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=131)** Now the programming operations we use to process data, like addition and subtraction, are applied to groupings of multiple bits, which are most commonly thought of in terms of bytes, or a byte is a group of eight related bits.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=145)** However, there are certain programming situations where we may want to think about and manipulate data in terms of individual bits, rather than larger sets of bytes representing some data.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=156)** This type of bitwise thinking is especially common in low-level and embedded programming.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=163)** For example, digital input and output pins on a microcontroller are often organized into groups called a port.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=170)** The port shown here has eight physical pins numbered zero through seven.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=176)** The microcontroller has a data direction register which holds eight bits in memory, representing the direction of those eight digital IO pins.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=184)** When a bit is set to one, that tells the microcontroller to configure and use the corresponding pin as input to the device.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=192)** And when it's set to zero, that means the pin will be used for output.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=196)** As the programmer, we need a way to work with these bits on an individual basis to configure the microcontroller.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=204)** To do that, we'll use bitwise operators, which apply logical operations on a pattern of bits at the individual bit level.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=213)** Rust includes the common set of bitwise operators with NOT, AND, OR, XOR, and Shift.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=220)** And we'll look how to use each of these starting with NOT.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=224)** The NOT operation is applied to a single bit to invert its value.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=229)** So if the input bit A is one, the output from the NOT operation will be zero and vice versa.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=237)** If we apply it to a sequence of bits, the bitwise NOT operator will look at all the bits individually and produce an output sequence where all of the bits have been flipped.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=248)** We can apply the bitwise NOT operator in Rust using an exclamation mark or bang symbol.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=254)** This will invert the bits in value and store the result.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=258)** I'll copy and paste the print statement to display the result after that.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=264)** And since we're changing value, we'll also need to mark it as mutable.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=270)** Now I can run the program.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=274)** And we can see that the bits have been inverted between these last two lines.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=279)** The next operator is bitwise AND, which takes two input bits, A and B.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=285)** And if both A and B are one, then the output of the AND operator will also be one.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=291)** Otherwise, for any other combination of A and B containing a zero, the result of the AND operation will be zero.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=299)** One common use for bitwise AND is to clear the value of a specific bit.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=304)** Let's say we want to change the value of the bit at this position to be zero while maintaining the current value of the rest of these bits.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=312)** If we bitwise AND that sequence with another sequence containing all ones, except for the bit we want to change to zero, the existing value for all the bits we want to maintain will effectively pass through, while the bit we want to clear is set to zero in the output.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=328)** In Rust, the bitwise AND operator is represented using a single ampersand.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=337)** This code will apply the operation we just examined, applying the bitwise AND to clear the bit at position three.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=345)** I'll paste the print statement again after that to view the result.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=349)** Run the code and we can see that bit at position three gets set to zero in the final output.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=356)** Another common use for bitwise AND is to check a specific value within a sequence to see whether it's one or zero.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=364)** If we want to check the value at this position, we can bitwise AND to the sequence with a pattern of zeros and a one at the position to check.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=372)** If the result of that operation is all zeros, then we know the bit we wanted to check was also zero.
>
> **[6:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=379)** Now, if we do the same thing to check a different bit in that sequence, whose value is one, we see that now the output contains a one.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=387)** Interpreted as an integer, this would represent a non-zero number.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=392)** In low-level programming, this type of operation is often used to check status registers which hold a collection of bits representing different things about the current state of the processor.
>
> **[6:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=404)** For a code example, let's use bitwise AND to check and display the value of the bit at position six in our value.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=413)** Notice that we're directly using the result of that operation in the print macro, and not assigning the result back to the variable because we just want to read that bit, not modify its value.
>
> **[7:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=425)** When I run this code, we see that bit six is zero.
>
> **[7:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=432)** A partner to the bitwise AND operation is bitwise OR, which also operates on two inputs, A and B.
>
> **[7:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=440)** If either or both of the input bits are one, then the output will also be one.
>
> **[7:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=446)** Otherwise, if both inputs are zero, the output of A or B will be zero.
>
> **[7:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=453)** This operation can be used if we want to change a specific bit to set its value to one.
>
> **[7:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=459)** For example, let's set this bit at position six to one.
>
> **[7:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=463)** By applying the bitwise OR with a sequence of zeros, and one at the position we want to set, the previous value for all the other bits will be maintained while the value of the bit we want to set will always be one in the output.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=478)** Rust uses the vertical pipe symbol to represent the bitwise OR operator.
>
> **[8:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=485)** So this code will set the value of bit six to one, and save the result back to the value variable.
>
> **[8:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=493)** When I run it, we can see that bit change from zero to one in the output.
>
> **[8:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=499)** Our next operator is bitwise XOR, which stands for exclusive or.
>
> **[8:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=505)** I'd like to think of it like a more selective cousin of our bitwise OR.
>
> **[8:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=510)** It also operates on two input bits.
>
> **[8:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=513)** And the output of XOR will be one when the two input bits are different.
>
> **[8:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=518)** And it will be zero if both of the inputs are the same.
>
> **[8:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=522)** So you can use XOR to check if and where the values may differ between two bit patterns.
>
> **[8:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=530)** The bitwise OR operator is represented using the carrot symbol or hat operator.
>
> **[8:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=536)** To demonstrate let's, XOR the value with a sequence of alternating zeros and ones.
>
> **[9:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=544)** When I run this program, the resulting value shows us which bits in the previous value differed from that alternating sequence.
>
> **[9:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=554)** The final two operators will look at our bitwise shifts which can be used to shift a bit pattern left or right by a specific number of bits.
>
> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=564)** The shift operator will shift in zeros to backfill the spaces leftover from shifting bits around.
>
> **[9:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=571)** We can apply the left shift operator to our value which is represented using two less than symbols, followed by the number of bits we want to shift the value by.
>
> **[9:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=581)** Let's shift it left by four.
>
> **[9:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=583)** I'll paste the print statement again.
>
> **[9:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=586)** And then after that, let's use the right shift operator which is represented with two greater than symbols to shift the sequence right by two bits.
>
> **[9:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=597)** And of course display the result.
>
> **[10:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=600)** I'll run that.
>
> **[10:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=602)** And looking at the last three lines of code, we can see that when we shifted the sequence left by four bits, the values that were shifted off the left side are lost and zeros were inserted on the right side of the sequence, to backfill those shifted bits.
>
> **[10:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=616)** Then when we shifted our value right by two bits, on the last line, zeros are used to backfill the shifted bits on the left side, which means we effectively lost this one bit when it was shifted off the left side.
>
> **[10:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=631)** So those are the six main bitwise operators in Rust.
>
> **[10:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=635)** NOT, AND, OR, XOR, Left Shift, and Right Shift.
>
> **[10:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/bitwise-operations?u=76281980&t=640)** Commonly used for low level programming when you need to manipulate bits on an individual basis

#### Boolean data type and operations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to integer and floating point data types, Rust has another primitive data type called a Boolean which can have one of two possible values either true or false.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=12)** We can use the same set of logical operators we saw in the previous video to evaluate Boolean values with NOT, AND, OR an XOR.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=23)** The bit value of one corresponds to true and zero corresponds to false.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=29)** In fact, if you cast the Boolean data type into an integer the resulting value will be a one or a zero.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=36)** This example demonstrates those four logical operations applied to the Boolean variables a and b, which are initialized on lines two and three to the values true and false respectively.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=48)** Lines four, through eight, then display the values of a and b as well as the results of applying the NOT and OR an XOR operations.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=58)** The NOT operator is represented with an exclamation mark or bang.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=63)** The AND operator is an ampersand or is a vertical pipe.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=68)** An XOR is a caret or hat operator.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=71)** When I run this program, we can see from the output that a is initialized to true and b is initialized to false, NOT a flips its value to false a AND b evaluates to false because B is false, a OR b is true because at least one of the values a is true and a XOR b is true because a and b are different.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=99)** Notice that the result of applying these operators to Booleans are also Boolean values.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=105)** These logical operations can be used to evaluate more than just individual variables as shown here, we can put more complex expressions on either side of the operators, as long as the expressions evaluate to either true or false.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=119)** For example, let's declare a new variable c, assign it the result of a XOR b, or a AND B, and then display that result.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=139)** This statement will individually evaluate the expressions on both sides of the OR operator, in the middle.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=145)** So a XOR b should evaluate a true and a AND b on the right side should evaluate to false and then it will apply the OR operator on those two results.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=157)** So true or false means c should end up with the final result of true.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=163)** When I run this program, surely enough, we can see that's the case.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=170)** When writing expressions using the AND and OR operators to evaluate two Booleans, we sometimes only need to look at one side of the operator, to know the result.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=180)** For example, after looking at just the left side of this expression we already know it will evaluate to false because false ended with anything will always be false.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=191)** Therefore there's no need to even look at what's on the right side of the AND operator.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=197)** Similarly, if we see that one side of an OR operation is true there's no need to evaluate the other side because we already know the result will be true.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=206)** We can take advantage of that efficiency in Rust, by using the short-circuiting logical AND and OR operators which are represented using double ampersands and double vertical pipes.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=217)** If those operators can determine the result, after evaluating the left side, they won't even bother looking at the right side.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=225)** So if we use the short circuiting OR operation in this expression on line 10, and then run the program it will evaluate the left side XOR B.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=238)** And upon seeing that result is true, the OR can immediately evaluate the true without doing anything on the right side.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=246)** The result is the same as before, c is true but the program executed a tiny bit more efficiently.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=254)** We can more clearly see that the right side of the short circuit OR, is not being evaluated by replacing the expression a AND B with a panic macro.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=264)** If the panic macro gets executed it will cause the program to immediately terminate with an error.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=270)** But when I run this program, everything works fine because the right side of that OR does not need to be executed.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=279)** Now, if I change that short circuiting OR to a short circuiting AND, now the program will need to evaluate both sides of the expression because we cannot determine the result of the AND operation from just the left side, which is true.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/boolean-data-type-and-operations?u=76281980&t=294)** When I run the program now, when it tries to evaluate the right side and reaches the panic macro, as intended, it crashes.

#### Comparison operations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Teacher] Another set of operators that can be used to evaluate two values and return a Boolean result are the comparison operators shown here.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=9)** Operating on integers a and b, declared on lines two and three.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=13)** The equality comparison operator on line five is represented using two equals symbols.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=19)** It compares the values on each side of the operator and if they're equivalent the expression evaluates to true.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=27)** Otherwise, if they're different values it evaluates to false.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=31)** The non equality comparison operator below that on line six, which is an exclamation mark and an equals sign, operates in a similar but opposite fashion.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=42)** If variables a and b are equivalent it will evaluate to false, and if they're different it will evaluate to true.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=50)** The greater than, and greater than or equals comparisons on lines seven and eight work the way you might expect.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=57)** If the value of integer a is greater than b the expression will evaluate to true, and if not, it will evaluate to false, and the less than, and less than or equals comparisons below that on lines nine and ten work in a similar but opposite manner.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=73)** If I run this program we can see the output that a is one, and b is two, followed by the Boolean results for those six comparison operations.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=84)** It's common to think of comparison operations like greater than or less than when working with numbers, but these comparison operations can also be applied to other data types that support these comparison traits.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=98)** For example, let's change a to the Boolean value true, and b to false.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=107)** When I run the code now, as we should expect, the equality comparison is false, because a and b have different values, and the non equality comparison evaluates to true.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=121)** Below that, notice that the two greater than comparisons both evaluate to true, meaning Rust considers a greater than or equal to b, and the two less than comparisons are both false.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=134)** These comparison results make sense if we think about the Boolean value of true corresponding to the integer value of one and the Boolean value false corresponding to zero, because one is greater than zero.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=147)** However, we cannot use these comparison operations to compare values if they're stored in different data types.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=154)** If I change a back to the integer value one, but leave b as a Boolean value, when I try to run this program it produces a long list of compiler errors.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=169)** Scrolling up near the top of those errors we can see a message that the equality operator is not implemented to compare integer and Boolean data types.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=178)** That's something to keep in mind when you're using comparisons.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/comparison-operations?u=76281980&t=181)** You can typically only use them on similar data types.

#### Char data types
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The Char data type can be used to represent a single character such as a single letter or perhaps a number.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=7)** In Rust, the Char data type is a Unicode scalar value and all Char's take up exactly four bytes of memory.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=16)** This is different than other languages like C and C++, which typically store character values using just a single bite.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=24)** As we'll see, in a moment since Rust stores characters using four bites and Unicode we can represent much more than just simple letters and numbers.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=33)** We create a Char literal in Rust by enclosing the character in single quotes as shown here.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=38)** The code on line 2 creates a new Char representing the letter a and assigns it to the variable, named letter.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=45)** And below that on line 3 we create another Char for the number one and assign it to the variable named number.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=52)** It's important to realize that representing the character one here with the Char data type is very different than representing the value of one with a numeric data type, such as an integer.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=64)** We cannot apply arithmetic operations like addition and subtraction to the character representation of one like we would with an integer or floating point value.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=74)** Now, as I mentioned earlier Rust Char's are stored as Unicode, which opens up a wide range of characters beyond letters and numbers including this collection of miscellaneous symbols.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=86)** Let's try to create a Char in our Rust program to represent this upward pointing finger, which corresponds to the hexadecimal character code 261D.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=97)** We can specify a Char using its Unicode value by using slash U and then putting the hexadecimal value, in curly braces, 261D.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=108)** Now that we've declared three characters let's use a print macro to display them all to the console.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=114)** Notice that I'm using the slash in sequence here to insert a new line between each of the characters.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=121)** Now when I run this program, we can see the output with the letter a, the number one and the finger symbol.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=129)** Something to keep in mind is that not all environments will be able to display some of the more exotic Unicode characters.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=136)** The VS code editors built in terminal is able to display the pointing finger.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/char-data-types?u=76281980&t=142)** But if I try to run that same program from the Windows command prompt we get the letter a and the one but the finger is replaced by a box with a question mark because the command prompt cannot display that character.

#### Challenge: Find the average

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right, folks.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=6)** It's time for a challenge to practice working with different data types and numeric operations in Rust.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=12)** Your goal for this challenge will be to compute the arithmetic average, or mean, of three given numbers that can be calculated by adding the three numbers together and then dividing their sum by three.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=25)** You should use this code from the exercise files as a starting point.
>

> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=35)** Notice that the three variables have different data types.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=38)** That's something you'll need to take into account for your solution.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=42)** Your job is to replace the comment on line six with your code to calculate the arithmetic mean of A, B, and C.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=49)** You should perform your calculation using 64 bit floating point values, and then assign that result to a variable named average.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=57)** The assert equal macro on line eight will check to make sure the average you calculated is equal to the correct answer of 45.1, and if it is, the program will output the message test passed.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=70)** Otherwise, you'll get an error.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=72)** There were several ways to solve this challenge so take some time to come up with your own solution and then watch the next video to see how I solved it.

#### Solution: Find the average
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=0)** - To create my solution for the arithmetic average challenge, I'll begin by declaring a new variable, named average, and then calculating the value to assign average by writing out the basic equation to average together three variables A plus B plus C divided by 3.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=25)** Notice that I've written 3 with a decimal place, so it will be stored as a floating-point value.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=31)** I did that because I want the result of the division operation to be stored as a float in case the average has a fractional value.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=39)** Next, I'll put parentheses around A, B, and C.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=44)** That will enforce order of operations to make sure they get added together to form the numerator before dividing by 3.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=52)** Now, let's try running the program.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=56)** And we get an error.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=59)** It failed to compile because we can't add together A and B which have different data types.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=64)** A is an integer, and B is a float.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=68)** So let's cast A to be handled as a 64 bit float like B.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=74)** We'll say A as F64.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=78)** I'll save that change.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=80)** Try to run it again.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=83)** And it fails once more.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=85)** This time, because C is an F32, even though A, B and C are all floating-point values in that equation, they have different bit lengths and therefore aren't compatible.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=96)** We cannot add an F64 with an F32.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=100)** So, let's cast C to also be an F64.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=106)** I'll run the program one more time, and now everything works.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=111)** And we get the output message that the test passed.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=114)** Now, it's important to acknowledge that this simple test passes because the average is exactly 45.1, which matches the assert equal macro on line 8.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=125)** That's the result we get when performing the calculation with those three numbers cast as 64 bit floating-point values.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=132)** However, if we were to cast those numbers as 32 bit floats, due to the differences in how those values are represented, using fewer bits, the result of that calculation is slightly different.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=144)** 45.100002.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=147)** It's close, but not exactly 45.1, and if the assert equal macros saw this number, it would panic and the test would fail.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=156)** Since calculations with floating-point numbers can have accuracy problems like this, and produce results that differ slightly from what's expected, in general, it's not a good idea to check for exact equality when working with floats.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=170)** I chose to use the assert equal macro in this example to keep the code simple and avoid external dependencies, but a safer alternative would've been to use a function that can determine if the floating-point result is approximately equal to the expected value.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=186)** The external crate, named approx, provides several macros you can use to test for approximate equality, with different floating-point-based types.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-find-the-average?u=76281980&t=195)** We'll see how to import and use external crates like this one later in the course.

### 3. Compound Data Types

#### Arrays
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to the primitive data types we've looked at so far which can each be used to represent a single scalar value, Rust also has a few compound data types which you can use to store multiple values.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=15)** An array can be used to hold a collection of elements that all have the same data type.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=21)** The elements are stored sequentially in a specific order.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=25)** One after another, in a contiguous section of memory.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=28)** In Rust, arrays have a fixed length which the compiler needs to know to determine how much memory will be needed to hold the array.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=37)** You cannot dynamically resize an array in Rust.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=41)** Conceptually, I like to use the analogy that an array is like a row of parked cars because all of the parking spots hold the same type of thing.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=49)** A car, the spots are all indexed with sequential numbers so you can locate your car along the row.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=56)** Notice that the number in the first spot here is zero.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=59)** Rust, like many other programming languages uses zero indexing which means the first element has an index number of zero and the next one after that is one, then two and so on.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=72)** Since array elements are stored in order, one common use for arrays is to hold time series data or a sequence of values indexed in time order that might represent something like the signal shown here.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=86)** One way to declare a new array in Rust is to enclose a comma separated list of initial values inside of square brackets.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=94)** So to declare an array containing the characters A, B, and C, I could type let letters equal A, B and C.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=107)** Now, to access individual elements in an array to retrieve or modify their value, we use square brackets and the numeric index corresponding to that element.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=117)** So if I want to retrieve the first letter from this array, I would type letters square brackets and then the index value zero.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=130)** I'll add a print macro after that to look at the first letter that was retrieved.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=137)** And when I run this program from the output, we can see that the array element at index zero was the letter A which was the first element in the array.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=148)** If we want to modify values in an array, we'll first need to make sure that it's declared as mutable.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=155)** Then we can use the equal symbol to assign a new value to a specific index location.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=161)** This will change the first element to be the letter X before retrieving and printing it.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=167)** And if I run this program again, now we see that the first letter is X.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=174)** It's worth noting that we can only replace elements of this array with other characters, because all of the elements must be the same data type.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=182)** And that data type must be known at compile time along with the length of the array.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=187)** The compiler can look at the values we use to initialize this array online too and tell that it contained three characters, but what if we don't have an initial set of values to use when declaring the array?
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=200)** Rust allows us to declare new variables without assigning an initial value as long as we specify the data type.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=208)** For an array of numbers, that would look like this, let numbers i32, 5.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=217)** This will create an array named numbers to hold signed 32 bit integers and it has a length of five elements.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=224)** Those two pieces of information, the data type and number of elements enable the compiler to determine how much space in memory the program will need to hold the array even though we haven't assigned it any values yet.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=237)** Now, let's see what happens if we try to access a value in that uninitialized array by indexing the last value which because of zero indexing, will be at index number four for this five element long array.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=253)** When I try to run that program, the compiler throws an error because it detects that we're trying to access an uninitialized memory location in the array which has the potential to cause some nasty bugs.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=266)** Although Rust lets you declare variables without an initial value, the compiler always checks to make sure you assign the variable a value before trying to read from it.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=277)** For simplicity, let's initialize this array to have all zeroes.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=284)** Now, listing out five zeros like this isn't too much trouble, but if we wanted to initialize a much longer array with hundreds or thousands of elements this way, individually typing out all those zeros would be a tedious waste of time.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=298)** A more efficient way to initialize an array with all the same value is to use a repeat expression.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=307)** This will produce an array containing five copies of the value zero.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=312)** When I run this program, we can see the displayed output for the last number is zero.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=320)** Now, let's see what happens if we try to index beyond the length of the array by changing the index online nine from four to five.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=329)** The numbers array is only five elements long.
>
> **[5:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=332)** So it's valid index values are zero through four.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=337)** When I try to run this program, it fails compilation because the compiler recognized that the index is out of bounds before the program could even run.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=347)** That's fairly obvious to see in this situation using a fixed value for the index, but let's see what happens if we try to use a variable to index the array.
>
> **[5:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=357)** I'll declare a new variable named index and assign its value to be the length of the numbers array which will be five by using the length function.
>
> **[6:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=367)** We'll cover that in more detail in a later video then I'll change the index online ten to use that variable.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=375)** One thing I'd like to point out here is that the data type we're using index the array is not one of the normal integer types we covered in an earlier video.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=385)** Instead, a raise in Rust must be indexed using a special data type called usize.
>
> **[6:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=392)** The length of this usize data type will be determined by the compiler based on the number of bytes that are needed to address memory and to target architecture you're compiling the program for.
>
> **[6:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=402)** For example, when compiling for a 32 bit target, a usize variable would compile to be four bytes long.
>
> **[6:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=410)** Whereas compiling for a 64 bit target would make usize eight bytes long.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=416)** Now, let's try running this program and see what happens.
>
> **[7:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=421)** It successfully compiles this time.
>
> **[7:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=423)** But during execution, when it reaches line tin and tries to index the numbers array, it panics with a runtime error that the index was out of bounds and the program exits.
>
> **[7:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=435)** This may seem obnoxious but it's actually Rust playing it safe and protecting us because it's safer to knowingly exit the program here, than allow it to continue running after making an invalid memory access.
>
> **[7:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=448)** Other low-level languages like C and C plus plus, do not prevent you from indexing array values that are out of bounds.
>
> **[7:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/arrays?u=76281980&t=456)** And that's a common source of bugs that are painful to track down and can produce invalid results that you may not even realize are wrong.

#### Multidimensional arrays
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=0)** - Arrays in Rust can have more than one dimension.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=4)** If we think of a basic one-dimensional array as being like a row of parked cars with each spot having a unique index number to identify it, then a two-dimensional array is like a parking lot where you'll need two index values to locate your car.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=19)** You'll need to know which row you parked on, and then which spot along that row.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=25)** Taking that analogy even further, a three-dimensional array is like a multi-story parking garage.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=31)** To find your car, you'll need three index values.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=33)** Which floor you parked on, which row on that floor, and then which spot along that row.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=39)** It's hard to imagine a four-dimensional parking garage.

> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=42)** So we'll stop the analogy there.
>

> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=71)** Let's spread that code out on two different lines.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=75)** This is functionally the same thing as having everything on one line, but I think it makes the code a little bit easier to read.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=83)** Now to index a single element from this two-dimensional array, we'll need two indexes.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=90)** The first points to one of the sub-arrays and the second index points to a specific value within that sub-array.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=98)** Using the parking lot analogy, this line will retrieve the element at row zero, which is the top row and spot number one which will be the second spot on that row.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=108)** It should retrieve two.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=111)** Let's print out the value of that retrieved number.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=118)** Then run the program.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=123)** And sure enough, we get back two.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=126)** If we changed those index values to something else, like one and two, this will get the value from array number one, spot number two.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=138)** And when I run this program, we see that it's six.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=143)** Now remember that the elements of an array must all have the exact same data type.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=149)** The two elements in our numbers array are both arrays of three integer values.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=154)** If we try to add an extra value to one of their sub-arrays, now they have different data types.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=162)** The first sub-array is a three integer array, and the second one is a four integer array.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=169)** If I try to run the program now, it fails to compile because of the mismatched types.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=175)** So when working with multi-dimensional arrays in Rust, the inner dimensions must all be the same.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=182)** I'll revert line back to what it originally was.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=187)** Now that we've seen how to work with a two-dimensional array of arrays, let's look at how we might create a three-dimensional array of arrays of arrays.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=197)** We could explicitly list out all the values using nested brackets, similar to what we did on lines two and three, but that would get a bit cumbersome if we add more dimensions and longer arrays.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=209)** So let's build up the data type for a new three-dimensional array named garage, starting with the inner most array which will have an integer data type and 100 elements.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=223)** Then we'll use that inner array as the data type for the next level which is like a two-dimensional parking lot.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=232)** And we'll give this lot 20 rows.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=236)** Finally, that 2D array will serve as the data type for our three-dimensional garage, which will have five floors.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=247)** So this defines the data type for a three-dimensional array with five by 20, by 100 integer elements.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=255)** If we want to initialize all of those elements to the same value, we can replace the i32 data type with that value.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=263)** Let's set them all to zero, and then assign that array to the garage variable.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multidimensional-arrays?u=76281980&t=270)** And that will give us a garage full of zeros.

#### Tuples
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Tuples are another compound data type that can be used to group a collection of related items together.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=7)** However, unlike an array whose elements must all be the same data type, a tuple can hold a mix of data types.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=15)** Otherwise tuples and arrays have a lot in common.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=19)** The elements have a relative order although when working with tuples that order is usually not the focus like it is when working with the arrays, the elements in a tuple are stored in a fixed length, contiguous section memory.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=32)** And as with arrays the compiler needs to know the data types for each of the tuple elements so it can determine exactly how much memory the program needs to store the tuple.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=44)** Going back to our car analogy, if a row of parked cars is like an array because every spot holds the same type of thing, a car, we can think of a tuple like the glove box in one of those cars.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=56)** It might contain a flashlight and a tire pressure gauge, the owner's manual and well maybe even a pair of gloves.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=64)** These are all different types of things but they're all related to each other as tools for operating and maintaining the automobile.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=71)** So we grouped them together in a glove box.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=75)** The syntax to declare a tuple in Rust looks similar to the syntax to initialize a new array except instead of using square brackets, we enclose the list of values in parentheses.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=87)** So let's declare a new tuple named stuff and give it an integer 10, a float value of 3.14 and the character x.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=100)** All three of these items are grouped together as a single tuple named stuff.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=104)** Accessing items in the tuple uses a slightly different syntax than indexing arrays.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=110)** To retrieve the first item from the tuple, we can type stuff.0.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=119)** Notice that tuples are also zero indexed like arrays.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=123)** So if we wanted to access the second item in the tuple, that would be stuff.1, the third item would be stuff.2 and so on.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=132)** I'll print that item we just retrieved, save and then run the program.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=142)** And we can see that first item is indeed 10.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=146)** Now the compiler will store each of the items in our tuple based on the default data type for the value we give it.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=153)** So 10 will be stored as a signed 32 bit integer and 3.14 will be a 64 bit float.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=162)** If we want those items to be stored using different integer and floating point data types, we can manually specify those types when declaring the tuple.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=171)** Let's store 10 as a u8, 3.14 as a 32 bit float and finally x which makes sense as a char.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=182)** That's the basics on how to initialize a new tuple.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=186)** If we want to modify one of the values that process is similar to what we saw with arrays earlier except using the tuples dot notation instead.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=195)** To do that, first we'll need to make our stuff tuple mutable.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=199)** Then let's say we want to increase the value of the first item by three.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=204)** To do that, we could type stuff.zero plus equal three.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=211)** This plus equal symbol is a shorthand that retrieves the value from the variable on its left.

> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=218)** We'll add three to it and then store the result back in that variable.

> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=231)** One more thing you'll commonly see done with tuples is something called de-structuring which allows us to break apart the items in a tuple and assign them to individual variables.

> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=243)** I'll write a new let statement with a tuple a, b, c and assign that the value of stuff.

> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuples?u=76281980&t=266)** And when I run the program, we can see that the variable b was assigned the value 3.14 from the stuff tuple.
