### 4. Functions

#### Function parameters
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Functions allow us to organize related sections of code into reusable modules that can be easily called again to execute from elsewhere.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=9)** It's rare to encounter a program that doesn't use functions.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=13)** In fact, every rest program has at least one function.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=17)** The main function, which serves as the entry point to begin execution.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=22)** We can declare additional functions by using the fn keyword, followed by a unique function name which is typically written using lowercase letters and underscores.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=33)** After that we have opening close parentheses and then a pair of opening close curly braces.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=40)** Since I named this function say hello let's make it say hello with a print macro.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=46)** Now that we've defined our new function, we can call it from within our main program, by using the function name followed by a pair of parentheses.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=56)** Notice that we've defined the say hello function after the main function, which is where it gets called.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=62)** Unlike some other languages which require functions to be declared before they're called, rust doesn't care where you define your functions.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=71)** By save and run this program the main function runs which then calls the, say hello function to execute which in turn prints the hello message.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=83)** If we want our program to say hello twice we can simply reuse that function by calling it again.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=89)** There will often be times when you want to pass some information to a function for it to use and that can be done with parameters.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=97)** A parameter is a special type of variable that's used to provide input data to a function.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=103)** Is defined as part of the functions signature which is what those parentheses after the function name are used for.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=110)** Let's define another function named say a number and we'll give it a parameter named number by putting that in parentheses, followed by a colon and then i32.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=125)** Number is the name of the parameter which we can use within this function like a variable to access the value that's passed in.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=133)** And with rest being a strictly typed language it requires us to specify the data type for any and all parameters.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=141)** Within this function let's simply use a print macro to display that number.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=151)** Now, let's call this new function but rather than just calling it from the main function like we did with say hello let's call say a number from within the say hello function.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=163)** And we'll pass it the value 13 as an input.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=168)** This concrete value that we're passing into the function is technically called an argument.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=173)** Whereas the name that we use to access it within the function, in this case number is the parameter.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=179)** That said, it's not uncommon to hear people use the terms argument and parameter interchangeably in conversation to mean one or the other.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=187)** Just keep in mind they technically mean different things.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=190)** When I run this program the main function calls say hello twice.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=196)** And each time it executes after printing hello it calls say a number which then prints 13.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=203)** Now functions aren't limited to just having one parameter.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=207)** Let's define one more function named say the sum which will take one parameter named A, which is a u8 along with a second parameter named B which will also be u8.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=225)** We can list out as many parameters as we want like this separated by commas.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=229)** We can also give parameters different data types but since we'll be adding these two together it makes sense for them to be the same here.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=237)** Let's declare a new variable name sum and assign it the result of A plus B and then print that sum value.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=248)** Up in the main function we'll declare two new variables named X and Y and assign them the values one and two and then pass them as input arguments to our say the sum function.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=267)** When we run this program we see from the last line of output that the sum of one and two is three.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=275)** Now there's something interesting going on with data types here.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=279)** We didn't specify the types of X and Y on lines four and five.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=284)** And since the values one and two are integers by default we would expect rust to store them as i32s.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=291)** However, the parameters for our say the sum function are both u8.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=297)** So how is this function able to use X and Y.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=301)** One potential explanation might be that rust is casting X and Y from i32 to i38 when they get past those arguments to say the sum.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=311)** But that can't be what's going on here because rust will never cast one data type to another without you explicitly telling it to.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=320)** If X or Y held a really big number using 32 bits then casting it down to a much smaller u8 could change its value.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=328)** What's really happening, is that the compiler is smart enough to recognize that we're using X and Y as inputs to say the sum, which requires u8.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=337)** So that's the data type it we'll use for them since we didn't specify anything else.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=343)** We can see that's the case by calling say a number on X after our call to say the sum.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=350)** Say a number expects an i32 as input.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=354)** So when we run this program, we get a compiler error at the call to say a number because it found a u8 Instead.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=364)** The compiler had already determined that X and Y should be u8 from the call to say the sum on the previous line.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=372)** A fix for that would be to cast X as an i32 lets say a number.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=378)** This ability of the compiler to determine the data types for variables based on where they're used is a benefit we get from having to annotate the data types for all function parameters.
>
> **[6:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-parameters?u=76281980&t=389)** It makes our life easier as a rust programmer not having to explicitly specify the data type for all of our variables.

#### Statements vs. expressions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I want to take a moment to talk about two terms you've heard me use throughout this course, statements and expressions, to explain what they are and how they're different from each other.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=11)** A statement is an instruction that performs an action, but in the end it doesn't have an output.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=18)** It doesn't return a value that can be used elsewhere.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=22)** We write statements in Rust as instructions that end with a semicolon.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=27)** For example, x = 1 is a statement because it performs its action, assigning the value 1 to x, but we don't get anything back as output after it.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=38)** Rust will not allow us to use that statement as the value to assign to another variable because that statement doesn't represent a value, it's just an action.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=48)** An expression, on the other hand, does evaluate to produce a resulting value that can be used elsewhere.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=55)** Expressions in Rust do not have a semicolon.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=58)** In fact, if you add a semicolon to the end of an expression, that will turn it into a statement.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=64)** So for example, 1 + 2 is an expression that will evaluate to the resulting value of 3.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=71)** However, if we add a semicolon to the end of that, that turns it into a statement and that result doesn't go anywhere.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=79)** Expressions commonly exist as parts of statement as in the example shown here.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=84)** A + b is an expression that will be evaluated to produce some resulting value, which then gets used as part of the larger statement to assign the result of that expression to the variable named sum.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/statements-vs-expressions?u=76281980&t=97)** This distinction between statements and expressions, knowing which instructions do and do not produce an output, will become important as we move on to writing functions that can return a value.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is an  (3), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Function return values
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We saw how to define functions with input parameters to parse data to a function.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=6)** Now, let's look at how we can use return values to get resulting output data back from a function.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=13)** To demonstrate that, let's define a new function to square a given input value.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=18)** For this function to pass a result value back to wherever it was called from, we'll need to specify the data type for that returned value in the function signature.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=28)** We do that after the input parameters, by typing dash, then a greater than symbol, which together look like an arrow, followed by the data type.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=38)** This function will return in i32.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=42)** Notice that we did not have to provide a name for the return value, like we did for the input parameter.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=48)** For the body of this function, we'll compute the square by multiplying X by itself.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=55)** I intentionally did not add a semi-colon to the end of line six, which leaves X times X as an expression.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=62)** In Rust, when we make the last line of a function an expression, the result from that expression will get passed out as the return value from that function.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=72)** Let's add a print macro at the beginning of this function so we can see that it's executing.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=80)** Then we'll call our new square function to compute the square of 13 and then print the result.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=93)** When I save and run this program, we see the output that the function is squaring 13 and then that the result is 169, using an expression worked here because the value we wanted to return was produced by the very last line in the square function.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=111)** But what if we want to return a value earlier?
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=114)** We'll learn about flow control mechanisms like loops and conditional logic later in this course, which can create situations, where we might want to return from a function sooner before the last line.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=126)** As a simple way to demonstrate that, let's add a second print macro at the end of the square function, after our expression to calculate the result.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=136)** If we try to run this program, it fails to compile.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=141)** the compiler is confused because it's not sure how to parse this code.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=145)** It doesn't make sense to have the expression X times X hanging out in the middle there.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=150)** To fix this, we can tell the function to return the result of X times X at line eight by using the return keyword and adding a semi-colon to the end of that line, which makes it a statement.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=164)** When I run the program now, the compiler warns us that any code following that line is unreachable so the final print macro will never actually execute but that's okay.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=177)** The program is still able to compile and run.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=180)** The output just doesn't include the end of function message.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=184)** Now, we're not just limited to returning simple data types, like in i32.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=189)** If we want a function to return multiple values, we can accomplish that by packaging them together into a tuple.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=195)** Let's say we want our function to return two values, the original value of X that was parsed as an input, along with the squared value of X.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=205)** We can do that by using a tuple of two i32s.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=210)** So, I'll change the output return type in the function signature accordingly.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=215)** And then on line eight, I'll create a tuple with X as the first value and the result of X times X as the second.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=225)** The statement on line three to print the result will not be able to print a tuple because the default formatter it uses doesn't handle that data type.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=234)** But we can tell the print macro to use special debug formatting instead, which is capable of displaying a tuple by inserting a colon and a question mark into the curly braces.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=248)** When I run this program, we see that the result is a tuple, containing 13 and 169.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=257)** That could be structured into its individual parts or whatever is needed to process those multiple return values.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=264)** For one final thought, I want to talk about what happens when you do not include a return value in a function definition.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=271)** In a function like this, which does something that doesn't return a result, Rust will implicitly return what's called the unit data type.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=280)** The unit data type is used when there is no other meaningful value that could be returned and is represented with a pair of open and closed parentheses like an empty tuple.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=290)** Similar to what we've seen when defining a function that does not have any input parameters.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/function-return-values?u=76281980&t=296)** If you want, you can explicitly define functions with a return value using the unit data type but for convenience, you can leave that off and the Rust compiler will infer it for you.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **Cross-References:** later in (1)

### 5. Program Flow Control

#### Conditional execution
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The simple programs we've written thus far have all executed their instructions sequentially in order from start to finish, but there may be times when we want to change the order in which those instructions get executed based on something that happens when the program runs.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=18)** Perhaps we don't want to execute certain parts of the code, or maybe we want to repeat a set of instructions multiple times.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=26)** Rust has several mechanisms we can use to control the flow of a program's execution in those ways.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=32)** If expressions let us control which code gets executed depending on conditions the program can evaluate at runtime.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=40)** It's like choosing which path to take when you reach a fork in the road.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=45)** To demonstrate that, let's write an if expression to conditionally execute some code based on the value of x, which is declared here on line two.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=54)** We start a new if expression with the keyword if followed by the condition, which is an expression that evaluates to a boolean value of true or false.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=64)** This expression with the equality comparison operator will return true if x is equal to three and false otherwise.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=71)** After that, we use curly braces to enclose the block of code we want to conditionally execute.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=77)** And for now, let's print a message that x is three.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=85)** When I save and run this code, this condition evaluates to true, because x is three, so it executes the block of code with the print statement.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=96)** If we change the value of x to something else, let's make it four, and then run the program again.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=106)** Now the conditional expression will evaluate to false, so the print statement does not get executed and we don't see any output from the program.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=115)** Some programming languages, like C++, allow you to simply use a numeric variable as the condition for an if expression.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=124)** In those languages, if x was anything other than zero, it would evaluate to true, and if x is zero, that corresponds to false.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=133)** However, if I try to run this code in Rust, the compiler stops us, because the if expression expects a boolean value for the condition, not an integer.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=145)** As usual, Rust is being super strict in regard to data types.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=149)** If we replace that condition with a literal boolean value of true and run it again, now it successful complies and runs.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=162)** This code will always print the message that x is three, which makes the if expression somewhat useless here, so I'll change it back.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=171)** Now, conditional expressions involving numbers can do a lot more than simply check if a variable is a specific value.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=178)** We can use other comparison operators to check whether it's greater than or less than a certain value, or perhaps if it's anything other than a specific value by using the not equals comparison operator.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=191)** That will evaluate to true if x is not three, and we can spice this up a little more by adding a bit of math to it.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=203)** This expression will compare the value of x plus one to determine whether or not it's equal to three.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-execution?u=76281980&t=209)** Conditional expressions can get quite elaborate in Rust, as long as the expression eventually evaluates down to a single boolean value.

#### Multiple conditions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If expressions can be used for more than just deciding whether or not to execute a single block of code.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=7)** We can also use them to evaluate multiple conditions to decide which code gets executed among several possible options.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=15)** For example, let's build an if expression based on these two variables, X and Y.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=21)** If X is greater than Y, let's print a message saying that.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=29)** This print statement will get executed if X greater than Y evaluates to true.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=34)** But if the condition turns out to be false and we want to take a different path, we can do that by adding an else block at the end of the if expression.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=44)** And for here, we'll print a message that X is not greater than Y.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=52)** When I run this code, the condition X greater than Y evaluates to false, so it executes the else block instead, printing the message X is not greater than Y.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=65)** Now, if we think about it, if X is not greater than Y, that could mean that X is less than Y or that X is equal to Y.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=75)** Let's say we want to print a unique message in either of those cases.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=79)** One way to accomplish that would be to use nested if expressions.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=84)** Let's replace the code in our else block with another if expression to check if X is less than Y, and if so, print a corresponding message.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=95)** And then, the else block for that will contain a print statement that X is equal to Y.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=109)** Notice that I've indented the nested if expressions on lines eight through 12, compared to the if/else block that's holding it.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=116)** This is common practice to make the code more readable and visually differentiate the nested levels, but the rust compiler doesn't care.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=124)** Again, the indentation is just to make it look nice for the programmer.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=130)** When I run this program, the condition on line five will evaluate the false, so its corresponding else block will get executed.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=139)** Then, the expression on line eight will evaluate to true, and therefore print the statement that X is less than Y.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=146)** Now, there will be times when it makes sense to write nested if/else expressions with multiple levels, as we've done here, but in this particular case, we can write the same logical sequence in a slightly cleaner fashion by using an else/if block.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=162)** To demonstrate that, I'll copy and paste the code we've already written, then in the copy, I'll modify the code to turn the else block on line 17 into an else/if expression.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=176)** This will execute if the condition that X is less than Y is true, and if so, it'll print the corresponding message.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=183)** I'll delete the last curly brace, which is now extra, and then clean up the indentation to make this more readable.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=194)** When this section of code executes, it will first check the condition on line 15.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=199)** If that's not true, it'll move down the sequence to check the condition on line 17, and if that's also not true, it will execute the else block and print that X is equal to Y.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=210)** So both of these expressions accomplish the same thing, just in slightly different ways.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-conditions?u=76281980&t=215)** And when I run this program, we can see that they both evaluate to the same result, printing the message that X is less than Y.

#### Conditional assignment
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When we build out complex if else structures with multiple possible paths of execution, it can create the potential for problems.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=8)** Consider the example code shown here, which initializes the make X odd variable on line two with a value of true, and then declares a second variable X on line three, which is not initialized.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=21)** That initialization is left to the if else structure on lines five through nine.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=25)** If make X odd is true, then we assign it a value of one.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=30)** Otherwise we'll assign it to two.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=32)** At the end of the program, the print macro on line 11 displays the value of X.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=39)** If I run this program, it works as we should expect.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=43)** Since make X odd is set to true, we get an output message that X is one.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=49)** Now, since make X odd is always true here, the else block on line seven will never get executed.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=56)** So there's no harm in commenting that out, right?
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=61)** Let's try running that again.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=64)** And now it fails to compile.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=67)** The compiler tells us that the print statement on line 11, which uses the variable X, could potentially use a variable before it's been initialized to a value.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=77)** In this specific example, we know that X will always be assigned the value one, because make X odd is always set to true.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=85)** But if the condition for the if expression on line five was based on something more dynamic that was determined at runtime, there is the potential that X would not get initialized before we use its value for the print statement.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=99)** The Rust compiler is keeping us safe by checking every possible path of execution between declaring X on line three and using it on line 11 to make sure it will always get initialized to a value.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=112)** It's protecting us from using uninitialized variables.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=116)** Using an if else structure to simply set the value of a variable to one of two possible values based on a condition is a common enough operation that Rust provides a compact shorthand that will allow us to replace all the code on lines nine through three with a single line, so I'll comment those out.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=136)** Now, in Rust, the if keyword technically creates an expression, which means it returns a resulting value.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=144)** That means we can use an if expression as the right side of an assignment statement.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=150)** So we can modify the let statement on line three to assign the value of X based on the value of the make X odd variable.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=159)** If make X odd is true, we'll assign the value of one.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=164)** Else, we'll assign two.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=168)** When I run this program, we see the output that X is one.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=174)** So this shorthand statement accomplished the same thing as the original program but in a more compact form, For fun, let's try modifying the else block in that statement to return a floating point value.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=187)** But we'll leave the first if block as an integer.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=191)** When I try running this program, the compiler complains that if and else have incompatible types.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=199)** This doesn't work because Rust needs to know the data type for every variable at compile time.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=204)** According to this code, X could end up being an integer or it could be a float.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/conditional-assignment?u=76281980&t=210)** So when you're using the result from an if expression as part of an assignment statement, or something else, make sure all of the if and else blocks return the same data type.

#### Loops

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] There will often be times when we need our program to repeat an action by executing a block of code multiple times.

#### While loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - The keyword while is used to create a while loop in Rust that will continuously repeat a block of code while a specific condition is true.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=10)** To demonstrate that let's create a while loop that repeatedly increments, the count variable declared on line 2, until it reaches the value 10.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=19)** We'll start with the keyword while followed by a condition.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=22)** We want this loop to continue repeating while count is less than 10, then we'll add curly braces to enclose the block of code we'll increment the count variable and then print it's value.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=40)** This loop, we'll start by evaluating the conditional expression count less than 10.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=44)** And if that's true, it will execute the block of code.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=48)** Then it loops around to check that condition again.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=51)** And if it's still true, it will execute once more and so on.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=57)** When I run this program, we can see that the process repeats until the count is incremented to 10.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=62)** It prints that value.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=64)** And then the loop checks the condition one last time, that time count is no longer, less than 10.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=70)** So execution continues past the loop and the program finishes.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=74)** Something to keep in mind when using a while loop is that since it evaluates the condition before each iteration of the code block, it's possible that the loop might never execute the code at all.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=86)** For example, if we change the initial count value to 20 And run the program again we don't get any output messages because the while loop sees that the condition is immediately false at the beginning and never executes.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=104)** Now, in the previous video, we looked at how a regular Rust loop can be used with an F expression and a break statement to exit when a certain condition is met.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=115)** That might seem a lot like what we're doing here with a while loop.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=118)** In fact, these two pieces of code would functionally accomplish the same thing to repeatedly do something as long as the count is below 10.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=127)** Notice, there is a difference in the comparison operator when we use the F expression in a loop which exits when the count is greater than or equal to 10 or as the while loop checks for the count to be less than 10 before entering into another loop iteration.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=143)** When we want to create an infinite loop that executes forever many programming languages, accomplish that by using a while loop whose condition is set to always be true.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=153)** And in Rust, both of these examples would repeat indefinitely.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=157)** However, under the hood, Rust does treat loop and while as two separate constructs with different capabilities.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=165)** One key difference here is that the break statement in a loop can be used to return a value.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=171)** The while loop can also use a break statement to exit prematurely but it does not have the same ability to return a value.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=180)** One common use for loops is to iterate through the elements of an Array, either looking for something in particular or to perform some kind of processing on the data.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=190)** To demonstrate that let's create a new Array with some letters and then reset the count value back to zero.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=202)** Then we'll use the counter value within the loop to index a letter from the Array and print its value.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=209)** We'll also move the line that increments the count to after the print statement.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=213)** So the first letter that gets retrieved will be at index zero.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=222)** When I run this code, it compiles and successfully prints all the letters in the Array, ABC.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=231)** But after that, the counter gets incremented again beyond the bounds of the Array which causes the program to panic and crash.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=239)** To stop that, we need to prevent the while loop from incrementing the counter beyond the length of the Array.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=245)** One way to do that is to use the Array's length method by using the Array's length in this comparison we can make sure the counter doesn't go out of bounds.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=258)** When I write in the code now, it works, the loop prints ABC.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=264)** Then the counter gets incremented one more time which puts it beyond the length of the Array so the loop exits without crashing.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=271)** Now using the Array length as part of the conditional expression worked in this case but this is just a simple example as your programs grow larger and more complex and go through changes from you and other developers using a while loop to index Array elements still has some potential for bugs like this in the future.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=290)** And that leads us to our third type of loop.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/while-loops?u=76281980&t=293)** The for-loop.

#### For loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous video, we saw the potential danger of using a while loop encounter to index array elements.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=7)** The better option would be to use a for loop, which is designed to repeatedly execute a block of code for each item in a collection, such as an array.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=18)** To demonstrate that, let's use a for loop to iterate through the elements of the array of characters on line two that spells out hello.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=27)** We'll execute our loop for each item in the message array.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=33)** And after that, the block of code will simply print the item.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=44)** When I save and run this code, we see the letters of our message array printed out one item at a time, spelling hello.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=57)** Under the hood, before processing those array elements, this for loop is actually converting the message array into something called an iterator.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=67)** An iterator implements the necessary logic to iterate over each item in a collection and determine when we've reached the end.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=75)** The iterator has a method named next, which returns the next item in the sequence.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=81)** Notice that we're not explicitly calling that next function in our code here, and that's because the for loop does that automatically.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=89)** It calls next to get the next array element, assigns that to the item variable and executes the block of code.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=97)** Then it repeats that process over and over until the iterator reaches the end.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=103)** Now, it's important to point out that I've updated this particular video to use a newer version of Rust than the majority of this course and that's because Rust version 1.53 implemented the into iterator trait for arrays, which you can read about in this release announcement on the Rust blog.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=122)** Understanding this update involves the Rust concepts of traits and references, which are both more advanced topics we'll cover later in this course, so that makes the change difficult to explain in detail now.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=135)** In short, if you're using an earlier version of Rust than 1.53, the code shown here will fail to compile.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=142)** Arrays could only be iterated over by reference, and that can be done by calling the iter method on the array.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=150)** Keep in mind, since I used Rust version 1.48 to record most of the videos in this course, you'll see me use this iter method in those other videos when iterating over an array with a for loop.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=163)** Now, moving on, there may be times when in addition to retrieving each item, we also want to know its index.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=171)** And that can be done by using the enumerate method on the iterator.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=176)** This creates an iterator, which returns a tuple with a pair of values, the current iteration count, along with the next value.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=185)** That iteration count corresponds to the item's index within the sequence, so let's destructure the tuple to capture its components as individual variables, and then add it to the print statement to display along with the item.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=208)** When I run this program, now we can see the index values for each letter.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=217)** Zero is h, one is e and so on.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=221)** Just as with the other two types of loops, if we need to exit a for loop early, we can use the break keyword, so let's add some logic to break out of this loop when we encounter the letter E.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=235)** If item is equivalent to e, then break.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=244)** Now, if I try to run this code, the compiler gives us an error.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=251)** It says that &char equal to char is not implemented.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=257)** This ampersand in front of the char data type indicates that instead of giving us the actual char value, the enumeration iterator is giving us a reference to a character element within the array.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=269)** Again, we'll cover the concept of references in a lot more detail later in this course but for now, know that if we want to get the actual value for that character, we'll need to include an ampersand operator in front of item.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=283)** With that in place, I can compile and run this code, and the loop exits after encountering the letter e.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=294)** Now, one more way you'll commonly see for loops used is to iterate over a range of numbers.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=305)** We can create a range by specifying the start value, dot, dot and then the end value.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=312)** This range will be inclusive of the start value but exclusive of the end value.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=318)** So the range shown here will include the values zero through four.
>
> **[5:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=323)** Let's print those numbers.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=334)** Run this code.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=339)** And we can see that the for loop iterates over that range to print the numbers zero through four and then the loop completes.
>
> **[5:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=347)** With three different types of loops to choose from, it can sometimes be a challenge to decide which one to use.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=353)** When you simply need to repeat a block of code over and over potentially forever, then use loop.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=360)** And if you need to return an output value when the loop exits, then using loop is your only option.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=366)** If you need to continuously repeat a block of code, as long as some condition you can check for with an expression is true, that's perfect for a while loop.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/for-loops-14412574?u=76281980&t=376)** And finally, to iterate over the items in a collection, and execute some code for each one, use a for loop, or if you need to repeat a block of code for a specific number of times that you know before starting the loop, you can use a for loop to iterate over the range from zero to that value.

#### Nested loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In rust, multiple loops can be nested inside of one another.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=4)** And one common reason for doing that is to process the elements of a multi-dimensional array.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's say we want to display the elements of this three by three array named matrix.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=15)** Since this is a two dimensional array we can use a pair of nested four loops to iterate through it and individually access each of the elements.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=25)** Each iteration of the outer loop, iterating over the matrix or return a one-dimensional sub-array which we'll call a row.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=32)** Then we'll use an inner loop to iterate through each row and return each of the elements, which will be numbers.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=40)** Finally, within that inner loop, we'll print each number.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=45)** Notice that I'm using print rather than print line, which means this print statement will not add a new line at the end of the string.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=53)** Also notice I'm using this special sequence backslash T which we haven't encountered yet.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=59)** This will insert a tab character after the number.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=63)** To print each row on a separate line, we'll use the print macro to insert a new line, immediately after the Interloop finishes.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=71)** When I run this program, we can see the values from the matrix separated by tab spacing with each row on its own line reading the array values to print them was easy enough but what if we want to modify those values in the array?
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=87)** To do that, we'll first need to declare the array as being mutable.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=93)** Then in the four loops, rather than using the inter method to get an inter-rater to loop over, we'll need to use a related function, iter_mute which gives us an iterator that returns mutable references.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=109)** And then within the inner loop let's use the plus equals operator to add 10 to each number.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=116)** When I run this program, the compiler gives us an error.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=123)** The problem is that the mutable iterator is returning mutable references to each integer value and that's not compatible with the plus equal operator.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=133)** Fortunately, the compiler gives us this handy help message suggesting we can dereference the numb variable by adding an asterisk in front of it.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=142)** We haven't covered dereferencing and D referencing variables in depth yet.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=147)** So for now, let's just follow the compilers advice.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=152)** I'll run the program again with that change.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=156)** And now the output shows all of the array values incremented by 10.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/nested-loops?u=76281980&t=161)** This example, not only demonstrated how to work with multi-dimensional arrays, using nested for loops but also how to modify the values when iterating through an array.

#### Solution: Max, min, mean
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=0)** (electronic music) [Instructor] To determine the max and min values in the array, I decided to use a for loop to iterate through the array to check each number and see if it's greater than the max value we've encountered thus far, or less than the minimum value, and update those variables accordingly.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=21)** I'll create that for loop with for num, in numbers.iter, and then, within the loop, I'll use an if expression to check if the current number is greater than max.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=35)** If so, I'll update the max by assigning it that value.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=40)** And then, if the current number was not greater than max, perhaps it was less than min, so I'll use an else/if clause to check for that condition.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=53)** And if so, I'll assign the number to min.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=56)** Now, for these two comparison operations to work, max and min both need to have an initial value to compare with the current number.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=64)** So, before the loop begins, I'll initialize both of those variables to the first number in the array.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=72)** That's it for the max and min values.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=74)** Now, to calculate the mean.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=76)** Since this for loop iterates through all the values in the array, I can also use it to sum them together.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=82)** So, before the loop, I'll initialize the mean variable to zero.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=88)** And then, within the loop, after the if/else structure define the max and min values, I'll use the plus equals operator to add the current number to the mean variable.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=99)** Now, since the mean variable is a floating point value, but the numbers in the array are integers, I'll need to cast the number into a floating point value so I can add it to the mean.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=111)** Finally, after the loop finishes, I'll divide the mean variable, which contains the sum of all the values, by the number of elements in the array.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=120)** And I'll get that by using the length function.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=124)** Again, since the length function returns an integer type, I'll need to cast it to be a floating point value so I can use it with the mean variable.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=133)** That completes my solution, so I'll give it a test run with Cargo.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-max-min-mean?u=76281980&t=139)** It passes all of the assert equal statements, and we get the final message that the tests passed.

### 6. Ownership

#### Variable scope
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] It's time to shift our focus from learning about basic programming constructs like functions and loops to discuss some of the key concepts you'll need to consider when programming in Rust.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=12)** Our first concept is scope which describes the region of a program or a specific variable is valid and can be used.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=20)** You'll hear the term coming into scope to describe when the variable becomes valid and it will remain valid and available to use until it goes out of scope.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=31)** In Rust, a variable binding is constrained to live within the block of code where it was created which is a section of code enclosed by a pair of curly braces.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=41)** We created new blocks of code every time we write a function, a loop or a conditional expression and you can even use a pair of curly braces on their own to define a block of code with its own scope.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=54)** Consider the scope of the planet variable initialized here.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=58)** It lives between the curly braces of the main function so it can be used anywhere within the main functions code after being initialized online too.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=67)** Now, let's create a new block of code within the main function by using an IF expression and for the sake of a simple example, we'll make the condition true so this block of code will always execute.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=82)** Now, the scope of planet is constrained to this inner block starting on line three when it's initialized and comes into scope to line five when the block ends and it goes out of scope.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=94)** The print statement to use planet is also within that same scope.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=97)** So when I run this program, it's able to use that variable and print the message that planet is earth.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=107)** Now, let's see what happens if we try to access planet after this block of code by copying and pasting that print macro.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=116)** I'll save that change and run the program again.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=121)** And now we get a compiler error for the second print statement on line six.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=126)** It says that planet could not be found in this scope because it goes out of scope at the end of the IF expression and therefore doesn't exist anymore for us to use by line six.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=137)** We can change the scope of the planet variable by changing where it gets declared up a few lines before the IF expression.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=145)** Now planet is constrained to the scope of the main function which is bigger and includes the IF expression and if I run the program now it's able to execute both print macros because they both exist within the larger scope of the main function.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/variable-scope?u=76281980&t=162)** As we'll learn later, this concept of scope plays a critical role in Rust programs because it determines when variables will be stored in memory and when they go away.
