#### Shadowing variables
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] When we first learned about variables, we saw that unless you explicitly declare a variable to be mutable, by default Rust will make it immutable, meaning its value can not be changed.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=13)** However, Rust will allow us to declare a new variable with the same name as an existing one.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=19)** And when we do that, the new variable will shadow or mask the previous variable as long as that new variable is in scope.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=28)** When we access that new variable name, we'll see the value from the second variable, which is shadowing the first one, which can make it seem as though we've replaced or modified the original variable.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=39)** Consider this variable named planet on line two.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=43)** To shadow it, we'll declare a new variable named planet later in the program using a second let statement.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=50)** We'll make this planet Mars, and then we'll print the value of planet again after shadowing it.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=61)** When I run this program, we see the first print macro on line three shows that the planet is Earth.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=68)** And then after that, it gets shadowed.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=70)** And the second macro on line five sees the planet as Mars.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=74)** Keep in mind this is different than changing the value of a mutable variable because we're using let statements to actually create a new variable to shadow the first one.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=84)** If we remove let from that second statement and try to run the program again, now it fails to compile because it's trying to modify the value of the original planet variable, which is immutable.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=99)** Since we're declaring a new variable with the let keyword, we can use this opportunity to give the shadowing variable a different data type than the original.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=108)** Let's change this string literal to the number four, since Mars is the fourth planet from the sun.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=117)** In addition to changing the data type, we can also use shadowing to change whether or not a variable is mutable.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=124)** I'll run that again, and now the planet has gone from Earth to four.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=132)** Now, since all of this code is within the same scope of the main function, after the original planet variable is shadowed, we only see the new planet variable for the rest of the program, but if these variables have different scopes, then the shadowing will only last as long as the shadowing variable is valid.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=152)** To demonstrate that, let's use a pair of curly braces to define a separate code block around the second shadowing planet.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=161)** And I'll also add one more print macro at the end of the program.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=169)** When I run this program now, the first print macro on line four shows that the planet is Earth.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=178)** Then the second print macro on line six shows it as four after being shadowed.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=183)** However, when the code block ends on line seven, the shadowing planet will go out of scope and disappears, but the original planet from line two is still valid so the final print macro shows the planet as Earth once again.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=198)** When used correctly, shadowing can be useful for things like reusing common variable names for different purposes, and the ability to intentionally change the data type or mutability of a variable can also come in handy in certain situations.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=212)** That said, if you're not careful and you unintentionally shadow an existing variable, that can lead to strange bugs in your program.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/shadowing-variables-14412576?u=76281980&t=221)** So always pay attention and think about the potential effects of shadowing when declaring new variables with let.

#### Stack and heap memory
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So far, we've looked at how information can be stored using different data types, but we have not talked about where that data is held in memory, how it's organized and how your program accesses it.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=12)** In some higher level programming languages like Python, you don't need to worry about any of that because those details are all abstracted away from you as the programmer.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=22)** But Rust is designed for lower levels systems programming, and which part of memory a variable lives in can affect how you use it.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=30)** In Rust, the program memory that's used to hold working data is divided into two sections called the stack and the heap, which store and access data in different ways.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=41)** Values in the stack are stored sequentially in the order they receive.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=46)** Those values are added and removed in a last in, first out or LIFO manner, which means when we remove data from the stack, the value that was added most recently will be the first one to go.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=58)** Conceptually, I like to think of the stack like a stack of boxes full of data.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=63)** When our program does things like declare local variables and call functions that adds data to the stack and more boxes get piled on top.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=72)** For example, consider the main function shown here.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=76)** When main declares a new variable named x that adds a box to the stack, storing the integer value 13.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=83)** After that main calls another function named function 1, which declares two more variables.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=89)** y is a floating point value and z is a character.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=93)** This process of adding more boxes is called pushing data onto the stack.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=98)** Functions 1, then calls function 2, which begins by declaring an array of numbers, which pushes another box onto the stack.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=107)** In addition to the variable shown here, the stack will also hold other information the program needs related to things like calling functions.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=114)** However, for simplicity, we'll only consider the variables for this example.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=119)** Now after function 2 is done doing whatever it does it will return, and when that happens, the array declared locally will go out of scope, so it gets removed from the top of the stack, which is called popping data off the stack.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=135)** Then when function 1 finishes executing, it will return, and the two variables it declared locally x and z will also be popped off the stack.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=145)** As we can see the stack is automatically growing and shrinking as the program executes.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=151)** One advantage of the stack is that data can be pushed and pop very quickly.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=156)** When a value gets pushed onto the stack, there's only one possible place it can go, the top.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=161)** So the program does not have to make complex decisions on where to put that data in memory.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=167)** Additionally, data in the stack can be directly accessed very quickly.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=172)** Now one limitation of the stack is that it's small maybe only a few megabytes, so we can't store a ton of data on the stack.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=180)** Another key limitation is that all the values stored on the stack must have a known fixed size, which can be determined by the compiler.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=190)** The boxes in the stack can be different sizes based on their data type, but that size is constant.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=195)** We cannot dynamically resize a box in the stack to make it bigger because it's stuck in place between other boxes above and below it.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=203)** This limitation of the stack is significant because we may not always know how much memory will be needed to hold each and every value in our program at compile time.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=213)** And that's where the heap comes in.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=216)** You can think of the heap like storing boxes of data in a giant warehouse.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=220)** To add new data to the heap the program first searches for a large enough section of memory to hold it like looking for open shelf space in the warehouse, when it finds a viable spot, it marks that chunk of memory as being used, which is called allocating, and then you can store your box of data on that shelf.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=239)** Now accessing data stored in the heap is a bit more complicated than the stack because the heap is less organized in the sense that a specific box could be anywhere in the warehouse.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=250)** As part of the allocation process when Rust assigns us a chunk of memory, it gives us the location of that memory, so we can access it using a pointer, which is a data type that stores a memory address.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=263)** Pointers have a fixed size which means the pointer itself can be stored on the stack.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=268)** You can think of pointers like an inventory sheet which tells you where to find a specific box in the warehouse.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=275)** Since we have to follow a pointer, accessing data on the heap is slower than the stack.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=281)** Adding new data to the heap is also slower because we have to search for an available section of memory.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=287)** The benefit of the heap is that we can dynamically add and remove data, which enables us to do things like resize data structures on the heap.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/stack-and-heap-memory?u=76281980&t=296)** Also compared to the stack the heap can provide a ton of usable memory space, so when we need to store really large data structures we'll need to put them on the heap.

#### String data type
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All of the data types we've used so far in this course from integers to tuples all have a fixed size that's known to the compiler, which means they'll be stored on the stack.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=11)** Before we can continue forward to explore Rust's key concept of ownership, we'll need to learn about a new data type that gets stored on the heap, the string.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=21)** Now, there are two ways to work with a string or sequence of characters in Rust.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=26)** We've already seen one type of string written out and enclosed in double quotes.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=32)** This is called a string literal because it's literally written in the code.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=37)** The compiler will hard-code that sequence of characters into the executable file, and when the program runs, it gets loaded into memory.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=45)** When the program needs to use the literal value, it will access it through a reference and we'll cover that in a bit more detail later in this course.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=54)** String literals are certainly handy and we've used them in all of our print macros, but they have limitations.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=61)** For one, they're immutable, meaning they cannot be changed.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=66)** Additionally, the value of the literal must be known before the program gets compiled because it's written into the programs code.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=74)** That means we cannot use a literal to store a string that's dynamically produced at runtime such as input from the user because we don't know what the user will enter.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=84)** It could be a short message or something longer which would require more memory space to hold.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=89)** We won't know how much memory we'll need to store what that input message is until the program runs.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=96)** For that, we'll need to use the String type which is different than a string literal.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=102)** Data for the String type is stored in allocated memory on the heap.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=107)** That enables the string to be mutable so it can be dynamically generated, resized, and changed as needed during program execution We can create a new string variable named message with an initial value by typing String with a capital S, ::from, and then passing it a string literal as an input argument.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=132)** This :: is a path operator which we'll cover in a later video and it allows us to access the from function associated with the String type.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=142)** Earth, here in double quotes, is a hard-coded string literal.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=147)** And the String type's from function will take that literal and use it to initialize the new string.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=153)** So the sequence of characters E-A-R-T-H will get stored on the heap.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=159)** They're stored in order so we can think of each character having an index within the string.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=164)** To access the strings stored on the heap, our program will hold a pointer to it on the stack.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=170)** That includes the memory address of the first character in the string so we know where to find it, the length of the string so we know how many characters are in it, and the capacity so we know how much room is allocated for it on the heap.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=184)** In this case, the capacity is the same as the length, but there could be situations where the string has been allocated more space on the heap than it currently needs, which leaves it with room to grow.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=194)** So the capacity should always be greater than or equal to the length.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=199)** Because the string is stored on the heap instead of the stack, we can dynamically change it.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=204)** In order to do that, we'll first need to make the string variable mutable.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=209)** Then, we can use the push string method to append a second string to the end of our original message.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=216)** We'll extend it to say that Earth is home.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=221)** Doing that grows the message on our heap to include the appended string.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=226)** If the program had to move the message from one part of the heap to another, to find a chunk of memory big enough to hold the extended message, it will update the pointer address on the stack accordingly.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=237)** It will also update the length and capacity information to represent the new size of the string.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=243)** To view those changes happening, let's add a print macro before the push string method,
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=253)** and again after it.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=257)** When I run this program, you can see that the message started as simply Earth, and then it was dynamically grown to say, Earth is home.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=268)** There are a ton of methods that can be used to manipulate strings and Rust.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=272)** We'll encounter a few more as we continue through this course, but I encourage you to explore the documentation page for the String type if you want to learn about all the ways you can manipulate strings.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/string-data-type?u=76281980&t=283)** Because they're stored on the heaps, strings can be modified in a lot of different ways.

#### Ownership
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the advantages of using the heap to store data is that it's significantly bigger than the stack but it's not infinitely big.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=10)** If we keep adding data to the heap without removing anything, we'll eventually run out of space and memory.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=16)** So, we'll need to free up blocks of allocated memory, after they're no longer needed by the program.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=22)** This task isn't unique to Rust and there are a few common approaches that other programming languages use for memory management.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=30)** One traditional approach is to make the programmer responsible for explicitly allocating and de allocating memory in their code.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=38)** For example, in C and C++, a programmer calls the memory allocation function named malloc, to request a block a memory of a certain size.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=47)** And then when they're done using it, they can call the free function to release that memory so it can be used for something else.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=55)** This approach gives the programmer a lot of control, which can enable a skilled programmer to write really efficient code.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=62)** The downside is that it can easily produce bugs because it's hard to make sure every possible path of execution, from a function call to allocate memory eventually leads to a corresponding call to free it.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=75)** Memory leaks occur when a program allocates memory but then fails to ever release it later.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=80)** And invalid memory accesses can occur when a program tries to use memory after it's already been freed.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=87)** Another common approach to memory management is garbage collection, where the program automatically runs a sub-routine that looks for unused memory and cleans it up.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=97)** Lots of modern high level programming languages use garbage collection including Java, Python, C#, Ruby and Go.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=105)** The advantage is that it's easy.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=107)** The programmer doesn't need to worry about memory management.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=111)** The downside of garbage collection is that it can be inefficient compared to explicit management.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=116)** And we can't always control when the garbage collector will run, which can cause a program to slow down or pause at critical moments.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=125)** Rust takes a third somewhat unique approach to memory management based on a system of ownership, which puts variables in charge of freeing their own resources according to a set of rules that can be checked by the compiler.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=138)** Every value or piece of data in a Rust program is associated with one and only one variable called its owner.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=147)** And when that owning variable goes out of scope, it's value will be dropped and the memory freed accordingly.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=154)** As we'll see later, there are ways to transfer ownership of a value from one variable to another but at any given time that value can only have one owner.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=164)** These rules are simple to describe and they bring some big advantages.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=169)** For one, the ownership approach protects against bugs like invalid access, which are common with explicit allocation and de allocation in other languages.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=179)** So, it helps guarantee the code you write with Rust is safe.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=182)** The other big advantage of ownership is that it's efficient because the Rust compiler is able to determine when data will be dropped at compile time so we don't have the unwanted overhead of a garbage collector running in the background while our program executes.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=197)** The downside of ownership is that while the concept is simple to describe, in practice, it can cause a lot of pain for programmers that are new to Rust and not used to programming with ownership.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=210)** And it's part of the reason why Rust has gained a reputation as being a difficult language to learn.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=215)** Programming with ownership isn't necessarily hard but it does require you to think differently than in other languages.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/ownership?u=76281980&t=222)** Keep that in mind as you continue to learn Rust and embrace the ownership mindset.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (3), else. (1), require (1), continue (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2), python (1), ruby (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Moving, cloning, and copying data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The key restriction that resources can only have one owner at any given time may sound simple but it can complicate things because different types of data work differently When we try to use them with multiple variables.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=16)** Consider this string variable on line three named inner planet.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=20)** I've named it inner planet because it's declared inside the scope of the curly braces between lines two to five.

>

>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=105)** This may seem like simple and expected behavior from the outside, but what's going on under the hood is important to understand.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=114)** Remember that the string type stores data on the heap.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=117)** So the letters dispel mercury lived on the heap and we assign that to a string variable on the stack named inner planet, which became its owner.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=127)** When we later assign that value of inner planet to outer planet that copied the value from one string variable to another.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=135)** That would mean we have two string variables on the stack that both point to the same chunk of memory on the heap.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=142)** That situation is similar to a shallow copy and other programming languages.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=147)** But according to the rust rules of ownership data can only have one owner at a time.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=153)** So when we assigned inner planet to outer planet rust invalidates the inner planet variable, leaving outer planet as the only owner of that string.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=163)** In rust terms transferring the ownership of heap data like this from one variable to another is called a move.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=172)** To witness the impact of that move.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=173)** Let's move the print macro on line five down another line.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=181)** Now the program will try to use the inner planet variable after it has moved ownership of the string to the outer planet.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=189)** When I try to run this program, the compiler is able to detect that we're trying to use inner planet after the value has been moved and it raises an error.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=199)** Now let's say we want to create a deep copy of our string meaning inner planet and outer planet will each be the owners of separate independent strings.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=209)** By default rust will never automatically copy data on the heap like that because doing so uses additional resources.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=217)** However, we can explicitly tell rust that that's what we want it to do by using the clone method to duplicate data.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=226)** This tells rust to duplicate the string data on the heap and assign that second copy to the outer planet variable.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=234)** So instead of ending up with two variables pointing to the same data on the heap which violates the rules of ownership we get two separate instances of mercury on the heap One for each variable.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=247)** Since we've cloned it two independent copies of the string what we do to one will not affect the other.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=253)** So if we use the clear method to remove all of the contents from the inner planet string, then run the program.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=263)** We see that the print macro on line seven sees the inner planet string as being empty.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=268)** And then the print macro from line nine sees the outer planet string as mercury.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=274)** Now this entire example thus far has used the string data type, which is stored on the heap.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=280)** Rust behaves differently when we're working with data types that are held entirely on the stack, such as energizers.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=286)** To demonstrate that let's change the data type of the outer planet variable from string to I32.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=294)** Then at line four, instead of assigning outer planet a new string, we'll give it an integer value one because mercury is the first planet in our solar system.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=305)** The clear method on line six doesn't apply when working with an integer.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=309)** So let's change that to an integer operation of adding one to the inner planet.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=315)** Now this program will successfully compile and run with the code shown here, but since we're working with integers now instead of strings we can get rid of the clone method.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=329)** And when I run this program it successfully compiles and runs.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=334)** We see that the value of inner planet gets incremented to two while the outer planet remains its original value of one.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=341)** So why is it that we can now use the inner planet variable in the print macro on line seven after moving its data to the outer planet variable.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=351)** That did not work before when we were using a string, the reason has to do with where the data lives.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=359)** Now that we're using integers the data for our inner planet integer lives completely on the stack and does not reference anything on the heap.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=368)** Our outer planet variable also has a known integer worth of space carved out for it on the stack.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=374)** So when we assign the variable from inner planet to outer planet, instead of being moved it's just as efficient to copy the value over leaving us with two separate values associated with the different variable.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=388)** So there's no conflict of ownership.
>
> **[6:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=391)** In general data types with a known size, which are therefore stored on the stack we'll implement the trait, which allows them to be copied rather than moved like a string, which lives on the heap.
>
> **[6:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=403)** Copying data on the stack occurs implicitly.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=407)** Whereas cloning data on the heap must be explicitly done using the clone method.
>
> **[6:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/moving-cloning-and-copying-data?u=76281980&t=412)** These differences in behavior are some of the reasons why it's important to understand whether certain values in your program are being stored on the stack or on the heap.

#### Transferring ownership
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=0)** - The distinction between stack and heap data and whether values are copied or moved also comes into play when passing variables as input arguments to functions.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=12)** In the main function shown here, we declare an integer variable named rocket_fuel on line two.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=18)** Pass it as an input argument to the process_fuel function and then print its value afterwards on line four.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=24)** The process_fuel function accepts that input as a parameter named propellant and then simply prints a message with it.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=31)** When I run this program, we see the print statement from the process_fuel function first, followed by the print statement on line four.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=39)** Pretty straightforward.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=41)** What's not obvious here, is how the value of rocket_fuel is being passed to the process_fuel function.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=49)** Since rocket_fuel is an integer, which lives on the stack, when it gets passed as an input argument, it passes a copy of its value.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=57)** The propellant variable takes ownership of that copy and the rocket_fuel variable in the main function retains ownership of the original value.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=66)** We can see the impact of that by making the propellant parameter mutable and then incrementing its value before the print macro.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=78)** When I run this code, we see that inside the process_fuel function, the print statement sees the incremented value of propellant as two, but then after the function completes, and the main function prints the value of rocket_fuel, it has the original value of one.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=95)** The propellant copy was modified, not the original rocket_fuel.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=100)** Now let's change rocket_fuel from being a stack-based integer to a heap-based string.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=108)** Our rocket will use RP-1 as its fuel.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=112)** We'll modify the process_fuel function to accept a string as input.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=117)** It doesn't need to be mutable and incrementing its value doesn't make sense with a string.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=125)** Let's try to run that.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=130)** And it fails to compile.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=132)** The compiler points to the print macro on line four, using the rocket_fuel variable after being moved.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=139)** The problem here is occurring because the string is stored on the heap.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=144)** When it gets passed as an input argument, Rust does not implicitly make a copy of the heap data.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=150)** Instead, the propellant variable gets a pointer to that heap data.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=154)** And in doing so, takes ownership of it.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=157)** Remember every value can only have one variable that owns it at a time.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=162)** So in this situation, we're transferring ownership of the RP-1 string on the heap from the rocket_fuel variable to the propellant variable.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=172)** Now let's consider what happens after the process_fuel function prints its message and finishes.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=178)** At that point, the propellant variable will go out of scope.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=182)** And since it's the owner of the string data, that data will be dropped and the memory it occupied on the heap will be freed up to use for other things.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=192)** The compiler stopped us at line four because by that point the print statement is trying to use the rocket_fuel variable, which no longer owns the string.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=201)** In fact, the data it had originally pointed to is now gone because it was dropped.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=207)** These type of issues are a common source of frustration for new Rust programmers because if you're not used to thinking in terms of ownership, it's not always obvious when and where ownership is being transferred.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=219)** But the ability of Rust to identify this problem at compile time and prevent us from trying to use data that no longer exists on line four is what makes Rust code safe and efficient.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=231)** Now that we've encountered a problem, let's fix it.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=234)** One possible solution here would be to use the clone method to explicitly make a second copy of the string by cloning rocket_fuel.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=243)** We can save and run the program.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=245)** And now it successfully compiles.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=248)** That would leave rocket_fuel as the owner of the original string and propellant will be the owner of a separate cloned copy.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=256)** Now, if process_fuel does something to modify the string, then any changes it makes to its copy will not be reflected back in the original rocket_fuel.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=266)** Cloning may not always be the behavior we want.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=269)** Let's get rid of the clone method.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=272)** If we want to use the same string for this entire program, then the process_fuel function will need to pass ownership of the string back to the main function when it's done.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=283)** And we can do that with a return value.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=286)** Let's modify process_fuel to return a string.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=295)** We'll add propellant on the last line as a return expression.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=302)** And then in the main function, we'll shadow the rocket_fuel variable to assign it the return value and therefore take ownership of the string.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=310)** When we run the program now, it successfully transfers ownership of the string to the process_fuel function to print the first message and then transfers ownership back to the main function to print the second message.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=324)** Now the string we're passing as a return value doesn't have to be the same one that was passed as input.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=330)** It could originate from within the function.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=333)** Let's create a new variable named new_fuel.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=337)** Initialize it with a new string.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=340)** And that string will say LNG.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=344)** And then we'll return that new_fuel instead of the propellant.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=349)** When I run the program now, the second message shows that after the function, the rocket_fuel variable is now the owner of the LNG string.
>
> **[5:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=359)** Since we're no longer passing back ownership of the original RP-1 string, when the process_fuel function completes, the RP-1 string will be dropped.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=368)** If you're feeling a bit unclear on the rules of ownership and how ownership is transferred, I encourage you to play around with the examples, or write your own program to practice passing around ownership of different data types.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=381)** To become an effective Rust programmer, you need to build an intuition around ownership and program with it in mind.
>
> **[6:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/transferring-ownership?u=76281980&t=388)** Otherwise you'll end up spending a lot of time fighting with the compiler.

### 7. References

#### Borrowing references
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've seen how to transfer ownership of data to and from functions using input arguments and return values but in many cases we may not actually want or need to transfer ownership of data to use it in a function.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=15)** To demonstrate why, let's modify the process fuel function on line seven so that in addition to printing a message it returns the length of the propellant string.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=26)** We can get the strings length by using the length function on it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=34)** And now we have two values that need to be returned that length value as well as the original propellant string to pass back ownership of it.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=43)** When we need to return multiple values from a function we can package them together into a tuple.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=49)** So, let's modify the functions return type and then change the return value to be a tuple containing propellant and length.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=65)** Next, in the main function let's destructure the tuple that gets returned into its separate parts and then modify the print statement on line four to also display the length.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=83)** I can run this program and our function successfully processes the input string and returns ownership of it's done along with the strings length.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=94)** So this approach works but it's cumbersome and it's not the best way to handle this situation in Rust.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=103)** The output from the process fuel function that we really care about is the length value.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=107)** We're only passing back the string because we need to transfer back ownership of it.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=112)** But if you think about it, ownership of that string is really just making a round trip here.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=117)** It would be nice if we could leave ownership of that string data with the rocket fuel variable in the main function and simply use it in the process fuel function.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=128)** This is where the concept of borrowing becomes incredibly useful.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=131)** Rust allows us to borrow or access and use data without actually taking ownership of it.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=138)** We do that by using the borrow operator which is represented with an ampersand symbol to create a reference to the variable we want to borrow.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=147)** Let's modify our function to borrow a reference to the string instead of passing it by value.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=153)** To do that, we'll change the input parameter data type from string to ampersand string which indicates this as a reference to a string.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=163)** Now we're borrowing the string rather than taking ownership of it.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=167)** So we don't need to pass it back.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=169)** We only need to return the length value we care about.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=172)** Up in the main function, on line three we're no longer getting back at tuple so we can assign the single return to the variable length.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=182)** And then finally, rather than passing the string by value when we call the process fuel function on line three, we need to pass a reference to it by using the borrow operator.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=193)** When I run this program the output looks the same as before but what's going on under the hood is very different.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=204)** Now, instead of the propellant variable taking ownership of the string data, propellant will be a reference that points to the rocket fuel string to borrow it.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=213)** With that reference, we can access the string data stored on the heap while the rocket fuel variable maintains ownership of it.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=221)** That means when the propellant variable eventually goes out of scope, the heat data does not get dropped because it's been owned by the rocket fuel variable all along.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=231)** Borrowing is incredibly common in Rust programs and when working with data types that are stored on the heap, it's much more common to see them pass by reference than by value.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/borrowing-references?u=76281980&t=240)** That said, when working with references it's still important to keep in mind which variable actually owns the data.

#### Mutable references
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If we want to change the value of a borrowed variable, we have to explicitly tell Rust that that's what we intend to do by marking it as mutable.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=9)** Let's say we want our process fuel function to modify its borrowed propellant string by extending the message to say that the fuel is highly flammable.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=19)** This will be modifying the original string declared up on line two.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=23)** So we'll definitely need to make that mutable.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=26)** Now, when I run this program, the compiler gives us an error when we try modifying the string.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=35)** The red text gives us the reason for the error that the data our propellant reference refers to is not borrowed as mutable and the blue text above that, suggest changing it to a mutable reference.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=48)** Thanks, Rust compiler.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=50)** Similar to how we have to explicitly declare variables as being mutable, we also have to let Rust know that we intend for references we're borrowing to be mutable.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=60)** And we do that by adding the mute keyword after the borrow operator.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=65)** Let's modify the parameter on line seven to be a mutable reference and then change the function call on line three to pass in a mutable reference to rocket fuel.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=79)** I'll run the program again with those changes.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=83)** And now the process fuel function is able to change the borrowed input string that simply says RP-1 into the longer message that RP-1 is highly flammable which is 25 characters long.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=96)** When working with mutable references, there's one significant restriction that you'll need to keep in mind, which is that once you create a mutable reference to a variable, you cannot create any other references to it within that scope.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=109)** This restriction can be a source of frustration for new programmers but it exists for a reason because it enables Rust to prevent a nasty problem known as a data race which can occur when multiple references can access and potentially modify the same data.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=125)** Data races become a concern when you start writing programs with multiple threads that execute concurrently which goes beyond the scope of this course.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=134)** But by placing this restriction on references, Rust is able to prevent the database problem at compile time which is another part of what makes Rust such a safe programming language.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=146)** So to summarize the rules for references, Rust will allow you to create a single mutable reference to a variable within a particular scope.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=155)** If you're only working with regular immutable references, then you can create as many of those as you want pointing to the same variable.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=163)** The restriction comes in when you try to create references in addition to the one allowed mutable reference.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/mutable-references?u=76281980&t=169)** Even if those additional references are immutable, it creates the potential for problems and the Rust compiler will not allow it.

#### Dangling references
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've seen how to pass borrowed references as input arguments to a function.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=5)** Now, let's look at what can happen when you try to return a reference.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=10)** In this example shown here, the produce fuel function creates a new string to represent the new fuel it's producing.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=17)** And then it returns a reference to that string.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=20)** Notice that the return type in the function definition includes the borrow operator.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=25)** And we're also using the borrow operator on line eight to get a reference to the new fuel variable.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=32)** Let's try to run this program and it fails to compile.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=38)** The red error message says something about lifetimes which is a topic we'll cover later in this course.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=44)** But if we look at the help message, a couple of lines below that it says this functions return type contains a borrowed value, but there is no value for it to be borrowed from.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=55)** Our problem is that the produce fuel function is creating what's known as a dangling reference.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=62)** The string it creates on line seven gets assigned to the new fuel variable as its owner.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=67)** However, new fuel goes out of scope at the end of this function.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=71)** So the string data will be dropped and freed from memory.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=75)** That means, by the time the print macro on line three tries to use a reference to access the string, the string no longer exists.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=83)** In other languages that use pointers like C++ using an old stale pointer that references a memory location that's no longer valid because it's been freed for other uses is a common source of nasty errors.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=96)** But in rust, that will never happen.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=98)** The compiler guarantees that we'll never use a dangling reference because it checks to make sure data will not go out of scope before a reference to it does.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=107)** The solution to the situation shown here is to return the new fuel string itself rather than just a reference to it.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=115)** So, I'll delete the borrow operators.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=118)** This will pass ownership of the string to the rocket fuel variable, so the data will still be available for the print statement to use.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=127)** When I run this program, now it's successfully compiles and we get the message that rocket fuel is RP-1.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=134)** If you accidentally create a dangling reference, it's not an issue because the rust compiler will catch it.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/dangling-references?u=76281980&t=140)** But you do need to understand what's going on, so you'll be able to fix the problem.

#### Slices
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When borrowing data types that hold a sequence of elements like an array or a string, if we only want to reference a subset of those elements rather than the entire collection, we can create what's called a slice which references a contiguous sequence of elements within a collection without taking ownership of them.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=20)** One of the most commonly used types of slices is the string slice whose data type is written as ampersand lowercase STR.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=29)** We've actually been using that string slice data type throughout this entire course every time we write a string literal.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=36)** The data for the string literal is hard-coded into the executable binary and the program uses a string slice to access it.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=44)** To demonstrate a string slice, let's create a reference to the string online too with a message, greetings from earth that slices out just the last word, earth.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=54)** We'll declare a new variable named last word, and then use the borrow operator to reference the message string.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=62)** If we stopped here, this would create a reference to the entire string.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=66)** To create a slice, we'll add square brackets at the end, to describe the range of characters we want to reference similar to how we index an array.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=75)** The E for earth is the 15th character of the string.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=78)** So that'll be our start index.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=80)** And since earth is five letters long, let's calculate the end index as 15 plus five.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=88)** Then after that, we'll simply print the value of that last word slice.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=97)** Now, we'll run this program and we see that the full message string says greetings from earth, but the last word slice is only earth.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=108)** In this example, the message variable is still the owner of the entire string on the heap with a pointer to the beginning of that string along with information about its length and capacity.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=119)** The slice we created has a pointer to the letter E at the beginning of earth to reference it without taking ownership.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=126)** And it has the length of the slice which in this case is five letters.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=132)** Now, let's see what happens if we extend the length of that slice to something bigger.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=136)** Let's make it 50 characters long.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=139)** This will definitely extend past the end of our message.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=143)** Since the string is dynamically allocated on the heap at runtime, the compiler doesn't necessarily know how long a string will end up being.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=151)** So when I try to run this program, it successfully compiles because the compiler can't check against the length to realize that our slices index will go out of bounds.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=164)** But when the program runs after printing the greetings from earth message, it panics and crashes when it tries to index the string online five.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=173)** If we want the range for our slice to go from a specific index to the end of the collection, we can simply remove that last index value.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=182)** When I run the program now, now the range for the slice runs to the end of the message.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=188)** So it includes the exclamation point after earth.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=192)** One subtle but very important detail to keep in mind when creating strings slices is that its length is a number of bytes, not characters.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=202)** However, since characters in Rust are encoded using UTF-8, individual characters can sometimes span multiple bytes.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=210)** It's essential that the range indices for your slice occur at a valid boundary between characters.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=216)** If you try to index in the middle of a multi byte UTF-8 character, the program will panic.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=222)** That wasn't it a concern in this example message greetings from earth, because those are basic letters that each take up one byte.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=229)** You'll need to be careful if you include special characters or emojis which often span multiple bytes.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=236)** Now, in addition to slicing strings, we can also create slices that reference other types of collections, such as an array.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=244)** An example of that is shown here on lines eight through 10 which initialize an array representing the eight planets in our solar system.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=251)** Sorry, Pluto.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=252)** You're no longer a full size planet.
>
> **[4:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=254)** The program then creates a slice to the first four planets in the array, which are known as the inner planets.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=261)** Although it's not required here, I've explicitly included the data type of this array slice so you can see how it's written as a borrowed reference to an array of i32 values.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices?u=76281980&t=273)** When I run this program, the print macro online 10 displays the slice containing numbers one through four representing mercury, Venus, earth and Mars.
