#### Slices as function parameters
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Being able to reference only part of a collection is incredibly useful and you'll commonly see slices used as function inputs and outputs, especially on functions that work with strings.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=12)** For example, the get_first_word function shown here on line seven takes a borrowed reference to an entire string as input and then returns a slice containing just the first word of that input string all without taking ownership of the string.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=27)** It does that by converting the input string reference into a slice of bytes representing its contents and then iterates through that slice of bytes one at a time, checking for the byte sequence that represents a space character with the if expression on line 11.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=43)** If it finds a space, that means we've reached the end of a word and it returns a slice of the input string ranging from its beginning up to the index where we found the first space.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=54)** If the for-loop searches the entire string without finding any spaces, that means it was all one word so line 16 returns a slice containing the entire input string.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=65)** Note that we're iterating over individual bytes in the for-loop rather than characters, and we do that because the index for the string slice is in terms of bytes.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=74)** If the first word contains a UTF-8 character that occupies multiple bytes, then trying to index based on the number of characters we've searched instead of bytes would cause problems.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=85)** When I run this program to get the first word from the message string on line two, the get_first_word function returns a string slice with the word Greetings.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=96)** The string slice works great here because it includes both a pointer to the beginning of the first word, along with its length, packaged together into a single data type.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=106)** In a world without slices, we would have to deal with that pointer and length information as individual values which is not only cumbersome, but also more prone to errors.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=116)** Now, in this example, we're passing the entire message string as the input to the get_first_word function, but what would happen if we gave it a string slice instead?
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=128)** Let's modify the reference on line three to turn it into a slice starting at the second word of the message, from, which begins at index 10, and then continue that slice to the end of the string.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=142)** When I try to run the program now, it fails to compile.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=149)** The compiler says it expected a reference to a string because that's the input type of the get_first_word function, but instead, the compiler found a string slice.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=161)** When dealing with strings, a borrowed reference to a string is not equivalent to a slice from the string.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=167)** They're different data types.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=169)** Remember that a borrowed string reference will point to an actual string on the stack, which in turn points to and owns the string data that lives on the heap.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=179)** And the string also stores the length and capacity of that heap data.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=184)** A slice on the other hand only stores a pointer to the heap data, along with length information.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=189)** The slice doesn't need to keep track of capacity because the slice will never own anything on the heap.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=196)** Since a string contains all of the necessary information to function as a slice, that is, it has a pointer to heap data and length, Rust will allow us to use a string reference in places that ask for a string slice.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=210)** This convenience is known as Deref coercion.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=213)** We'll use the string reference like a slice whose length is the entire string.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=219)** However, that coercion does not work in the opposite direction.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=223)** We can not use a string slice in place of a string reference because the slice is lacking some of the information that the reference can access.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=233)** To fix our example program, we'll need to change the function definition on line seven to use a string slice as the input parameter instead of a string reference.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=243)** Now we can run this program, passing that function the string slice on line three, and it successfully runs and shows us that the first word in that slice is from.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=257)** If we change that argument from a slice back to a string reference and run the program again, the program will still compile and run by coercing the string reference into a slice.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/slices-as-function-parameters?u=76281980&t=271)** As a rule of thumb, when you're writing functions to work with strings without taking ownership, you should use the string slice data type for the input and output parameters because it has the flexibility to also work with string references.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** function (8), type. (1), let (1), from, (1), continue (1)
> **Code Identifiers:** get_first_word (4)
> **Env Vars:** utf (1)
> **Definitions:** known as (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] All right folks it's time for our next challenge.

>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=24)** That's either a string reference or a string slice and it should return a string slice with any leading and trailing spaces removed.

#### Solution: Trim spaces
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=0)** - My solution to the challenge begins with the trim space function at line 25.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=10)** The functions input type is a string slice which enables it to accept both string references and slices.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=17)** And the output type is also a slice.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=20)** Within the function I use the for loop online 28 to search through the input string starting at the beginning to locate the first non-space character.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=31)** I'm using the care's method on the string S to get an iterator over the characters in the string and then using the enumerate method to get the iteration count along with the character value.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=43)** The if expression online 29 checks to see if the character is anything other than space and if so I saved the current index to the start variable which I'll use to create the final slice.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=55)** I don't need to search any further after that so I break out of the loop.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=60)** I can iterate through the string and index it in terms of characters here instead of fights because the space character is only a single bite.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=68)** That means the index value for the first non-space character will be the same as its bite index.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=76)** The second for loop at line 37 performs a similar process to find the location of the last non-space character in the string but it does so by searching the string in reverse from back to the front.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=89)** To do that I applied the rev or reverse method on the iterator to reverse its direction working backwards through the string when the if expression discovers the first non-space character it calculates the index of that last space by subtracting the current search index from the total length of the string and bytes.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=110)** Finally, online 44 my trim spaces function uses those start and end indices to create and return a string slice.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=119)** To prove that this works I'll run the program and I see the output message indicating it passed all seven of my test cases in the main function.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=130)** If you enjoyed this challenge and want some additional practice working with strings and slices try enhancing your trim spaces function to remove other types of white space as well such as new lines and tabs to make it more similar to the string types built in trim method.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=147)** Another bonus challenge idea is to write a function to return a slice containing the second word from a given string.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-trim-spaces?u=76281980&t=153)** If you choose to tackle either of those challenges I recommend writing a handful of test cases first so you can test it when you're done.

### 8. Modules

#### Rust Standard Library and prelude
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Like most programming languages, Rust has a standard library to provide a basic set of capabilities that most programs need.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=8)** It includes core data types, like the string type we've been using, common functions, shared macros and lots of other things.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=16)** Admittedly, the Rust standard library isn't as expansive as the standard libraries in some other languages like Python but it provides a minimal set of battle-tested code that you can trust and serves as a foundation for writing portable Rust software in the broader Rust ecosystem.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=34)** The documentation for the standard library is included when installing Rust and also available online from [rustlang.org](https://rustlang.org).
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=43)** The standard library is organized into a collection of modules, which you can see listed by clicking this link on the main documentation page.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=51)** Clicking on any one of these module names will take you to it's documentation page.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=56)** So, for example, if we want to write a multi threaded program, we'll probably need to use the thread module.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=63)** The format and amount of information in these documentation pages differs between modules but they usually provide an overview of the module along with example code showing how to use it.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=74)** Notice that this short example starts with the line, use std::thread.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=83)** To use modules from the standard library or other libraries which we'll cover later, you need to explicitly bring them into your program with the use declaration, which brings the path for that module into the scope of your program.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=97)** These use declarations are usually placed near the top of a program.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=101)** For example, that thread module is part of the standard library but it's not part of the Rust language itself.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=108)** If we tried to use that thread modules spawned function in our program without bringing it into scope, Rust wouldn't know where to find it because it doesn't know what thread refers to.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=118)** After we bring the thread module into scope with a use declaration, now, Rust knows where to find it, spawn is part of the thread module and thread is part of the standard library, which is abbreviated STD.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=132)** The standard library is available to all the code you write by default so you can easily bring in modules you need into scope using the use statement.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=142)** Now, you may be wondering why we haven't seen any of these use statements so far, in this course.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=147)** After all, we've been using the string module, which comes from the standard library.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=152)** Having to manually import every single module you needed every time you use it would be annoyingly verbose.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=159)** The creators of Rust realized this so they created the prelude, which is a list of things that rust automatically imports into every program.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=169)** Importing more than you need is not good programming practice so the prelude does not import the entire standard library.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=176)** It only imports a handful of the most modules that almost every rest program needs and it's intentionally kept as small as possible.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=186)** The standard library documentation includes this page, which lists out the preludes contents.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/rust-standard-library-and-prelude?u=76281980&t=191)** If you need a module that's not included in the prelude, you'll need to manually import it into your program with a use statement.

#### Standard input
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If we want the user to be able to interact with our program and provide input, one of the simplest ways to do that is through these standard input stream.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=9)** Which allows the user to type a message into the command line that our program can then read and use.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=15)** Functions for working with standard input are included in the rest standard libraries IO module.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=22)** The IO module is not included in the prelude, so to use it we'll need to manually import it at the top of our program with a use statement.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=32)** Now, after the user enters a message into the command line, when our program goes to read it from the standard input stream, we'll need a place to store it.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=41)** The data will come as a string, so let's create a new string named buffer.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=47)** This new buffer string is currently empty, but it's mutable.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=51)** So we'll be able to update it when we receive the input message.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=55)** We don't know how long the user's message will be but that's okay because the string type is stored on the heap, so it can dynamically resize to fit whatever input we get.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=65)** Next, let's print a message to let our user know we want them to type something.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=72)** Then from the IO module we'll use this standard in function to create a new handle to access the standard input stream.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=79)** Then use the read line function on that to get a line of input.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=83)** And finally, we'll pass a mutable reference to our buffer as an input argument to the readline function.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=92)** When this function reads a line of input, it will update the buffer with that string.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=97)** So to see that, let's print out the contents of the buffer, then I'll run this program.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=107)** We can see the message to enter a message and then the program waits.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=111)** The readline function will block execution and wait until there's a line of input available for it to read.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=117)** So let's type in a message, good times, I'll press enter.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/standard-input?u=76281980&t=123)** And after that, we can see that the buffer was filled with good times.

#### Parse strings
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The data we get from standard input is a string, but if we need to interpret and use that input information as a different data type, we'll need to parse it.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=10)** For example, let's say we want the user to enter a number.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=14)** If we want to work with that input as a numeric value, we'll need to convert the input string into the corresponding integer value it represents.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=23)** To do that, let's declare a new variable to hold that value.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=28)** And then we'll parse the string buffer to get it.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=33)** The first thing we'll do is apply the trim method to remove any leading or trailing white spaces from the string.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=40)** And this is necessary because the string we get from standard input will include a new line character at the end, so we need to get rid of that before we try to parse it.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=51)** Next, we'll use the parse method on that trimmed string.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=55)** Now, parse is a general method that can parse strings into a lot of different data types, and it's not clear here how we want the parse method to interpret this string.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=65)** So, we need to explicitly tell it that we want it to parse the string into an integer.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=71)** One way to do that is to use what's often called the turbofish operator, consisting of double colons followed by ankle brackets containing the data type.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=83)** Adding this after the parse method tells Rust to parse this string into an i32.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=88)** Another option would be to explicitly specify the data type of the number variable we're assigning the result to.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=96)** The compiler will see that and know that the parse function should produce an i32.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=101)** Now, the thing we actually get back from that parse function will not actually be an i32, but something called a result enum.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=109)** Anytime a program is working with data from an external source, especially coming from a user, it creates the potential for problems.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=116)** We're asking the user to enter a number here, but what if they misbehave and type something else?
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=122)** The parse method would not know how to interpret that, which would cause an error.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=127)** The result enum we get back from the parse method will either contain the i32 value we want, or it could contain an error.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=135)** We'll cover the result enum in detail later in this course when we talk about error handling, but for this example, we'll simply use the unwrap function to extract the i32 value from it.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=149)** Finally, to prove that our number variable actually contains an integer, let's print its value incremented by one.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=157)** This plus one operation would not be possible if the number was still a string.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=163)** Now, if I run this program, I can enter a number, and it successfully parses that input into an integer, which I could use for other purposes.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=177)** Let's try that again.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=180)** But this time, instead of entering the digits for 13, I'll type it out as thirteen.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=188)** The parse method is not able to interpret that, so it returns an error, and the program panics and terminates.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=194)** As you can see, if the parse method doesn't get a properly formatted input, it will cause an error.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/parse-strings?u=76281980&t=200)** And we'll look at better ways to deal with those errors later in this course.

#### Crates
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=0)** - In rust, the term crate is used to describe a collection of Rust source code files.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=6)** And crates come in one of two possible flavors.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=10)** A binary crate contains the code you can compile to produce an executable program.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=15)** Although I haven't been referring to them as crates.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=18)** All of the programs we write in this course are technically binary crates with a main.rs source file.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=25)** The other type of crates are called libraries.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=28)** The library itself isn't really a program but it's a collection of code that's intended to be used in other programs.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=36)** Creating your own library crates goes beyond the scope of this course, but we will look at how to bring existing third-party libraries into your program.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=45)** [Crates.io](https://Crates.io) is the official registry for crates from the Rust community.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/crates?u=76281980&t=50)** If your program needs something that's not included in the standard library, there's a reasonable chance you'll be able to find it on [crates.io](https://crates.io).

#### Solution: Higher or lower
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-higher-or-lower?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

### 9. Input and Output

#### Command-line arguments
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Command-line arguments allow us to pass information to our program when it's invoked as arguments that get passed to the main function.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=9)** Command-line arguments are often used to provide the path to external files of program will need to use, or as configuration flags which tell the program to behave a certain way or operate in a specific mode.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=22)** Your program will need to make sense of any arguments it gets passed to act accordingly.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=27)** To read the values of command-line arguments in Rust, we use the args function which is included in the standard libraries environment module.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=36)** Args will return an iterator with any command line arguments that were passed to the program when it was called.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=43)** The first argument is traditionally the path to the executable itself.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=48)** However, that's not guaranteed in every environment so your program should not rely on that being the case.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=55)** The environment module is not included in the prelude, so we'll need to import it at the top of our program.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=63)** Next, to access the input arguments, we'll call the args function from within main.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=69)** This returns an iterator so let's use the enumerate function to get the indices along with each of those values and then use a for loop to iterate through those arguments and then print each argument with its index.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=95)** Now to run this program, I'll type cargo run just like before but then I can pass additional arguments to the program after that.

>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=123)** Hyphens are often used as a prefix to indicate command line arguments that represent a configuration setting often called flags.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=132)** As far as Rust is concerned, there isn't anything special about the hyphen in front of a flag.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=137)** It treats all of the arguments the same and it's your program's responsibility to parse and interpret those arguments strings to use whatever information they represent.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/command-line-arguments?u=76281980&t=148)** If we want to get a specific argument from the argsIterator, one way to do that is to use the nth method which returns the nth item from an iterator.

#### Reading from files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's a good chance your program will need input from more than just the user in the command line.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=6)** You'll often need to read data from files.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=8)** Fortunately, the fs module from the Rust Standard Library for manipulating the file system provides a handful of functions to make doing that simple.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=19)** The fs module is not part of the default Rust prelude.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=23)** So, to use it in our program, we'll need to explicitly import it.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=28)** That gives us access to some simplified functions for manipulating files.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=32)** For this demonstration, let's look at how we can use it to read a file.

> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=39)** I've included this file named planet.text in the project folder for this example.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=44)** It lists the names of all eight planets in our solar system on separate lines.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=49)** To read that file into our program, we'll declare a new variable to hold its contents.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=54)** And then from the fs module, we'll use the read to string function.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=62)** That function takes a single input argument of the path to the file you want to read.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=67)** Since planet.text is in the project directory, we can simply give it the string planets.text.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=75)** There's a lot that can go wrong when working with files.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=78)** For example, the file we want to read might not exist.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=81)** So, the read to string function returns the result enum type which can be used to implement error handling.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=88)** We'll cover air handling in detail later.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=90)** So, for now, we'll simply use the unwrap function to get its value.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=95)** Finally, to show we've actually read something into this program, let's use a print statement to display its contents.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=105)** Now, I can run this program and we see it print all eight planets from the text file.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=113)** The function reads the entire contents of the file all at once into a single string but that string contains multiple lines that we may want to process individually.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=124)** To do that, we can use the lines method on the string which gives us an iterator that will yield the lines of the string one by one.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=134)** Of course, a common way to use an iterator is with a for loop.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=140)** And we'll use that to print each line individually.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=146)** Now, when I run this program, the for loop prints each line from the file, one at a time.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=153)** Now, while many files contain text data that we can work with as a string, you may need to read other file types that contain text data such as an image or video.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=165)** In that case, you'll need to read and work with the data as a sequence of bytes, rather than a string of characters.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=172)** Fortunately, the fs module provides another simple read function to do just that.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=177)** I'll copy and paste lines four and five from earlier and then modify the read to string function to just be read.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=190)** This read function will return a vector of U8 values, representing the individual bytes in the file.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=197)** We'll cover the vector data type later but for now you can think of it like an array that can be dynamically resized.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=205)** The standard string formatter is not able to display vectors so we'll need to modify the print statement to use the debug formatter.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=213)** Now, when I run this program, if I remember to save before I run it, we can see the final output looks like an array of numbers.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=226)** These are the individual bytes from that file interpreted as unsigned integers.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=233)** The read and read to string functions are great because they're simple to use but they're actually built on top of several other functions.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=241)** As the documentation page for the read function points out, it's provided as a convenience function for using the file open and read to end functions with fewer imports.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=252)** If you want to learn more about those lower level functions, you can read their respective documentation pages as well.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=259)** One other thing I want to mention is that in this example, I used a string literal to store the path to the planets.text file.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/reading-from-files?u=76281980&t=268)** That worked here to keep things simple but if you'll be writing programs to work with more complex paths and need to work on multiple operating systems, I encourage you to explore the standard libraries path module which provides cross platform support for manipulating paths.

#### Writing to files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] After your program has generated some useful data, you may want to to write it to a file to use it again later.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=7)** For example, the program shown here builds a string containing part of president Kennedy's famous speech about going to the moon.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=14)** Those historic words are worth saving, so let's write them to a file.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=18)** The fs module contains a simple function for writing to file, so we'll start by importing that.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=25)** Then, in the main function, we'll call the write function from the fs module.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=31)** This function takes two input arguments: the first argument is a path to the file we want to write.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=38)** Let's save this as speech.text.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=41)** And then the second argument is the contents you want to write to the file.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=45)** For that, we'll pass in the speech string.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=50)** And that's all there is to it.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=52)** When I run this program, it creates a new file named speech.text in the project directory and when I opened that up, we can see that it contains those four lines of the moon speech.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=66)** This write function from the fs module is great because it's simple to use.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=71)** But there are two important things you need to keep in mind when using it: one is that if the file path to write already exists, it will completely overwrite and replace the contents of that existing file.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=84)** The other thing to consider is that the write function writes what you give it as the entire contents of the file all at once.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=91)** So if you need to write data to the file in multiple pieces, it doesn't work well.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=98)** Just as with the fs modules read functions, the documentation page for it's write function points out that it exists for convenience as a simpler way to call the file create method and write all method with fewer imports.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=112)** If you want to learn more about using those lower level methods individually for more control, you can follow the links to their respective documentation pages.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=121)** Now let's say rather than replacing the contents of an existing file, we want to append some new data to the end of it.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=128)** For example, let's say we want to reinstate Pluto as a planet by appending it to the list in planets.text that we used in a previous video.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=138)** To do that, we'll declare a mutable variable to serve as a reference to the file.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=143)** Then from the fs module, we'll access the OpenOptions structure, and then use its new method.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=152)** This creates a blank set of options that we can use to configure how the file will be open.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=158)** Since we want to append data to the end of an existing file, we'll call the append method, and pass it in input argument of true.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=168)** Then call the open method and pass in the path of the file we want to open; planets.text.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=177)** Finally, since this returns the error handling result enum type, we'll need to use unwrap to retrieve its value.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=184)** This gives us a file handle that will append data to the end of the file when we write to it.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=190)** Now, in order to write to that file, we'll need to import the ability to write the files which we can get from the standard libraries io module.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=200)** This imports something called a trait for writing data, and we'll cover what traits are in a lot more detail later in this course.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=207)** The statement on line two will explicitly import just that Write trait, but there are couple of other traits that are commonly needed for io heavy applications, such as the Read trait.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=218)** So the io module provides a prelude to import all of those together, which you'll commonly see used rather than individual imports.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=227)** Now that we've imported the ability to write data, let's use the write function to a pinned Pluto to the end of planets.text.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=236)** We'll type file.write and then Pluto.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=241)** And we'll want Pluto to be on its own line, so I'll add a new line character at the beginning of that string with \n.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=250)** We're passing a string literal here, but this write function doesn't care what the data we want to write represents.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=256)** It just thinks of data as a series of bytes, and it expects the argument it's given to be an array of UA values.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=263)** So we'll need to mark this string literal to be interpreted as a collection of bite values by adding the lowercase b prefix in front of it.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=273)** Now I can run this program.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=278)** And in addition to the previous code overwriting the existing speech.text file, the new code on lines 13 and 14 will append Pluto to the end of planets.text.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/writing-to-files?u=76281980&t=289)** Checking that file, sure enough, we see that Pluto has been reinstated as one of the nine planets.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### Solution: Check the roster

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-check-the-roster?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] My solution to the check the roster challenge starts by importing two modules from the standard library at the top.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-check-the-roster?u=76281980&t=12)** The environment module to access the command line arguments and the file system or FS module to read the input text file.

### 10. Structs

#### Defining structs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=0)** - Tuples are useful when we need to concisely package a couple of related values together.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=6)** But, the fact that their values are stored and accessed based on their relative order, means if there are a lot of elements and their order isn't obvious.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=15)** It can be tedious keeping track of which item is first, second, third, and so on.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=20)** When you need to access them.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=23)** That's where a struct can be useful.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=25)** A struct or structure is like a tuple, in that it allows you to package together related values of different data types.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=33)** But unlike a tuple, you assign a name to each value to indicate what it means and you use that name to access it.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=40)** That means you don't have to worry about the order in which those elements are stored.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=46)** To define a new custom struct data type we'll use the keyword struct followed by the name of the struct.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=53)** Let's define a struct to represent a space shuttle.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=57)** It's common to capitalize the name for a custom struct data type as shown here.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=62)** Next, inside the curly braces we'll define the elements of our shuttle struct called fields by giving each field a name and its associated data type.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=72)** Let's give it a field for the name of the shuttle, which will be stored as a string.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=78)** We'll give it a field for the crew size, which would make sense to represent as a U8.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=85)** And finally, we'll give it some amount of propellant.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=89)** Which will store as a 64 bit float.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=92)** That definition tells Rust what goes into a shuttle struct so we can create and use shuttles in our program.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=99)** But that definition itself doesn't have any real data.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=103)** For that we'll need to create a new instance of a shuttle struct by giving it actual concrete values for each of the fields.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=111)** Within the main function, let's declare a new variable named vehicle.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=117)** Then we'll instantiate a shuttle and give it values for each field.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=123)** Let's make the name a string that says Endeavor.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=130)** It's crew size will be seven.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=135)** And for the propellant, we'll load it up with 835,958 gallons.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=146)** Accessing field data from the struct is similar to accessing tuples, but instead of using a number we use the field name.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=154)** So, if we want to print the value of the name field, we would type vehicle.name.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=161)** I'll run this program.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=166)** And we see the output that this vehicle is named Endeavor.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=171)** Now, if we want to modify the values in a struct we'll of course need to declare it as mutable.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=176)** That mutability does not apply to the struct definition on line one that just defines a new data type.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=184)** We'll need to declare our specific instance of the shuttle as mutable by inserting the mute keyword on line eight when we instantiate a vehicle.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=193)** Now that it's mutable, we can assign a new value to the name field.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=197)** Let's rename the shuttle from Endeavor to Atlantis.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=203)** And instead of just printing the shuttle's name let's print the entire vehicle struct.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=211)** Since this is a custom data type, the default formatter won't know how to display it.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=216)** So we'll need to use debug formatting.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=221)** Let's save and run that.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=225)** And it gives us a compiler error.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=229)** It says that the shuttle cannot be formatted using debug along with a help note that suggests adding hashtag derived debug, or manually implementing the debug formatting.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=241)** This gets into a concept called traits, which we'll cover later in this course.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=245)** So for now, let's just follow the compiler's advice and add that line before the shuttle definition.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=256)** I'll save and run the program again.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=259)** And now we can see the value for each of the shuttle fields displayed in the output.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=265)** Now that we've seen how to use a struct.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=267)** Let's talk about where that data is stored in memory.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=271)** Unless you explicitly put it on the heap, struct data will be stored on the stack.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=276)** And that makes sense if our struct contains stack only data types like numbers.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=280)** The compiler knows how much space is needed for each number and therefore how much space the entire struct will need on the stack.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=288)** If the struct also contains heat based data types like a string, then the string pointer and associated information will live on the stack with the rest of the struct while the contents of the string lives on the heap.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=302)** In fact, under the hood the string data type itself is actually a type of struct.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=308)** In the scenario shown here, the struct owns the string which in turn owns the data on the heap.
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=314)** So when the struct goes out of scope the strings heap data will also be dropped.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=319)** Things get tricky when you want to store a reference data type within a struct such as a string slice.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=326)** In this case, Rust requires us to annotate the lifetime of the struct to ensure the data it references will be valid for as long as the struct is alive.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/defining-structs?u=76281980&t=335)** And we'll cover how to do that later in this course.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** struct (26), let (9), type. (2), finally, (1), function (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Cross-References:** later in (2)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1), such as (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Warnings:** note that (1)
> **Speakers:** - tuples (1)

#### Struct update syntax
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Teacher] We only define a struct once, which is done here on line two at the top of the program.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=6)** Once we've done that we can use that definition to instantiate as many separate instances of the struct as we need.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=13)** Let's expand our fleet of spacecraft by creating a second shuttle named vehicle two, and we'll make its name Discovery.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=27)** Now, instead of explicitly defining the rest of the fields let's say we want our second vehicle to be initialized using the same values as the first vehicle.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=38)** For that we can use struct update syntax, which allows us to copy values from an existing instance.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=44)** It's written as .. and then the name of the existing instance to copy values from.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=50)** This tells Rust that any fields that are not explicitly set should have the same value as the first vehicle.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=57)** To see that let's copy and paste the print statement to look at the first vehicle, and modify it to print the second vehicle, and we'll also delete this extra print statement on line 20.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=78)** Now I'll save and run that program, and we can see two vehicles with different names but the other fields have the same values.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=90)** Keep in mind these two structs are separate instances.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=94)** If we make changes to the first vehicle after the second vehicle has been instantiated using those values those changes will not be reflected in the second vehicle.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=105)** For example, instead of changing the vehicle's name on line 20 let's change its crew size from seven to six.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=113)** When I run the program now we see that the first vehicle ends with a crew size of six, but the second vehicle still has a crew size of seven, because at the time it was initialized that was the first vehicles crew size.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=129)** Now, instead of giving our second vehicle a unique name let's see what happens if we try to use all the fields from the first existing vehicle.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=139)** I'll save and run that, and it gives us a compiler error about moving values.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=147)** Since name is stored as a string and that string can only have one owner, when we instantiate vehicle two to use values from the first shuttle it takes ownership of that string.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=158)** Therefore, when we try to print the first vehicle it no longer owns the string because it was moved.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=164)** That problem doesn't affect the other two struct values because they're both numbers which are stored on the stack, and therefore copied by default when instantiating the second vehicle.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=174)** One potential solution could be to use a slice here instead of a full string, but that introduces another complication with lifetimes, which we'll cover later.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=185)** The benefit of storing that data as a string is that it will stay valid as long as the struct that owns it remains in scope.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=192)** So, another solution to this situation is to clone the first vehicle to populate the value of the second vehicle.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=201)** The shuttle data type doesn't natively have the ability or trait to clone data so we'll need to derive that at the struct definition as well.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-update-syntax?u=76281980&t=212)** Now when I run this program the second shuttle will get its own separate string, stored on the heap, to hold its name, and everything works.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Warnings:** keep in mind (1)

#### Struct methods
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Teacher] In addition to just storing data Rust also allows us to define subroutines called methods for our structs which are similar to functions that can manipulate and use the data stored within a struct.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=12)** Like functions, methods contain code that runs when you call them, they can have input parameters, as well as an output return value, and methods and functions are even both declared in Rust using the same fn keyword.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=26)** The key difference between them is that methods are defined within the context of a struct and their first input parameter is always a reference to the struct itself which gives us access to the data in the specific struct instance the method is being called on.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=41)** To define methods within the context of the shuttle struct we'll put them within an implementation block, which is defined using the keyword impl followed by the name of the struct type.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=55)** Now, within the implementation block let's define a method named get_name to get a shuttles name and return it as a string slice.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=64)** The first parameter will be a reference named self and then the return type will be a slice.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=72)** Within the method we can access the name field from the shuttle instance this method was called on with self.name and since we want to return a slice instead of the entire string we'll add a borrow operator to the front of that.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=86)** Now, to use our new method, down in the main function we'll declare a new variable, named vehicle_name, to hold that slice,
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=100)** and then assign it the return value from vehicle.get_name.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=106)** The syntax calls the get_name method on the shuttle instance named vehicle.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=111)** Let's print the result of that, save, and run the program, and we can see the vehicle is named Endeavor.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=127)** If we called get_name on another shuttle instance with a different name we would get a different result.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=133)** Now, in addition to retrieving data from a struct we can also define methods to modify the values of a struct.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=143)** Let's define another method to add fuel to the shuttle.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=148)** Since it will be modifying the value of the shuttle's propellant field we'll need to declare the self parameter as a mutable reference.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=156)** We'll also pass in a second parameter for the number of gallons to add as a float.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=162)** Then, within the function, we'll use the addition with assignment operator to add that many gallons to the propellant field.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=170)** Down in the main method let's print this starting propellant value,
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=183)** then call the add fuel method to load it with 1,000 more gallons, and then copy and paste that print statement to view the propellant levels again afterwards.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=194)** I'll run the program, and we can see that the propellant level is initially zero, and then after adding fuel it's up to 1,000 gallons.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=204)** Now, both of these examples are super simple and you might be wondering why we would ever create a method to add fuel rather than simply modifying the structs propellant field directly.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=215)** By encapsulating that modification within a method we can add additional logic to make sure the propellant level never unintentionally gets set to an invalid value.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=225)** We can make sure that the shuttle never gets filled with more propellant than it can actually hold.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=230)** Of course, I've kept the example code here simple on purpose.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-methods?u=76281980&t=233)** The code in methods can be as long and complex as any other function.
