#### Associated functions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to using implementation blocks to define methods, we can also use them to define functions that are associated with a struct data type.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=10)** They look similar to methods, but the key difference between a method and an associated function is that the function does not have a self input parameter.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=19)** So you cannot use an associated function to reference the data from within a specific instance of a struct.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=26)** Instead, associated functions can provide sub-routines that are related to the struct data type in general.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=33)** You'll commonly see associated functions used as a constructor to build a new instance of a struct, perhaps with some default values.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=41)** So let's define an associated function for our shuttle struct to build a new shuttle.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=48)** We'll name the new function new and give it one input parameter for the name, which we'll pass in as a string slice.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=57)** And the return value from this function will be a new instance of a shuttle.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=64)** Within the function, we'll instantiate a new shuttle, then use the name input parameter to initialize a new string for its name, assign the crew size to a default value of seven and assign the propellant a default value of zero.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=85)** Now down in the main function, instead of instantiating all those fields to create a new shuttle, I can simply call our new associated function.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=95)** Since this function is associated with the shuttle data type as a whole and not a specific instance of a shuttle, we'll call it by referencing the shuttle type.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=104)** Then using the double colon path operator, we'll access the new function and we'll pass it in input string literal that says endeavor.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=114)** I'll run this code.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=118)** And it works similar to the previous video, but now we've got a handy constructor function to use to create new shuttles.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=124)** That makes instantiating a second shuttle as easy as copying line 26, changing the variable name to vehicle2 and then changing the input string to whatever we want this new shuttle to be named.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=139)** Let's call it Discovery.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/associated-functions?u=76281980&t=142)** Both of these shuttles will be initialized with the same default crew size and propellant values, but they'll each have their own unique name.

#### Tuple structs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've learned about tuples and we've learned about structs.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=3)** Now, let's talk about tuple structs, which exist somewhere between those two data types.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=10)** Tuple structs are defined to look similar to tuples in that they don't have names associated with their fields, just the data type of the fields.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=19)** But, they have the advantage of being distinguishable as a unique data type, like structs.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=24)** This can be convenient when you want to give a whole tuple a name to differentiate it from other tuples, but naming every field, like in a regular struct, would be overkill and verbose.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=35)** An example of this would be a tuple that represents a color.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=39)** Computers commonly represent colors as a sequence of three values representing the amount of red, green and blue that make up the overall color.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=48)** It's standard for those values to be listed in that order, so it would be redundant to name each field.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=54)** We can define a tuple struct to represent a color by starting with the struct keyword, the type name, which will be color, and then a tuple with the data type for each value.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=65)** We'll use three U8s here.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=69)** And I'll add a comment at the end of this line to make it clear for anyone reading this code later that this color represents a red, green, blue value.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=78)** That defines a new tuple struct data type for colors, so down in the main function, let's declare a new variable named red, and assign it the color tuple 255, zero, zero, which represents a color that's all red with no green or blue components.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=98)** Now, since these fields are not named like in a regular struct, we access them in the same way as any other tuple, using their relative index order.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=108)** Let's print the value of the first element in our red tuple.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=114)** And we'll do that with red.zero.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=117)** If I run this program, we see that the first red value is 255.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=124)** Now let's define another tuple struct.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=128)** This one will represent a point in three-dimensional space.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=133)** So it has three values representing X, Y, and Z coordinates.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=137)** We'll make those all U8s as well.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=141)** At the surface, color and point look the same because they both contain three U8 values.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=147)** But, those values mean very different things.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=151)** By defining them as structs, we're defining unique data types that we can use as variables, input parameters, return values, and so on.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=159)** For example, let's define a function to get the Y value from a point.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=164)** We'll name it get Y, and we'll take a point as input, return a U8 value, and we'll get that by accessing the corresponding Y value, which is at index one.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=178)** Now, down in our main function, let's try calling this on our red tuple struct, and then printing the results.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=193)** When I go to run this program, it fails to compile because the get Y function expects a point tuple struct, but we passed it a color.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=203)** Again, they're different data types.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=206)** So let's initialize a new point named coord ahead of that function call.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=214)** Change the argument for get Y from red to coord.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=221)** And then run the program again.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/tuple-structs?u=76281980&t=225)** Now it works because Russ can distinguish between the color and point tuple struct types.

#### Challenge: Represent shapes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Narrator] It's time for a challenge to practice using structs.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=8)** Your goal for this challenge is to define a struct named Rectangle to represent, well, a rectangle.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=15)** It should store two fields for the width and height of the rectangle.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=19)** Both stored as 64 bit floating point values.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=23)** In addition to those fields, your struct also needs to implement a couple of methods and a function.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=28)** The first method named, get area, should return a 64 bit float representing the rectangles area.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=35)** Which can be calculated by multiplying its width by its height.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=39)** The other method named, Scale, should take a 64 bit float value as input.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=45)** And then scale both dimensions of the rectangle by that value individually multiplying the width and height by the scaler.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=53)** Finally, your rectangle struct should have an associated function named, New, to create a new rectangle.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=59)** It should accept two input arguments for width and height and then return a new rectangle with those dimensions.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=67)** I've included this simple test routine in the exercise files, which creates a new rectangle.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=72)** Checks it's area, scales it by 0.5, and then checks the area again after being scaled.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=78)** You can use this as a reference to see how each of the methods and functions will be called.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=82)** And when you're done creating your solution you can run the program to see if it passed the test.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/challenge-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=87)** Good luck.

#### Solution: Represent shapes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] My solution for the challenge to represent a shape starts by defining a new struct named Rectangle with two fields, width and height, both stored as 64-bit floating point values.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=16)** It's pretty straightforward.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=18)** The more interesting part of this solution is in the implementation block starting on line six which contains my methods and associated function.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=28)** Since get_area on line seven is a method, it takes the reference to the struct itself.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=34)** It then uses that self-reference to access the values from the width and height fields, and calculate the area of the rectangle, which it then returns as an f64.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=45)** The next method on line 11 named scale will need to modify the width and height values, and therefore, takes a mutable reference to the struct as its first input argument, along with the scalar value to multiply them by.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=59)** Lines 12 and 13 then use the arithmetic multiplication and assignment operator to individually multiply the width and height variables by the scalar, and assign them the respective results.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=73)** Below that, on line 16 is the associated function named new.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=78)** You can tell it's an associated function and not a method because it does not have a parameter named self, just two inputs for the width and height.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=88)** It uses those two inputs to build a new Rectangle struct on line 17 which it then returns.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=93)** And that's all there is to my solution.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=96)** The test code in the main function will create a new Rectangle with dimensions 1.2 and 3.4, which should have an area of 4.08.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=105)** It then uses the scale function to shorten both dimensions in half, which reduces the area to a quarter of the original size which is 1.02.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=115)** To prove my solution works, I'll run the program and we can see that all the tests pass.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=122)** If you want some additional practice working with structs, you can take this challenge further by defining structs to represent other common shapes such as a circle or a triangle.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-represent-shapes?u=76281980&t=132)** Think of the fields that would be needed to represent each of those shapes and consider additional methods and associated functions that might be useful.

### 11. Generic Types

#### Generic struct definitions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In my solution to the previous challenge, I defined a struct to represent a rectangle which stores its width and height as 64 bit float values.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=9)** The code shown here is a simplified version of that solution.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=14)** Now, what if instead of using floating point values, we want to represent this rectangle using a different data type?
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=21)** Let's try modifying the instantiation values on lines nine and 10 from floats to integers.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=30)** When I try to run the program, we get a compiler error and this makes sense.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=36)** Rust is a strictly typed language and we defined the rectangle struct using 64 bit floats but we tried to instantiate it with 32 bit integers.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=46)** Those are different types so the compiler's going to complain.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=50)** If from now on, we only plan to define rectangles using integer values, then the straightforward solution would be to change the struct definition.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=59)** But what if we wanted the flexibility to define structs using either integers or floating point values?
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=68)** One solution in that case would be to define two different versions of a rectangle struct with different names, one that uses F64s and another for I32s.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=79)** That will work but it makes our job a bit more tedious because we have to make sure we're using the right type of rectangle struct.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=87)** Also, it doesn't scale well.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=90)** What if we want to be able to instantiate rectangles using other data types, like U8s, U16s, U32s and F32s?
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=99)** That becomes a lot of unique struct types to keep track of and maintain.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=104)** The better solution here is to use generic types which serve as abstract stand-ins for concrete data types or other properties.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=112)** They can be used with structs, functions, methods, and other elements in Rust to help eliminate duplicate code.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=119)** Using generics, we can define a single rectangle struct which is flexible to store its values as any data type.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=127)** The syntax for generics uses ankle brackets to enclose a variable name to define the generic type.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=133)** You can name that variable anything you want, but it's conventional to use single capital letters starting with T.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=141)** To use generic types when defining a struct, add angle brackets with the generic variable name as a suffix to the name of the struct.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=150)** Then, within the struct definition, you can use that variable name anywhere you would specify a data type.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=156)** So let's change width and height to both be stored as the generic type T.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=164)** When I run this program now, it works.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=168)** And we get the output message that the rectangle has a width of one and a height of three.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=174)** The compiler recognizes that we're using integers to initialize the struct on lines nine and 10, so the generic T is replaced with I32, and it creates the struct accordingly.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=186)** If we explicitly make those values a different type of integer, for example, U8s, and run it again, it still works, and now the values are being stored as U8s.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=202)** Now, let's see what happens if we try to initialize the rectangle with two different data types by changing the height from a U8 to a U16.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=213)** When I go to run this program, it produces a compiler error that says it expected a U8 but found a U16 instead.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=223)** The compiler sees the U8 value on line nine first so it substitutes the generic type T and the struct definition with U8.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=231)** Therefore, it expects the height variable to match and be a U8 as well.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=236)** If we want to use multiple generic types, we'll need to add another variable to the struct definition.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=242)** Since we used T for the first type, let's make the second type named U, which is just the next letter alphabetically.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=252)** And then we'll change the type of height from T to U.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=258)** When I run the program now, everything works.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=264)** If you need even more distinct generic data types, you can continue adding more variables separated by commas when defining the struct name and then use them as the types for struct fields.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=276)** Now, if you're wondering if using generics will impact the performance of your code at runtime, there's good news.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=282)** Generics are a zero-cost abstraction.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=286)** One of the core principles of Rust is to provide abstractions that make writing and maintaining code easier but do not create additional overhead that would slow down the program execution.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=297)** In the case of generics, the compiler uses a process called monomorphization, in which it replaces the generic placeholders with the concrete data types that are needed.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=307)** So if your code defines a rectangle struct using generics and then creates an instance of that struct with a U8 and a U16, the compiler will include and use a struct defined with those data types.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=320)** If elsewhere in your code, you also instantiate a rectangle using two floating point values, the compiler will use a second copy of that struct definition with F64s.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-struct-definitions?u=76281980&t=330)** So in the end, everything you define using generic types, it's translated into concrete types at compile time.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** struct (20), let (4), type. (3), abstract (1), type, (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (4)
> **Env Vars:** u16 (3), i32 (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2)
> **UI Navigation:** go to (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Generic method definitions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In addition to using generics to represent the data types of fields within a struct, we can also use generics when implementing methods for a struct.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=9)** When defining the implementation block for our rectangle struct, which has two generic variables T and U, we'll need to include those two generic variables after the implement keyword.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=21)** And we'll also need to include them after the name rectangle as well.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=26)** Including T and U after the implement keyword hell's the rust compiler that will be implementing methods and functions for a rectangle with two generic types.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=36)** And we'll see why that matters shortly.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=39)** Next, let's define a method to get the rectangles width.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=44)** The return type for that method will be whatever data type T is and we will return a reference to the width value.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=54)** We have to return a reference here rather than the width variable itself.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=58)** Because at this point, we don't know what data type T will be replaced with.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=63)** If T ends up being a numeric data type that lives on the stack, then returning the width field directly, would simply return a copy of its value.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=71)** But, if the rectangle gets initialized with T being a heat based data type, then returning the variable could transfer ownership of it.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=80)** We don't know what we're dealing with yet, so we use a reference which can be safely returned in either situation.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=87)** Down in the main function, let's add a print statement to get and display the rectangles width.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=100)** I'll run this code, and we can see that the width is one.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=108)** In addition to implementing methods for this generic rectangle, we can define methods that only apply to a specific type of rectangle.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=117)** To do that, we'll create another implementation block.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=123)** But this time, we do not put the generic variables after the implement keyword.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=128)** Then, after the name rectangle, we'll specify the two concrete types we want to use.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=132)** Let's make those both u8s.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=137)** By not including generic variables after the implement keyword, we're telling the compiler that this implementation block is for a specific concrete type of rectangle.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=147)** In this case, methods defined in this block will only be available for rectangles with u8, for both the width and the height.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=155)** Within this block, let's define a method to get the rectangles perimeter which will return as a u8, and we can calculate its perimeter as two times the width plus two times the height.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=174)** Finally, down in the main function, we'll add another print statement to get Inprint perimeter.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=189)** Now, let's try building this program which currently instantiates a rectangle with a u8 and a u16.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=199)** As you might expect, we get an error.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=204)** It says that the get perimeter method was not found for a rectangle with a u8 and a u16.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-method-definitions?u=76281980&t=211)** Now, if we change the rectangle on line 20 to be instantiated with both u8s and then run the program again, now the perimeter method is available to the rectangle and we see that the perimeter is eight.

#### Generic function definitions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Presenter] We've seen generic data types used to define methods.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=4)** So it shouldn't be a big surprise to learn that generics can also be used when defining functions.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=10)** To demonstrate that let's implement a function that returns the biggest of two given input values.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=18)** This function will have one generic type named T and then two input parameters named a and b which are both of that same type T.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=28)** And then the return type will also be T.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=32)** This syntax is similar to what we saw earlier when implementing methods first track using generics.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=38)** Within this function, we'll use an if else expression to return a or b depending on which one is greater.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=45)** If a is greater than b return a else return b.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=54)** Then down in the main function, we'll create a print statement.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=60)** And we'll call our get biggest function to compare two values.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=65)** For simplicity, let's compare one and two.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=71)** Now, if I try to run this program, we get a compiler error.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=77)** It says that the binary operation greater than cannot be applied to type T.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=83)** Below that, the help message suggests restricting the tight parameter T by adding code that references the standard library, compare module partialOrd.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=94)** PartialOrd describes a data types ability to compare two values and determine if one is greater than the other which enables them to be sorted into order.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=103)** The problem here is that we're using a generic data type which could be anything.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=108)** And the type it ends up being may not have the ability to be compared.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=114)** For example, consider the rectangle struct we used in previous videos.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=119)** If we wanted to compare these two rectangles to determine if the left rectangle is greater than the right one, what do we compare?
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=127)** Which one has the largest area, biggest perimeter, which one is wider, which one has the greatest height?
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=134)** To use the greater than operator to compare two rectangles, our rectangles struct would need to define how it should be compared with other rectangles.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=142)** That gets into the topic of Traits and Rust which we'll cover in the next chapter.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=148)** For now to resolve the problem in our code, let's follow the advice of the compilers help message.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=153)** After defining the generic type T we'll add a colon and then PartialOrd.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=160)** This sets bounds or limits on the possible data types T can be.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=165)** Now, this function will only accept data types such as numeric values that can be compared because they implement the PartialOrd trait.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=173)** As a note, this PartialOrd trait is included in the rust prelude which is why we did need to describe it's full path as shown in the help message.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=183)** Now I'll run the program again and this time it successfully compiles and we get an output message that two is the biggest number.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/generic-function-definitions?u=76281980&t=193)** We can change those two integer arguments to be floating point values, run it again and it still works, thanks to generics.

#### Box data type
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Rust Standard Library includes several data types that use generics as part of their type definition.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=7)** And the first example of this we'll look at is the Box Data Type.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=11)** Box allows you to store data of some generic type T on the heap.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=15)** Even if T is a data type that would normally be stored on the stack.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=20)** The box consists of a pointer on the stack which points to a chunk of memory on the heap.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=25)** That's been allocated large enough to hold whatever generic type T is.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=30)** Boxes are considered a Smart Pointer, because they provide some additional functionality beyond what a regular reference can do.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=37)** Beyond just simply pointing to a memory location.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=41)** For example, unlike a borrowed reference, a box has ownership of the data it points to.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=47)** And when the box goes out of scope it will automatically drop and deallocate the memory it was using on the heap.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=54)** Rust has several other types of smart pointers with even more features.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=58)** But box is the most straightforward.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=61)** To demonstrate, let's use a box to store an instance of this struct representing a space shuttle.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=67)** As a struct, the shuttle would primarily live on the stack with the exception of the name string which gets stored on the heap.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=74)** The example shown here, instantiates a new shuttle variable named vehicle on lines 10 through 14.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=81)** Then the print statement online 15 uses the size of val function from the standard libraries memory module, which returns the size of the pointed two value in terms of bytes.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=92)** So we can see how much space the vehicle struct is occupying on the stack.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=98)** When I run this program, the output shows that the three fields that make up a shuttle struct.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=106)** The name, crew size, and propellant, occupy a total of 40 bytes on the stack.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=112)** Now, let's move that struct to store it on the heap.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=115)** Instead, by putting it in a box.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=119)** We'll declare a new variable named boxed_vehicle.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=123)** And its data type will be a box followed by the shuttle type name in angle brackets.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=130)** This tells Rust that the thing this box is pointing to is a shuttle.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=134)** That way the compiler knows how to interpret the data on the other end of the pointer and how much space it will occupy on the heap.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=142)** Next we'll use the boxes new function to create a new box and then we'll pass it the existing vehicle instance of the shuttle as input.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=153)** When we use the new box function, our program will allocate the necessary amount of memory on the heap to hold a shuttle, and then place the existing instance into it.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=164)** Note that this is not a copy operation.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=167)** So the vehicle variable loses ownership of the struct and will no longer be valid.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=172)** Instead, the box to vehicle variable becomes the new owner of the struct through the box pointer, which lives on the stack.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=180)** To view the effects of that, I'll copy and paste the statement after boxing the vehicle.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=187)** Then modify it to show us the size of the box to vehicle on the stack.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=195)** Then I'll copy and paste that message again, and I'll modify it to show us the size of the data structure stored on the heap.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=208)** Now the size of val function we're using here gives us the size and bytes of whatever the given reference points to.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=216)** Passing in a reference to the boxed vehicle variable will give us the size of the boxes pointer, which lives on the stack.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=223)** But what if we want to follow that pointer to the data on the heap that it references and get the size of that.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=230)** For that, we'll need to use the dereference operator, which is represented using the asterisk or star symbol.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=237)** When applied to a pointer, the dereference operator gives us the location or thing that the pointer points to.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=243)** If you have a background programming in C or C++, This should be familiar territory.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=250)** The dereference the box vehicle variable on line 19.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=253)** We'll insert the star operator immediately in front of the variable name.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=259)** Now the ampersand operator will give us a reference to the data on the heap, rather than the pointer on the stack.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=266)** When I run this program.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=269)** We now see that the box vehicle only takes eight bytes on the stack for the pointer, and then 40 bytes on the heap for the struct data.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=278)** If we want to move that box data back from the heap to the stack, we can do so by dereferencing it.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=285)** Let's declare another variable named unboxed_vehicle.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=289)** Its data type will be a regular shuttle struct.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=292)** And then we'll use the dereference operator to assign the struct that the boxed vehicle points to.
>
> **[4:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=298)** This will place the struct back on the stack and pass ownership to the unboxed vehicle variable.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=305)** Let's copy and paste the print statement for the box size on the stack and then modify it to show the unboxed size.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=318)** I'll run that program.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=321)** And now we can see that in the end, there were 40 bytes of unboxed vehicle living on this stack.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=327)** Now that we've seen how to use the box data type, you might be wondering, what would you actually use it for.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=334)** One use case is to store a data type who size cannot be known at compile time.
>
> **[5:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=339)** However, you need to use the value in a context that requires a known size.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=345)** An example of this could be a recursive data type.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=348)** Such as a struct, which includes another struct of the same type as one of its fields.
>
> **[5:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=353)** You may not know how many recursive layers deep the nested struct will go compile time.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=358)** And therefore do not know the total size of the overall structure.
>
> **[6:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=363)** Another reason you might use a box is to transfer ownership of data that would normally live on the stack rather than copying it.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/box-data-type?u=76281980&t=370)** This can be useful in working with large data structures because copying large amounts of data around on the stack can take a long time and you can potentially improve performance by moving it to the heap where ownership can be more easily transferred.

>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=13)** Those requirements said that the function should be named sum boxes, that it would be using a single generic type T, so I'll need to define that after the function name in ankle brackets.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=25)** And then that the function should accept two input parameters.
>

>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=36)** And the second parameter will be named B.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=38)** And it's also a box T.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=42)** Finally, the return value from this function should also be a box T.

> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=48)** Now, I don't want to add the two input boxes themselves together, I want to add the values stored within those boxes.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=56)** To access those values, I'll need to use the dereference operator on A and B to follow both box pointers to the values they're storing underneath.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=65)** This expression will add those two stored values together which gives me a numeric result.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=70)** But this function is supposed to return another box.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=74)** So I'll use the result from that expression as the input argument to create a new box which will be what gets returned.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=82)** That seems like it should do it.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=84)** So I'll try running the program.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=87)** And, oh oh, I get a compiler error.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=90)** This error is similar to another error we saw in a previous video working with generics.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=96)** Since we'll be covering traits in the next chapter, I won't go into detail about this error here.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=101)** For now, I'll simply follow the compiler's help message which suggests adding these additional details to the generic type definition.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=110)** I'll copy that from the terminal, and then paste that part into the function definition, save and try running the program again.

> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-sum-boxes?u=76281980&t=125)** And now it works and passes all the tests.

### 12. Traits

#### Implement traits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Rust provides an abstract way to define the capabilities or functionality of specific data types using traits, which are collections of methods representing a set of behaviors necessary to accomplish some task.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=15)** When a data type implements a trait, that means it implements those specific methods so they'll be available to use with it.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=23)** We've already encountered traits in regards to generics which impose bounds to specify that a generic type can be any data type as long as it implements specific traits, and therefore has the necessary methods to use it.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=37)** If you have experience in other programming languages like C++ or Java, you'll recognize traits as being very similar to a feature in those languages called interfaces, although there are some differences.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=51)** In addition to a handful of common traits that come standard with Rust, you can also define your own custom traits to specify certain capabilities.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=60)** To demonstrate that, let's implement a new trait for the two struct types shown here on lines one and six for a satellite or a space station.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=69)** Down in the main function, this program instantiates a Satellite to represent the Hubble Space Telescope and a SpaceStation to represent the ISS.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=79)** Then, it attempts to print those structs.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=81)** However, as we've seen in earlier videos, the print macro does not have the ability to format and display a struct.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=89)** So let's implement a trait to get a nicely formatted description of the struct.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=94)** Our two structs hold different data and represent different things so their descriptions should be unique to reflect that.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=102)** To define a new trait, we'll use the keyword trait followed by the name of the trait.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=107)** Let's call this trait Description.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=110)** Within that code block, we'll list the method signatures for all the methods that a type implementing the description trait will need to have.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=118)** You can define multiple methods for a trait, but for this example, we'll keep it simple with a single method named describe.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=126)** And that describe method will return a string describing the data.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=130)** You can think of this trait as a contract that says any data type that implements the description trait will have a method named describe that returns a string.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=140)** Now that we've defined the trait, we can implement it for our Satellite data type using an implementation block.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=148)** We'll say implement Description for Satellite.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=155)** Next, within that implementation block, we'll define the Satellite's describe method, and then within that method, we'll use the format macro to build a string that says the satellite's name and its velocity.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=176)** Now, to implement the description trait for the SpaceStation struct, I'll copy and paste that block of code, change Satellite to SpaceStation, and then change the formatted string to reflect a SpaceStation.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=192)** We'll say that this station is flying however many miles high with some number of crew members on board.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=206)** With the description traits implemented, now we have the describe method available to use with both our struct types.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=214)** So I'll modify the code on lines 38 and 39 to call describe and pass the returned string to the print macro.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=224)** Now I'll run this program and we see that the Hubble Telescope is flying at 4.72 miles per second and the International Space Station is flying 754 miles high with six crew members aboard.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=240)** This example demonstrated how to define and implement traits, but you might be wondering why we couldn't have just implemented describe methods for our two structs.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=250)** Defining the description trait seemed like an extra unnecessary step here.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=255)** That's true in this simple scenario.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/implement-traits?u=76281980&t=257)** We could've accomplished the same thing without creating a new trait, but as we'll explore later, the value of using traits to abstractly define the behaviors of data types is that they allow us to do things like set bounds on the data types that can be used when working with generics.

#### Default trait implementation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In certain situations, it may be useful to have a default implementation for one or more of the methods in a trait.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=8)** That could be especially useful if you have a trait with a large number of methods and you don't want to have to implement every single one of them for every single data type with that trait.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=18)** You can provide a default implementation as part of the trait definition.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=23)** We'll do that here for our describe method on line 13 by replacing the semicolon at the end of the method signature with curly braces.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=31)** Then within that code block, we'll create a new string, and we'll give it a generic description that this is an object flying through space.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=42)** Now, both of our structs are currently implementing their own unique versions of the describe method, which will override or replace the default version we just defined.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=53)** So they're individually implemented versions of describe or what will actually get used.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=58)** But now that we have a default describe method, we don't have to individually implement it for each struct.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=65)** So let's delete the describe method for the satellite.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=70)** That leaves us with an empty implementation block.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=73)** We're still saying that satellite implements the description trait, but now it will use the default describe method.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/default-trait-implementation?u=76281980&t=80)** When I run this program, we now see that Hubble uses the default description that it's an object flying through space, whereas the ISS still displays its customized description.

#### Derive traits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you define a new custom data type with a struct, by default, the new struck does not implement any traits.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=8)** It's your job as the programmer to explicitly give it the traits it needs.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=13)** Consider the example shown here which instantiates two satellites to represent the Hubble space telescope and a GPS satellite and then compares them on line 15 to determine if Hubble and GPS are equal.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=26)** If I try to run this program, we get a compiler error.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=32)** It says that the equality operator cannot be applied to the satellite type.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=37)** And it has a note suggesting that an implementation of the partial equality trait might be missing.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=43)** We've encountered similar errors earlier in this course.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=48)** Our custom satellite struct doesn't automatically implement the partial equality trait which provides the ability to compare satellites to each other to see if they're equal.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=58)** We could implement this trait for our satellite struct.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=61)** Like we would any other trait by defining the required methods for it.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=66)** Or, we can take another easier route and simply derive that functionality.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=72)** The rust compiler is able to provide a basic implementation for a handful of common traits via the derived attribute.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=80)** When you derive traits, the compiler will generate default code for the required methods.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=85)** If you need something more complex or specialized you'll need to implement the methods yourself.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=91)** The list of derivable traits includes the comparison traits for equality and order clone, copy hash default to create an empty instance of a data type and debug which we've used in several previous examples to provide a formatted debug string to view the contents of a struct.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=112)** To derive traits for our satellite struct on the line immediately before the struct definition, we'll type a pound symbol square brackets derive parentheses and then the name of the trait, PartialEquality.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=130)** The default implementation of this trait when derived for a struct, will compare two instances of the struct and determine them to be equal only if all of the fields are equal.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=142)** When I run this program, the print macro displays the result of comparing Hubble and GPS for equality, which is false.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=151)** And that makes sense because they're holding different data.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=155)** Now, let's try a different way to compare two satellites.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=159)** We'll copy the print macro on line 16, and then replace the equality comparison with the greater than operator.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=169)** The ability to compare two values in this way comes from a different trait.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=174)** So we'll need to derive that as well.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=176)** You can derive multiple traits by listing them out, separated by commas.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=182)** The second trait we'll need for the greater than comparison is PartialOrd.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=188)** When I run the program now, we see that it assesses Hubble to be greater than GPS.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=195)** But that raises the question, what's it comparing?
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=198)** Their names?
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=199)** Velocities both?
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=202)** When derived on structs, PartialOrd will compare the values in each field in order according to the struct definition, until it determines one to be greater than the other.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=212)** So, this program starts by comparing the names of the two satellites and it determines that the characters in the Hubble string have a greater value than GPS and therefore determines that Hubble is greater.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=224)** It never even considered their velocities.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=227)** Although that might make more sense to compare.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/derive-traits?u=76281980&t=230)** If you want to compare satellites in some way, other than this derive fashion, keep in mind that you can always implement your own version of the traits.

#### Trait bounds
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Although we've encountered them briefly in several earlier examples, I want to take a moment to more formally address the concept of trait bounds.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=9)** When working with generics, you'll often need to use traits to serve as bounds to stipulate the functionality a type implements.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=17)** Bounding restricts a generic to only data types that conform to those bounds so you can write functions that accept many different data types while also guaranteeing that the types it accepts will have the necessary behaviors for your function to use it.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=33)** Consider the print_type function shown here on line three, which accepts an input item of some generic type T and then prints a message with its data type by using the any module's type_name function, which returns the name of a given type as a string slice.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=50)** Since we're passing the input item as a parameter to the print line macro on line four, for this function to work, that given item has to be able to be displayed as output, which means it has to implement the Display trait.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=64)** The compiler can recognize that need and will prevent the program from compiling until we explicitly set a bound to restrict the generic type T to data types that implement Display.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=75)** The Display trait is part of the Rust Standard Library's format module, so to reference it, I'll need to import that module, and then, after defining the generic type T in the function definition, we'll add a colon, and then the name of the trait it's required to have.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=92)** fmt::Display.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=96)** In this program's main function, we call the print type function on the value 13, expressed using three different data types.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=103)** An integer, a float, and a string slice.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=106)** All three of those types implement the Display trait and can therefore be used with the print_type method.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=112)** When I run this program, we see three output messages indicating those three types passed as inputs.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=121)** Now, let's try passing the print_type function an input type that does not implement the Display trait.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=128)** Let's try passing an array containing the value 13.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=134)** When I try to run this program, the compiler produces an error.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=140)** It detects that the array does not meet the bound requirements so it gives us an error message along with some helpful suggestions.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=148)** Although the array type does not implement the Display trait, which is intended to provide user-facing output, it does implement the related Debug trait which is intended to provide programmer-facing output in a debug context.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=162)** So, let's modify our bounds to require the Debug trait instead.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=170)** And then modify the print_ statement to use debug formatting to display the item.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/trait-bounds?u=76281980&t=176)** Now, when I run the program the print_type function is able to accept all of those types as input arguments and we get four output messages describing each type.
