#### Multiple trait bounds
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There may be times when you need to impose multiple trait bounds on a generic type.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=5)** For example, consider the compare and print function shown here, which accepts two input parameters a and b, which could potentially be different data types.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=16)** It then compares a and b and prints a message to indicate whether or not they're equal.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=22)** Although this is a simple task, there are several traits that a and b need to implement for it to work.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=28)** First of all, since the print statements display the values of a and b, they will both need to implement the display trait.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=36)** So let's import the format module at the top of the program.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=42)** Then add bounds for the generic type T to implement display.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=52)** As well as the generic type U.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=58)** Next, since we'll be comparing a and b for equality, they'll need to implement the partial equality trait.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=65)** To specify multiple traits, we list them out separated by plus operators.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=70)** So T needs to implement display plus partial equality.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=79)** And U needs to implement display plus partial equality.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=85)** Note that the partial equality trait is included in the standard library's prelude, which is why we did not need to import the comparison module to reference it like we needed to import the format module for the display trait.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=98)** Now, there are a couple more traits that we'll need for this function, and they're not super obvious.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=104)** The equality operator compares two values of the same data type but this function allows a and b to be two different data types, T and U.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=115)** That's why line four uses the from method to convert the value of input b into data type T so that it matches type a and they can be compared.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=126)** For that method to work, the data type U has to be something that's able to be converted into type T.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=133)** So we'll need to set one more bound on data type T to make sure it has the from trait to convert from type U.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=142)** Now, when we call the from method on line four, that consumes the variable b.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=148)** Effectively moving it to a new variable of type T to use for the equality comparison.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=154)** That means when we try to use b in the print statements on lines five or seven, b will be unavailable because it's been moved.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=162)** The solution is to require that the data type for b implements the copy trait.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=170)** Fortunately, that's the end of the trait bounds we'll need for this function.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=174)** As you can see, the function signature has grown quite long.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=178)** If we had many more input variables and trait bounds, it could quickly grow to an unreasonable size.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=184)** To make this function signature more readable, REST provides an alternate syntax using a where clause.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=190)** To demonstrate how that looks, let's comment out line three so we still have it for reference.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=196)** Then copy and paste that function signature.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=210)** And remove all of the trait bounds we just added.
>
> **[3:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=221)** Next, on the following line but before the opening curly brace, we'll type the keyword where.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=232)** Then I'll copy and paste all of the bounds we placed on T and U.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=243)** And finally, I'll split those onto two lines after the comma.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=249)** This accomplishes the same thing as the commented out line three but it's much easier to read.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=256)** To test this function, the main function calls compare and print twice on float and integer values.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=263)** When I save and run this program, we see from the output that one is equal to one, but 1.1 is not equal to 1.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=276)** Now, let's try changing the data type for one of those inputs from an integer representation of one to a string slice representing one.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=287)** When I try to run the program now, it fails to compile.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=297)** The trait bound, that generic type U, can be converted to type T does not apply here because a string slice cannot be converted into a float value using the from method.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-trait-bounds-14414549?u=76281980&t=308)** By establishing all those bounds, we've ensured this generic function will only accept compatible data types.

#### Return types with implemented traits
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've used traits to set bounds on the generic data types that can be passed as arguments into a function.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=7)** Now, let's look at how we can use traits in the other direction to set bounds on the type of data that can be returned by a function.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=15)** Let's define a function that can return any data type as long as it implements the display trait.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=22)** Since we'll be referencing the display trait for this program, we'll first need to import the format module at the top of the code.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=30)** Then we'll define a new function named get displayable.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=35)** It doesn't have any input parameters, but it will have a return value.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=41)** We'll specify a return type that can be anything as long as it implements display.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=47)** And we do that by typing impl display.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=53)** This type of syntax for a parameter using the implementation keyword can be used in some situations as an alternative to the trait bound syntax we saw in the previous videos.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=64)** For simplicity, let's have this function return an integer value 13, which can definitely be displayed.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=71)** And then down in the main function, let's add a print macro to display the result of calling the get displayable function.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=80)** If I run this program, it works.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=85)** And we see from the print statement that the output was 13.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=89)** If we changed that return value from an integer to another type that also implements the display trait like a string slice and then run the program again, it still works.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=104)** The code calling the get displayable function doesn't need to note the exact type it's getting back, it just knows it will be something that implements the display trait and that's all it needs to use it.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=116)** As you might expect, if we change the return value to a data type that does not implement the display trait like an array, and then try to run the program, it fails to compile.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=132)** Now, let's see if this capability to return any type based on the traits it implements will allow us to return potentially different data types each time a function is called.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=143)** Let's add an input parameter to our get displayable function named choice which will be a Boolean.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=155)** If choice is true, we'll return 13 as an integer.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=161)** Otherwise, we'll return 13 as a string slice.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=168)** Then down in the main function, we'll pass the call to get displayable the value true which means we should get back an integer.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=177)** I'll try to run the program now, and sadly it fails to compile.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=183)** Even though this function is written to return any data type that implements the display trait, when the compiler runs, it needs to determine that actual data type that will be returned so it can include an execute the correct code.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=197)** The subroutines to display an integer value is probably a bit different than the subroutine to display a string literal.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=204)** The rust compiler doesn't allow this type of ambiguity and return type from a function.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/return-types-with-implemented-traits?u=76281980&t=209)** If you need to work with data types and trait bounds that can not be known until runtime, that gets into the topic of dynamic dispatch, which goes beyond the scope of this course.

### 13. Lifetimes

#### The borrow checker
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Earlier in this course, when discussing references and borrowing, we saw that the Rust compiler can detect when your program tries to use a reference, after the thing at points do has gone out of scope.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=12)** That's known as a dangling reference.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=15)** Consider the example program shown here, which declares a variable named propellant on line two without initializing it, who's scope is the body of the main function.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=25)** After that, a pair of curly braces enclose the code on lines four through six, which therefore exists in an inner sub scope.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=34)** Those three lines initialize a new string variable named rp1, assign the propellant variable a reference to that new rp1 string and then print propellant.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=45)** Since everything from the creation of rp1 to its final use takes place within that inner code block, if I run this program, everything works fine and we get the output message that propellant is rp1.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=60)** However, if we move that final print statement to after the inner code block and then run the program again, now we get a compiler error.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=72)** And this is an error we've encountered before in this course.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=77)** It says that the borrowed value of rp1, which is assigned to the propellant variable does not live long enough.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=84)** Rp1 goes out of scope at line six and gets dropped but the propellant variable, which references it, is used later in the print statement on line seven.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=94)** I really appreciate the level of detail and information the Rust compiler provides when it discovers errors like this.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=101)** Now, let's talk about how it does that.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=105)** Under the hood, the Rust compiler uses what's called the borrow checker, which compares the scope of variables to make sure all the borrows are valid.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=115)** Consider our example program with two variables, propellant which is declared at the top of the main function, though not initialized and rp1, which the propellant variable later borrows.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=127)** The borrow checker will determine the lifetime of the propellant variable or how long it's valid, which lasts here from when it's first declared until it goes out of scope and gets dropped.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=138)** We'll annotate that lifetime with the name apostrophe or tick mark a, which is the convention for labeling lifetimes in Rust.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=146)** Along those same lines, the lifetime of rp1, which we'll call tic b, runs from when it's declared until it goes out of scope.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=155)** The line where the propellant borrows rp1 falls within both lifetimes.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=160)** So, both variables are valid and everything works fine.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=165)** Similarly, the print statement on the next line is within the scope of both variables so we're good to go.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=172)** Now, let's consider the program, when the print statement is moved to after the inner code block.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=178)** The lifetime for propellant is the same as before but now the lifetime of rp1 is a bit shorter.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=185)** When propellant borrows rp1, that's still within both lifetimes so everything is fine.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=191)** But when the print statement uses the propellant variable which references rp1, now that's outside of rp1's lifetime.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=200)** The borrow checker recognizes the problem and gives us a detailed error message about it.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=205)** So, how might we resolve this problem?
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=208)** One solution would be to move the line declaring rp1 to before the inner code block, giving it a larger scope.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=216)** The lifetime of propellant is the same as before and rp1 has a lifetime that's nearly the same.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=223)** Now, the print statement using propellant is within both lifetimes and everything will work fine.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=229)** This concept of lifetimes may seem like we're just rehashing the concept of variable scope, which we already covered earlier in this course.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/the-borrow-checker?u=76281980&t=236)** And they're definitely related but it's important to understand that Rust not only considers the scope of variables you create and use but it will also look a layer deeper to consider the scope or lifetime of the values they reference using the borrow checker.

#### Lifetime annotation syntax
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are certain situations where the Rust compiler will require you to explicitly annotate the lifetime of variables.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=7)** Consider the best fuel function on line one which when given two options determines which one is the best type of rocket fuel.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=15)** It accepts two string references as input representing the fuels to compare, and then an if else expression compares the length of the two strings slices and returns whichever one is longer.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=27)** I'll admit it's not a scientific way to evaluate the quality of rocket fuel, but it works for a simple programming example.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=34)** Down in the main function, we initialize strings for the two types of propellant past references to those strings to the best fuel function, and then print the result.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=45)** There's nothing tricky going on here, and both propellant strings will still be alive and in scope when they're borrowed for the best fuel function and when the result is printed.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=54)** So it seems like everything should work.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=57)** Let's try running this program and surprise, we get a compiler error.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=64)** And this is a new one for us.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=67)** It says we're missing a lifetime specifier and points to the returned string reference saying it expects a named lifetime parameter.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=75)** The problem is that we're returning a borrowed reference but it's not possible to know which borrowed value that will be x or y.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=83)** That will depend on which string is longer.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=85)** Sure, you and I could look down at the main function in this example to see what the best fuel function is being passed as inputs and figure out what's going to happen, but when writing a function, we're writing code that could potentially be reused elsewhere, so it needs to be robust.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=102)** If we passed it string references based on user input there would be no way to know which one is longer until runtime.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=109)** The compilers error message suggests we introduce a named lifetime parameter and then provides an example function signature.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=118)** As always the rust compiler is doing its best to help us, so let's copy that line from the error message and then use it to replace our function signature on line one.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=131)** This syntax is similar to the generics we covered earlier in this course, but instead of defining a generic data type, it defines a generic lifetime for the input and output parameters.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=143)** We define the lifetime in angle brackets after the function name and then associate it with parameters.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=150)** Explicitly defining the lifetime of parameters like this is called lifetime annotation.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=156)** The name of a lifetime parameter must start with an apostrophe or tick mark.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=162)** Although you can name the lifetime anything you want, a convention is to use a single lowercase letter that keeps the name short like generic data types.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=171)** The example signature we got from the compiler defines a single lifetime, take a, which it then associates with both input parameters as well as the return parameter.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=183)** Notice that the lifetime parameter annotation is placed immediately after the borrow operator and adds a space to separate the annotation from the data type.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=193)** Now this lifetime annotation does not do anything to affect how long the references it gets passed as inputs lift.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=200)** That's purely on those referenced variables themselves.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=204)** What it does is tell the compiler how the lifetimes of the input parameters relate to each other.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=210)** This is telling Rust that the lifetime of the returned reference will be as long as the lifetime of the two input parameters x and y.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=219)** If x and y have different lifetimes the compiler will use the most restrictive or smaller of the two.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=225)** The borrow checker then uses that lifetime to validate that wherever the returned reference gets used later the value it points to will still be alive.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=235)** If I run this program now that we've added lifetime annotations it's able to compile and we get an output message that the result is RP 1, which makes sense because that's the longer of the two strings.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=249)** This all works because the lifetime of the two propellant variables are roughly the same from where they're declared on line's 11 and 12 to the end of the main function.
>
> **[4:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=259)** When they're borrowed as inputs on line 13, and then one of them is referenced as the result on line 14, both will still be alive.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=268)** Now let's add a set of curly braces to reduce the scope of the second propellant variable.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=276)** Now, propellant two will only live until the end of that code block at line 15.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=281)** When the borrow checker goes to validate the lifetime of the result, it will use the lifetime of propellant two because it's the more restrictive of the two inputs.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=290)** If I try to run the program now it fails to compile.
>
> **[4:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=296)** The borrow checker recognizes that the value of propellant two does not live long enough to potentially be referenced by the print statement at line 16.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=305)** The Rust compiler is really smart, so you might be wondering why it can't just analyze the body of our best fuel function to determine the lifetime of the returned value.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=315)** It's obvious we'll be returning either x or y and therefore the return value should have the same lifetime.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=321)** Why do we need these annotations?
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=324)** Sure, in this example it's obvious, and the compiler could analyze the body of the function but in a more complex function that references external code from a library or some other source it becomes nearly impossible for the compiler to determine the parameter lifetimes on its own.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=343)** For example, if the if else expression on lines two through six were part of a function from an external compiled library, the compiler would not have access to that source code to analyze what it's doing.
>
> **[5:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-annotation-syntax?u=76281980&t=356)** By explicitly annotating the lifetime of parameters we're giving the borrow checker the information it needs to be able to validate the returned reference.

#### Multiple lifetime annotations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the previous video, we saw that this program fails to compile because the lifetime of the return value from the best_fuel function on line one is annotated to be the most restrictive of the two input parameter lifetimes and the borrow checker detects that the propellant variable declared on line 13 doesn't live long enough to potentially be referenced as the result on line 16.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=23)** Now, let's see what happens if we remove the possibility that our best_fuel function could ever return the second input parameter by changing line five from y to x.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=35)** The function is still using the value referenced by y for the comparison on line two but then it just ignores the result of that comparison to always return x.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=46)** Let's try running this program and it still fails to compile with the same error as before, because propellant2 goes out of scope before line 16 prints the result.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=59)** But it's obvious here that propellant2 could never be the result because our best_fuel function always returns the first input x, so why is this still failing?
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=71)** Even though y will never be returned, the annotations we added still specified that x, y and the return reference all share the same generic lifetime, take a.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=83)** So that's what the borrow checker will enforce.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=85)** Since the lifetime of y doesn't have anything to do with the return value now, we don't actually need it.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=91)** So let's delete that from the function definition and then run the program again.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=99)** Now the borrow checker only requires the returned reference to have the same lifetime as x, so everything compiles and works.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=106)** Even though we don't technically need an annotation for y in this scenario, if someone comes along to use this code later it might not be clear if that was intentional or if we simply forgot to include the annotation on y.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=120)** So to make things more explicit, let's define a second generic lifetime for y.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=126)** In the angle brackets after take a, we'll add a comma, followed by the name of our second lifetime, take b then we'll annotate the parameter y to use take b.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/multiple-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=140)** This accomplishes the same thing as before when we remove the annotation from y but now it's more explicitly clear that the lifetime of the returned reference is not at all dependent on the lifetime of y.

#### Lifetime elision rules
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] You might be wondering why we've been able to write Rust functions throughout this course without needing to explicitly annotate lifetimes.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=8)** Then when we reach this chapter suddenly the compiler starts requiring it.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=13)** For example, the code shown here is from an earlier video about using slices as parameters.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=19)** The get_first_word function on line seven has borrowed string slices as its input and output parameters.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=25)** But this program will still compile and run just fine without lifetime annotations.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=31)** Back in the early days of Rust before it even reached version 1.0, the compiler required the programmer to explicitly annotate the lifetime for every parameter that was a reference.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=43)** So back then this get_first_word function would have had to define a lifetime, tick A and then associate that with the input parameter as well as the return parameter.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=57)** Requiring those annotations made sense at the time, because it aligned with the goal of making Rust a memory safe language that catches reference problems during compilation rather than later when the program is running and the error can have significant impact.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=72)** However, after writing lots of code, the team of developers behind Rust realized there were some common situations that followed patterns in which the borrow checker could safely infer the lifetime on its own and adding these explicit annotations wasn't actually necessary.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=89)** So, to make life easier for Rust programmers, they gave the compiler the ability to recognize those patterns using a set of Lifetime Elision Rules.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=99)** It's a series of rules the compiler follows to analyze reference lifetimes.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=104)** If your code matches these certain patterns, you can allude or omit the explicit lifetime annotations.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=111)** However, if after going through the rules there's still any ambiguity about the lifetime a reference should have, the compiler will raise an error and require you to include annotations.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=123)** There are currently three Lifetime Elision rules.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=126)** The first rule assigns each input parameter that's a reference, its own distinct lifetime.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=132)** So for example, a function with only one reference parameter would need to have one lifetime.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=138)** Keep in mind, lifetimes only apply to parameters that are references, so if there are other non-reference parameters, those do not get assigned lifetimes.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=149)** A function with two input parameters that are references, like get_longest shown here would get two separate lifetimes.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=156)** Three input references would be assigned three distinct lifetimes and so on.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=161)** The second rule says, "if there is exactly one input lifetime that lifetime gets used for all output lifetimes."
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=169)** And this makes sense because the lifetime of an output reference needs to match one of the input lifetimes.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=175)** If there's only one input lifetime then that choice is obvious.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=180)** So this rule would apply to the get_first_word function shown here and since the compiler is able to infer all of the reference lifetimes, this would qualify for elision and we can choose to omit the explicit annotations.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=194)** However, rule two does not apply to the get_longest function with two input references because rule number one assigned it two separate input lifetimes.
>
> **[3:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=204)** Since it doesn't meet the elision requirements, the compiler will require us to add explicit annotations to get_longest.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=212)** The third rule applies to the signature for methods which we'll look at in the next video on Storing References and Structs.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=219)** If one of the input parameters is a reference to self or a mutable self reference then the lifetime assigned to self by rule number one will be used for all output lifetimes.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=230)** For example this send_transition method transmits the input message and then returns a string slice with information about the sender.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=239)** It would be assigned to input lifetimes by rule number one and then rule number three would use the lifetime assigned to self, shown here as tick A, with a return value.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=250)** This is intended to cover the common use case where method returns the value from one of a structs fields which in this case is a reference.
>
> **[4:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=258)** That said, if the reference that the method is returning is not coming from the struct itself, but somewhere else the compiler will catch that and require you to include explicit lifetime annotations.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=270)** If after working through these rules, the compiler is still not able to determine the output lifetime, it will require you to include explicit annotations.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=280)** Keep in mind, these elision rules are not for you, they're for the compiler to follow to make your life easier.
>
> **[4:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=287)** But as a Rust programmer, it's helpful to be aware of these rules, to know when you can leave out lifetime annotations.
>
> **[4:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=294)** Also, there's no harm in including explicit annotations even when they're not required.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=301)** Also keep in mind, these elision rules are enhancements that were added to Rust since its earliest days.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/lifetime-elision-rules?u=76281980&t=307)** So, it's possible that as the compiler continues to be improved, future versions may have even more elision rules to describe common patterns or annotations can be omitted.

#### Struct lifetime annotations
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=0)** - Instructor] When struct stores data using the string data type it has ownership of that string.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=6)** So when the struct goes out of scope that string data will also be dropped and removed from the heat.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=12)** Consider the simplified shuttle structure shown here which stores the shuttle's name as a string.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=18)** It has a single method named send transmission on line six which accepts an input slice for the message to transmit, prints that message and then returns a reference to the shuttle's name.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=29)** If I run this program line 17 calls that method to transmit a greeting from orbit and then we see from the return string slice that the sender was endeavor.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=41)** Now let's try modifying that struck to store the name as a string slice instead of a full string.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=48)** We'll change the struct definition.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=51)** And since we're now using a borrow reference we no longer need to use the borrow operator for the return expression on line eight.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=59)** We also need to change line 14 to initialize the new shuttle, using a string slice for the name.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=67)** When I run the program now It fails to compile.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=73)** It gives us an error that says it's missing a lifetime specifier.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=77)** Since the struct no longer owns the name variable because it's a reference it's not clear how the lifetime of the borrowed string relates to the lifetime of the struct.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=87)** If that string is dropped then disappears while the struct is still in scope then the struck tries to reference and use the no longer existence string.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=95)** And that would be a problem.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=96)** So we need to add explicit lifetime annotations to indicate how the lifetime of the struck relates to the string slice it's storing.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=105)** And fortunately, the compiler gives us a hint about how to do that.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=109)** After the struct named shuttle on line one, we'll use a pair of angle brackets to declare a new generic lifetime tick A and then we'll annotate the reference with that lifetime.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=120)** Doing that actually changes the structure data type.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=123)** So we'll need to modify line five so that the shuttle data type matches its definition on line one with the lifetime annotation.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=131)** And in addition to that we'll also need to declare a lifetime parameter after the implementation keyword.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=138)** This introduces the lifetime tick A to the whole implementation block so we can use it.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=144)** I can run the program now and everything compiles and works and endeavor sends that's greeting from orbit.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=153)** Notice that we did not need to include lifetime annotations in the method definition on line six.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=158)** And that's because of the third lifetime allision rule.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=161)** Rust will assign the lifetime of self to the return value and that makes sense because we're returning the struct name which is part of the struct itself and has a similar lifetime.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=172)** Now let's say, instead of returning to shuttle's name we want to return the message that it just transmitted.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=178)** To do that I'll modify line five, to return the transmitted message and then run the program again.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=187)** And now it fails to compile.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=191)** It says there's a lifetime mismatch with the message parameter.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=196)** Remember that lifetime allision rule number one we'll start by giving each of the input parameters their own lifetime.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=203)** Rule number two, doesn't apply here, but rule number three does and it will assign the lifetime of the self reference to the output parameter.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=211)** However, since we're now returning the message that output lifetime will need to match it instead of the struct.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=218)** To fix that we'll need to add explicit annotations to the method signature.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=223)** We'll annotate these self-reference with tick A, give the message a second generic lifetime tick B, and then annotate the return parameter to use tick B as well.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=235)** Finally, since this introduces a new lifetime parameter tick B we'll need to declare it.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=241)** And we can do that for the entire implementation block by adding it alongside tick A after the implement keyword.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/struct-lifetime-annotations?u=76281980&t=249)** When I run the program now It's able to compile and we see that the send transmission method was able to return a reference to the message.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** struct (10), self (3), let (2), type. (1), finally, (1)
> **Speakers:** - instructor] (1)

#### Static lifetime
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There is a lifetime name that's reserved in Rust for special purposes, and that is static.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=6)** The static lifetime indicates that data is available for the entire duration of a program.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=12)** That data never gets dropped, so you can safely reference it at any time.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=16)** For example, the text for a string literal is stored directly in the program's binary, so it never goes away.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=23)** That data is available from start to finish.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=26)** Therefore, all string literals have a static lifetime, which can be annotated as shown here.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=32)** The static lifetime can be associated with a reference to indicate that it will never become invalid and that it lives longer than any other lifetime.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=41)** However, references that have a static lifetime such as string literals can be coerced to other more restrictive lifetimes if that reference is passed as the output parameter from a function with another lifetime annotation, though that's rarely done.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=57)** You may also see the static lifetime used as a trait bound when defining generic data types to ensure that the data has a static lifetime so the receiver can hold onto it and use it as long as they want knowing it will never become invalid.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=71)** For example, in addition to implementing the display trait, the generic type T described here includes the static lifetime, which indicates that whatever data type T is, it does not contain any non-static references.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/static-lifetime?u=76281980&t=86)** A full demonstration of the static lifetime is beyond the scope of this course, but I want to make sure you're aware of it in case you encounter it in existing code or perhaps need to use it for your own programs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** static (10), function (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (2), such as (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Match operator
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In rust, enums are commonly used in match expressions, which can control the flow of a program.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980&t=6)** Similar to a sequence of, if else expressions the match operator compares a given value to a series of patterns to determine which code to execute, similar to a switch statement in several other programming languages.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980&t=20)** A good analogy for the match operator is a coin sorting machine which uses a ramp with a series of sequentially larger holes to sort coins, based on the relative size.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980&t=30)** If you drop a penny into the sorter it will roll pass the first hole because a penny is larger than a dime.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980&t=36)** And then when it reaches the second hole which is sized for a penny, it will fall through to be collected in a container for pennies below.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-operator?u=76281980&t=44)** If you drop a quarter into the sorter it will roll past all the smaller holes until it reaches the quarter size slot, and then it will fall through.
>

#### Match with default placeholder
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The match operator can be used to evaluate more than just enums.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=4)** To demonstrate that, let's initialize a variable named my number and assign it the value one, which will be stored as a u8 by using the suffix notation to specify its data type.

>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=64)** If my number is one, we'll return a string that says one, two will return two, three, three.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=76)** Hmm, there's a lot of potential values that my number could be.
>

> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=84)** That's a lot to type out and I feel like stopping right here.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=89)** We can see that my number is assigned the value of one on line two and our match statement has a case for one so that's covered.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=96)** If none of the other cases will ever get executed, do we really need them?
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=101)** Let's try running this program to see what happens.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=106)** Surprise, surprise, it fails to compile.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=109)** The error message says that patterns ranging from the number four to the maximum value of a u8 are not covered.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=116)** And the help message reminds us that all possible cases must be handled which can be done by adding wild cards or more match arms.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=123)** So, even though in this particular example, it's clear that only the case for one will ever get executed, the compiler will not let us leave any ambiguity in a match expression.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=134)** And that keeps things safe.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=136)** If we change the value of my number later on, or perhaps if my number comes from an external source, like user input, we want to make sure there is no possible way the program will end up in a state where it doesn't know what to do next.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=149)** Now, rather than explicitly typing out all 256 possible cases here, Rust gives us a handy wildcard pattern, which will match any value.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=161)** To use that, we'll add one more match arm to the match sequence after all the rest using an underscore character which represents the wild card.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=171)** You can think of this as the default case which will get executed if none of the other arms before it match.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=177)** For this case, rather than returning a string slice, let's also print a message.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=182)** To execute multiple operations for a match arm, we'll define a code block using curly braces.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=189)** We'll print a message that the number did not match, and then return a string slice that says it was something else.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=200)** That covers all possible cases.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=202)** So I'll run the program.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=205)** And we get a message that the result is one, which is the value of my number.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=210)** Next, let's change my number to a value we haven't accounted for like four, run the program again.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=220)** And that matches our default case which prints a message that four did not match.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=224)** And then we see the returned result is something else.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=227)** It's important that we put the wild card pattern for the default case at the end after all the other match arms, because the match operator will evaluate each pattern sequentially from top to bottom and execute the first match it finds.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=241)** Let's see what happens if we use the wildcard pattern earlier in that sequence by replacing the pattern to match for one.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=248)** And then change the value of my number to something that should match later in the sequence, like three.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=257)** When I run the program now, the output shows that the returned result is one.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=264)** Which shows that the match sequence never reached the pattern for three on line eight.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/match-with-default-placeholder?u=76281980&t=269)** So, when you're using the wildcard pattern, always make sure to include it at the end.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (10), case, (2), match. (2), else. (2), type. (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (2)
> **Cross-References:** earlier in (1), later in (1)
> **Analogies:** similar to (1)
> **Best Practices:** always make sure (1)

#### Option<T> enum
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=0)** - In addition to working with values that represent something.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=4)** In programming we'll often also need to represent the concept that a value could be nothing.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=10)** Many programming languages use a null value to indicate when there is no value.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=15)** In those languages, variables can either be null or not null meaning they do have a value.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=21)** It's a simple and straightforward system.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=24)** But problems arise when you try to use a null value in something that requires a not null value.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=29)** It's easy to do by accident and it's a common source of runtime errors.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=34)** That's not safe.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=36)** And therefore Rust does not have a null value in the traditional sense like many other languages.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=42)** Instead, Rust implements the concept using a generic Enum named option, which can be one of two variants.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=49)** If it's the sum variant that indicates it's not null and does have a value which is stored Enum.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=56)** Otherwise, if the Enum is the none variant that indicates it does not have a value.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=62)** This achieves the same concept as the traditional null value, but by implementing it in terms of an Enum data type, the compiler can check to make sure you're handling it correctly.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=73)** The option Enum is so commonly used.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=75)** It's included in the prelude so you don't need to import anything extra to use it.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=80)** You can instantiate a new option Enum to hold something using the variant name, some followed by the value to store in parentheses.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=89)** The generic type T will be replaced with the data type of the value you passed in.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=94)** To instantiate a new option Enum holding nothing, you can simply use the variant name none with a capital N.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=102)** To demonstrate how you might encounter and use the option Enum, consider this simple example with an array of five numbers and a print statement online for that attempts to index element five which is beyond the range of the array.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=116)** If I try to run this program, as we should expect the compiler throws an error because the index is out of bounds.

> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=139)** If I run the program now, it successfully compiles and runs without crashing.

> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=153)** So there was nothing to get.

> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=155)** Let's modify the get method to use a valid index, such as four.

>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=174)** Rust knows that the number of variable will always be assigned in option Enum referencing an integer so the compiler can verify its usage based on that data type.
>

>

>

>

>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=210)** And this time it fails to compile.

> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=213)** The error message says, it cannot add an integer to the option Enum holding an integer.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=219)** Keep in mind the option Enum is its own data type.

>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=257)** This time, although the program successfully compiles when it's running and tries to unwrap the non-value it panics and crashes.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=265)** And that makes sense because there's nothing to unwrap.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=268)** The recommendation here is to use a variation of the unwrap method called unwrap or.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=274)** This takes in an input parameter for the value to return if the option is none.

> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=282)** Now, the data type for this parameter will need to match the data type of the option Enum which will be a reference to an integer within the array.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=290)** So we'll need to add the borrow operator in front of zero to make it a reference.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=295)** So, if number has some value the unwrap or method will return it.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=300)** Otherwise if it's none, we'll get back zero.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=304)** I'll run the program one final time.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=307)** And since the get method returns none, unwrapping it will return zero.
>
> **[5:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144757?u=76281980&t=312)** And zero plus one is one as shown in the output.

#### If-let syntax
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've learned about the match pattern and its requirement to handle every possible case, you might be wondering, what if I only care about one of those many possible patterns?
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=12)** In the example shown here, we instantiate an option enum with the integer value 13 and then use a match expression to evaluate it.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=20)** If it's 13, we'll print a message, but otherwise, if that option enum is anything else, including none, we do nothing.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=28)** This code will work and I can prove it by running it and we get the output message of 13.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=34)** However, Rust provides a simpler way to write expressions like this one, which only check for a single condition by using an if let statement.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=43)** We'll replace the match expression with the keywords if let and then the pattern we're looking for, which is some(13).
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=54)** After that, we'll use the assignment operator, followed by the variable we're checking, and then the code to execute.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=62)** In this case, just a print statement that says 13.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=66)** If I run this program, it accomplishes basically the same thing as the match statement did before but now in a slightly simpler syntax.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=77)** If we look at the match and if let versions of that code side by side, we can see the same components at play.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=84)** The number variable serves as the value we want to compare and the sum value with 13 is the value we're looking to match and then print the message 13.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=94)** The if let syntax on the right can be described as syntactic sugar for the corresponding match expression on the left because it's simpler to write, and it's something you'll likely encounter when programming with Rust.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/if-let-syntax?u=76281980&t=106)** To see more examples of how if let expressions can be used, I recommend looking at this page from the Rust by Example documentation on the if let syntax.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (6), case, (2), enum (2), else, (1), for, (1)
