### 15. Error Handling

#### Unrecoverable errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've seen a lot of errors throughout this course.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=3)** Usually done on purpose to demonstrate a point, but nonetheless, errors are part of life when it comes to software.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=10)** Fortunately, Rust has several features for handling errors to make our code more reliable.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=16)** Now, when we're talking about errors here, we're not talking compilation errors that prevent your program from being built, we're talking about runtime errors that occur after your code has successfully compiled and is executing.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=30)** Rust categorizes those errors into two categories, recoverable and unrecoverable.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=37)** Recoverable errors are problems that your program can reasonably do something about to resolve.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=42)** For example, if a user tries to open a file that doesn't exist, your program might display a message that the file was not found and tell them to try a different path.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=53)** Unrecoverable errors are problems like indexing beyond the bounds of an array, which is not allowed in Rust.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=59)** Most languages do not distinguish between these two types of errors and they use exceptions to handle all of them.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=66)** Rust does not have exceptions in the traditional programming sense.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=70)** Instead, we have the Result enum type for recoverable errors and the panic macro for unrecoverable errors.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=79)** Calling the panic macro causes the program to terminate immediately and stop execution while also providing feedback to the caller of the program about what went wrong.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=89)** We can manually call the panic macro in our program as panic followed by the bang symbol, followed by an error message.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=98)** Houston, we've had a problem.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=103)** If I run this program, we can see from the error output that the thread main panicked at Houston, we've had a problem.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=113)** And then it points to the file and line where the panic occurred.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=116)** This tells us to look at main.rs line two, five characters in to investigate the cause of the problem.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=124)** The process will also exit with the code 101, indicating that it panicked.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=129)** That's how to intentionally cause your program to panic and stop.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=132)** Now let's look at a scenario when something else causes it to panic.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=136)** I'll comment out the macro on line two, and then paste in a little code snippet, which will cause a problem.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=144)** This routine initializes an array with the numbers five through zero, then iterates through those numbers to print a countdown message and also uses those numbers as the denominator in the division operation on line eight.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=157)** That division operation isn't doing anything useful but it's there to cause a problem for this example.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=163)** When I run this program, we see from the output that it was able to successfully count down to zero and then the program panicked when it attempted to divide by zero, that's not allowed.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=176)** Notice that the file it points us to this time is not our main.rs file, but instead it's arith.rs which looks to be located somewhere in the Rust library.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=187)** If I hold control and click on that file path, the vs code editor, will open it.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=193)** This way looks to be part of the division operation from the arithmetic library which is what our program called and caused the panic.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=201)** To get more information about the sequence of function calls that led to it, we can enable the Rust backtrace environment variable as described in the panic message.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=211)** To do that, in the Windows PowerShell, I'll type $Env:RUST_BACKTRACE=1.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=226)** Then I'll press enter to set the variable.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=229)** If you're using another environment or operating system, the commands to set an environment variable may differ.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=236)** Now I'll use cargo run to run the program again.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=239)** And this time we get a ton of output when the program panics.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=243)** This is called a backtrace and it shows us all of the functions that have been called to get us to this point.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=250)** To read the backtrace, start from top and then scroll down until you see the file names that are part of your program.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=256)** It looks like the fifth item here, references and unrecoverable error on line eight of main.rs, which is where our program tried to divide by zero.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=265)** Everything above that is the code our program called.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=269)** You can see lines three and four reference the division operation from the core library.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=274)** All of the lines below our file indicate code that called our program.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=278)** Which might include code from the standard library or other core Rust code.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=283)** If your program is panicking for unknown reasons, looking at the backtrace can sometimes help you locate the source of the problem.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/unrecoverable-errors?u=76281980&t=290)** When you're done, you can turn off the backtrace feature by setting that backtrace environment variable back to zero.

#### Result<T, E> enum
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Many runtime errors are recoverable meaning they're not so serious we need to panic and terminate the program, we can still do something to smoothly handle the problem and continue onwards.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=11)** For example, if our program tries to access a file that does not exist that's a problem.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=17)** We might resolve it by letting the user know and giving them the opportunity to select a different file path or our program might choose to create the missing file.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=27)** It just depends on what we need the program to do.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=30)** To facilitate handling those types of recoverable errors, the Russ standard library defines the result enum with two variants, okay and error representing the result of an operation that can either succeed or fail.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=44)** The okay variant stores the value for the success as generic type T and the error variants stores the error value as generic type E.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=53)** Like the option enum, the result enum is common enough to be included in the prelude so you do not need to explicitly import it into your programs.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=63)** We've actually encountered the result enum several times throughout this course without directly addressing it.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=69)** For example, the read_to_string function on line four opens a file and reads its contents to a string.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=76)** That has the potential for an error if the file is unavailable.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=79)** So rather than returning a string that function actually returns a result enum indicating whether or not the operation was a success.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=88)** The program will capture that result with the contents variable and then line five will use debug formatting to print it.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=95)** If I run this program, we see that content is the error variant of the enum which is storing the error with code two, the kind is not found and a message that says the system cannot find the file specified.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=110)** The read_to_string function was trying to open a file named the_ultimate_question.txt which doesn't exist because well nobody knows the ultimate question of life, the universe and everything.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=122)** However, we do know the answer to that question and that's a file that does exist on my computer.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=127)** We'll all modify the file name on line four to be the answer to the ultimate question.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=134)** I'll run the program again and this time because that file does exist, the read_to_string function is successful and we get back an okay variant of the result enum, holding a string that says 42 which is the contents of that text file.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=150)** That result enum is storing the string data we want within it but we generally cannot use the result enum directly.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=157)** So how do we get that string that it's holding out?
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=161)** One option would be to use the unwrap method.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=165)** You'll probably recognize this method because we've used it several times throughout this course as a simplified way to work with result enums.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=172)** If the result is okay, the unwrap method will return it's stored value.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=177)** So if I run this program, we see that now the output is just a string that says 42 not the result enum holding that string.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=187)** We've successfully extracted its value.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=190)** While the unwrap method is simple to use, I don't recommend it because if the result is an error it will panic and terminate the program.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=199)** Let's change that file name back to just the_ultimate_question which does not exist, run the program again and now it panics when it reaches that line and tries to unwrap the error.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=213)** That panic message points us to the result unwrap method as the source of the problem.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=218)** If we want to panic with a custom error message, we can use a close cousin to the unwrap method, expect.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=226)** Expect accepts an input parameter for the message to display.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=230)** So let's say if the program fails to open the_ultimate_question.txt that's because nobody knows the ultimate question.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=240)** I'll run the program again.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=243)** And now when it panics, it includes our custom message along with the other contents of the error.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3144759?u=76281980&t=249)** Unlike the unwrap method, the expect method is simple to use but I generally recommend against it because there are better ways to handle recoverable errors as we'll see in the next video.

#### Matching Result<T, E> to recover from errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Rather than simply panicking if a result enum is an error by using the unwrap or expect methods to extract it stored value, a better solution is to use a match expression to process the result.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=13)** And in the case that it is an error, you can potentially take some other action to resolve it.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=18)** Continuing from the instate of the previous video, let's delete the expect method from line four.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=25)** And since we're no longer extracting the contents from results with the expect method, let's also change the variable name from contents to result to better reflect that it's a result enum.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=37)** Next, we'll use a match expression to get the contents from the resulte enum.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=42)** Let contents = match result.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=47)** If the enum is okay, then it will be storing the message that was read from a file.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=53)** So let's return that message string.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=59)** And if the result enum was an error, rather than panic and terminate the program like before, let's return a string with the message that nobody knows the ultimate question.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=75)** Now, if I run this program, the output message shows that the contents returned by the match expression was our error string because the ultimate question.text does not exist.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=89)** There may be times when you want to perform different error handling actions based on the type of error that occurs.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=95)** For example, if a file is not found, maybe your program should create it.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=99)** The errorkind enum lists a bunch of variants of general IO errors your program may encounter.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=105)** And we can use this with a match expression to select different types of errors.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=110)** Errorkind is part of the standard libraries IO module.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=113)** So to use it, we'll need to import the IO module at the top of our program.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=118)** Next, we'll replace the string for our error case with another match expression.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=125)** Which we'll match on error.kind.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=129)** This kind method returns an errorkind enumm indicating the type of error.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=135)** For the first match arm, let's check to see if it's a notfound error.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=142)** And if so, to keep this example simple, let's just return a string indicating that the file was not found.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=149)** For the next match arm, let's check for the permissiondenied error.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=155)** And if so, we'll return a string saying permission denied.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=160)** Finally, since there are lots of other kinds of errors, let's use the wildcard pattern to handle all of those.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=167)** If the error was anything else, we'll panic with a message that it was another type of error.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=178)** And we'll use debug formatting to display that error.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=182)** If I run this program now, the match expression detects that the error was a file not found error and returns the corresponding string.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/3142804?u=76281980&t=192)** Using match expressions like this to handle recoverable errors enables you to write programs that are more resilient to runtime errors that will inevitably occur.

#### Propagating errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Teacher] Sometimes when code within a function has the potential to produce an error, rather than handling that error within the function you may want to propagate it upwards to the higher level code that called the function where more information and context may be available to better decide how to deal with that error.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=18)** Consider the read and combine function shown here on line four.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=22)** It takes the path of the two input files, reads both of their contents, and then combines their contents to produce a single output string.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=30)** Notice that the return type for this function is a result enum, which will either be the okay variant containing the combined string or an error.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=39)** Since the read to string function that it uses has the potential to fail with an IO error and returns a result enum, the match statements on lines five and nine check those results and if either one produces an error it will return that error, propagating it up to where this function was called.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=58)** We can see down in the main function, line 19 calls read and combine on two files and then the match statement on line 20 prints one of two messages based on the result.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=70)** If I run this program we can see from the output that it was successful because it displays the result showing a list of the eight planets combined with the five known dwarf planets.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=82)** Now, I'll modify one of those file paths to be invalid, we'll change planets.txt to planet.txt which does not exist, I'll run the program again, and now it fails to open the file.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=98)** The function passes that error back to the main function for it's error handling code to deal with.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=104)** Now let's return that file path back to normal.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=109)** This pattern for propagating errors is so common Rust actually provides an easier shorthand way to accomplish the same thing.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=117)** Putting the question mark operator immediately after the result enum will accomplish the same thing as the two match statements in the read and combine function, returning if there's an error.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=127)** Let's modify the first match statement to use the shorthand by deleting the match keyword, and then deleting the code block.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=139)** Finally we'll add a question mark after the read to string function.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=143)** I'll leave the second match statement as the more verbose match statement for reference so we can see them side to side because they accomplish the same thing.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=154)** If I run this program it performs just like before, but the code on line five is more compact and simpler to read.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=163)** Keep in mind you can only use this question mark shorthand in functions that return a result enum.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=169)** Also, note that the return type, defined on line four, specifies the error type as an IO error.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=176)** If we called a function within read and combine that returned a result enum with another type of error we would not be able to propagate that result enum as the return value because the enum types won't match.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=189)** If you find yourself needing more flexibility to potentially return different types of errors check out this section from the Rust By Example documentation which provides several strategies for handling multiple mixed error types.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/propagating-errors?u=76281980&t=205)** There's also some great discussion in the error handling section of the Rust book that I recommend reading if you'd like to learn more about when it makes sense for code to panic versus returning a result enum.

#### Solution: Handle errors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Here's my solution to the challenge to add error handling to the higher or lower game.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=10)** Since the read line and parse methods are the two parts of this program that could potentially produce an error, and calling parse is dependent on the read line method having been successful, so there's something in the buffer to parse, I implemented my error handling by putting them in a pair of nested match statements.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=29)** If the call to read line on line 11 is successful, then the parse method will get called on line 12 to attempt to parse the contents of the buffer into a u32 value.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=39)** If that is then successful, the match arm on line 13 returns that value, which gets assigned to the guess variable.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=47)** If the result from either of those two method calls is an error, then one of the match arms on line 14 or 19 will execute.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=55)** Those code blocks print a message describing what failed, prompt the user to guess again, and then call the continue keyword.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=62)** Calling continue will terminate the current loop iteration so none of the if else statements down below execute.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=68)** Instead, execution will jump back to the beginning of the loop at line nine to get another input from the user.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=75)** That's all there is to it.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=78)** Now, if I run this program, I can enter a valid number like 50, as five, zero, and it responds like before, telling me that guess was too low.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=91)** If I enter something that cannot be parsed into a u32, such as 50.2, the program says it failed to parse the input and allows me to guess again.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=103)** I'll try another invalid entry, fifty written out as a string, and the same thing happens.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-handle-errors?u=76281980&t=110)** The program will let me keep guessing until I finally start giving it valid inputs and eventually guess the secret number.

### 16. Collections

#### Vectors
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] As we approach the end of this course, I want to show you a couple more common data types, you're likely to encounter when programming with Rust.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=8)** The first of those is the vector which we've already encountered as the return type from some standard functions.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=15)** A vector holds a collection of elements with the same data type and stores them sequentially in order.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=22)** That may sound like the definition of an array, but there's a key difference.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=26)** Arrays have a fixed size that must be known at compile time because array data gets stored on the stack.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=32)** Vectors on the other hand, can dynamically grow and shrink by adding and removing elements so you do not need to know its size when instantiating a vector.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=42)** The vectors data is stored on the heap, and therefore we need to handle ownership and borrowing like we would with string data which is also stored on the heat.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=51)** The code on line two shows the Syntax for creating a new vector which will start out empty without any elements.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=59)** Notice that we have to specify the type of data the vector will hold, which in this case will be strings.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=65)** I've also made the variable mutable so we can add and remove items from it.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=70)** The next three lines use the push method to add several astronaut names.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=74)** The push method will append each item to the back of the vector.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=79)** Finally, line six displays the content of the vector with debug formatting.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=84)** I'll run this program.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=86)** And the output shows that the astronauts vector has three names in the order in which they were pushed onto it.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=93)** The Syntax to access vector elements is similar to arrays.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=97)** Let's get the third item, which will be at index two because like arrays, vectors are also zero indexed.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=107)** Now, since the vector owns the value it's storing, we won't be able to pass ownership of the string to the third variable.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=114)** Instead we'll need to reference it using the borrow operator.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=119)** Then after that let's print the name of our third astronaut.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=125)** I'll run the program.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=129)** And now we see that the third American astronaut into space was John Glenn.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=134)** To remove items from the vector, We can use the pop method, which removes the last element from the vector and returns it as an Option enum.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=143)** Let's pop the last astronaut off that list and assign it to a variable name last and then print its value.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=153)** Since it's an Option enum, we'll need to use the debug formatting here.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=156)** Now, if I run this program, it successfully compiles and we see that last is the sum enum containing Glenn, but then the program panics and terminates.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=169)** The problem is line 11 which is trying to index the third item but that item no longer exists because we popped it from the vector on line eight.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=178)** This is similar to the index out of bounds error we previously encountered with arrays, but unlike arrays, since the size of a vector is dynamic, the compiler cannot check to make sure an index will be within bounds.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=192)** That will only be known at runtime when you try to access it.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=195)** In alternate and perhaps safer way to access vector items is with the Git method.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=200)** I'll comment outline 11 then add another line to instantiate the third variable and I'll assign it astronaut.get with an index of two.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=213)** This Git method returns an Option enum containing a reference to the value at the specified index.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=220)** And since we are turning an enum, we'll need to use the debug formatter to display it.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=224)** So I'll modify the print statement, then I'll run the program.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=232)** And since the third element of the list has been removed, by the time the Git method is called on line 12, it returns the non-variant of the Option enum.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=240)** If there had been a third value, it would have returned to some, with a reference to it.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=245)** One last thing to show you regarding vectors, when creating a new vector, if you want to pre-populate it with some initial values, you can do that using the vec! Macro.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=256)** Let's create a new vector representing a countdown.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=261)** This shorthand is a bit cleaner to read than instantiating a new empty vector and then pushing those five values onto it one at a time.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=270)** Also notice that we don't have to specify the data type for the vector elements.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=274)** The compiler recognizes these initial values are 32 bit integers and configures the new vector accordingly.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=282)** There are lots of other methods available to manipulate the items in a vector.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/vectors?u=76281980&t=286)** And I encourage you to check out these standard libraries documentation page for vectors to see all the methods that are available to use.

#### HashMaps
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Another common data type for working with collections is the HashMap, which stores data and key value pairs.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=7)** Instead of accessing elements using a numeric index, like with a vector, you look up data in a HashMap by providing a key to retrieve the corresponding value.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=18)** Note this key value pairing only maps one way, so you cannot easily go the other way to provide a value and get the corresponding key or keys.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=28)** Under the hood, REST uses a hashing function to determine how to store the keys and values in memory so they can be quickly located.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=36)** If you have experienced programming in other languages, you may have encountered a similar data structure, although often under different names, such as map, hash table, dictionary or associative array.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=49)** the keys and their corresponding values can be different data types represented with the generic type variables, K and V respectively.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=56)** However, all of the keys must have the same type and all of the values must have the same type.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=63)** Additionally, each key can only have one value associated with it at a time.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=68)** It's possible to have multiple instances of the same value associated with different keys, but you cannot have duplicate keys.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=77)** Let's take a look at some example code.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=80)** Unlike vectors, which are common enough to be included in the prelude, to use a HashMap, we need to manually import it as I've done here on the first line.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=89)** Line four shows the syntax to create a new HashMap, in this case, a HashMap storing the number of missions flown by several astronauts.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=97)** Notice that I have not defined the data types for the HashMap's key and values on line four.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=103)** by inserting entries into the HashMap on lines five through seven, the compiler is able to infer those data types.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=110)** The first argument for the insert method is a key, which in this example, are string slices representing the last names of astronauts.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=118)** And then the second argument is the value to associate with that key.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=123)** When I run this program, the print statement on line eight shows that Barron has zero missions, Hurley has three, and Hadfield has three.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=134)** Something to keep in mind when working with HashMaps is that the order keys are stored is not necessarily the order in which you inserted them.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=142)** It depends on the seed value for the underlying hash algorithm, which can vary from run to run.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=148)** Therefore, you should not rely on the keys being stored in a certain relative order when using a HashMap.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=155)** Now that we've stored a bunch of values in a HashMap, let's see how to retrieve them.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=160)** Let's say we want to see how many missions Kayla Barron has flown.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=165)** On the missions_flown HashMap, we'll call the get method and pass it a key for it to retrieve the corresponding value.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=175)** That get method returns an option enum.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=178)** If the key does not exist, you'll get back none.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=180)** Otherwise, if it does exist, you'll get the sum variant containing the value.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=185)** Next let's use a print statement to display that return value.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=194)** I'll run the program, and we see that Kayla Barron hasn't flown any missions yet.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=203)** Let's change that.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=205)** There are three common ways to update the entries within a HashMap.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=209)** One is to overwrite an existing key value pair with a new one.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=213)** You could insert a new key value pair into the HashMap if the desired key does not already exist, or when a key does exist, you can update an existing value based on its existing value.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=226)** Let's look at examples of those three ways to update HashMaps in that order.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=232)** First, to overwrite an existing entry, we can simply call the insert method again with the same key.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=239)** To demonstrate that, I'll copy and paste line seven for Kayla Barron's missions flown.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=244)** We'll replace it with a new entry saying Barron has flown one mission.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=248)** I'll run the program.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=252)** And now we see that astronaut Barron has flown one mission.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=256)** The next common way to update a HashMap is to insert a new key value pair if the key doesn't already exist.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=263)** To do that, we'll call the entry method, and we'll pass it in the key we want, in this case, Barron.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=271)** That will return the corresponding entry from the HashMap, which we can then use for in-place modification.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=277)** So on that entry, we'll call the or_insert method, and insert a value of two if the entry does not exist.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=288)** I'll run this program.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=292)** Notice that it still shows Kayla Barron has only flown one mission.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=297)** That's because her entry already existed, so the or_insert method did not do anything to change this value.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=303)** Let's try modifying the key for that entry method to the name of an astronaut that doesn't exist yet.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=311)** Stone, that's me.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=313)** When I run the program now, the output shows that a fourth astronaut has been added to our HashMap for the key Stone with two missions under his belt.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=322)** We still want to send Kayla Barron on another mission, so let's look at the third common way to update a HashMap based on its existing value.
>
> **[5:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=331)** When you call the or_insert method, in addition to inserting a default value if the entry does not already exist, it also returns a mutable reference to the entries value, which we can use to modify it.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=344)** Let's create a new variable named Kayla to capture that reference.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=348)** We'll call the entry method on the missions_flown HashMap to get the entry for Kayla Barron, and use the or_insert method to get a reference to it.
>
> **[6:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=360)** Then, on the next line, we'll use the dereference operator to access that value in memory, and then increment it by one.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=370)** I'll run this program one final time.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=374)** And now we see that Kayla Barron's mission count has increased from one to two.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=380)** As in the previous video on vectors, we only covered a portion of the methods that are available to use with the HashMap.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/hashmaps?u=76281980&t=386)** So I encourage you to look through the standard libraries documentation page to get a sense for everything HashMaps can do.

#### Solution: Count words
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] My solution to the count words challenge has four stages.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=9)** So let's walk through those in sequence.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=11)** The first section on lines, seven through 20 reads the input text file, and then breaks it up into a vector containing the individual words.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=20)** The match expression on line seven checks to make sure the program was passed and input argument for the file to open.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=27)** If it's Some, the second match expression on line eight, leads that file to a string and then the Ok match arm on line nine uses the two lowercase method to convert all the texts to lowercase.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=40)** That way we can ignore capitalization when comparing words.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=45)** If either of those match expressions fail, I print an error message to let the user know what went wrong and then exit the program.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=52)** Line 20 takes the lowercase string of file contents and uses the split white space method, to break it up into individual words.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=61)** That method returns an iterator, so I use the collect method on it to turn the iterator into a vector of string slices.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=69)** Collect is a really useful method when you need to put items from an iterator into a collection.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=74)** So I recommend adding it to your bag of rest programming tricks.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=79)** The next section of code on lines 23 through 26, counts how many times each unique word occurs.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=86)** To do that, I initialize a hash map, which will use each of the words for keys stored as strings slices and the corresponding value will be an integer counting how many times that word occurred.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=97)** The for-loop iterates through the vector of word, if a word already exists in the hash map, then I increment its count value by one.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=106)** Otherwise, if it's a new word that has not been added to the hash map yet, I insert a new entry for it with an initial count value of zero.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=115)** After populating the hash map of word counts, the next section of code searches through the hash map to determine which word or words has the highest count value.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=124)** Lines 29 and 30 initialize variables to keep track of the top words and their count value, then the four loop on the next line iterates through each key value pair in the hash map.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=136)** If a value is greater than the current top count we've seen thus far, it updates the top count variable, then clears and pushes the corresponding word onto the top words vector.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=147)** The LCF clause on line 36, checks to see if the value is the same as the top count variable which indicates we've found another equally used most common word, so it gets pushed onto the top words vector as well.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=162)** Finally, the last section of the program prints a message with the results using a for loop to iterate through the top words vector to print them all if there happened to be multiple top words.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=173)** Now, to demonstrate my solution and show that it works, I'll call cargo run and then pass it in argument for the text file containing Jule's burns' book "From the Earth to the Moon."
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=185)** I'll press Enter to build and run the program, and we see that the top word is 'the' not too exciting but also not totally unexpected.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/solution-count-words?u=76281980&t=195)** If you enjoyed this challenge and want to take it further, rather than just finding the most common word, try enhancing your program to find the top five most commonly used words that will require you to get a bit more creative with how you search the hash map for top words.

> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=11)** Which raises the question, what should you do next?
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=15)** You could learn how to manage and build larger more complex projects using Cargo to create your own modules and crates.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=22)** You can choose to focus on writing code for concurrent or parallel execution, or you might want to learn how to write code that bypasses some of the compilers strict memory checks, known as unsafe code.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=34)** There are countless different directions you can choose to take your learning journey, so I want to leave you with a few resources to help take that next step.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=43)** The Rust website has a fantastic collection of resources for learning about Rust under the learn section.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=50)** One of the most popular resources for new learners in the Rust community is "the book."
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=56)** The entire book, formerly titled "The Rust Programming Language" is available for free, and it walks you through learning Rust from the very beginning with hello world, all the way to building a multithreaded web server.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=69)** The book includes pretty much everything we covered in this course and a lot more.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=74)** Another great resource to use in addition to the book is Rust by Example, which is a huge collection of example code to demonstrate lots of different programming concepts and how to implement them in Rust.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=85)** It's well-organized and serves as a great reference.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=89)** In addition to the book which is great for beginners, the core rust documentation includes several other books that are focused on specific topics, including Cargo, how to write documentation for your own crates, and details on the Rust compiler.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=106)** As you grow more comfortable using Rust in general, you may want to specialize in certain types of applications and there are several books to help you with that too, including how to build effective command line applications, how to build browser native libraries through WebAssembly, and how to write Rust programs to target microcontrollers and other embedded systems.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=127)** Finally, when you get stuck or have questions, you can turn to the Rust community.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=132)** The Rust website has an active users forum where you can go to ask for help and join in the conversation.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=139)** I hope you've enjoyed this course and have a great experience continuing to learn about Rust.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/rust-essential-training/where-to-go-next?u=76281980&t=144)** Thanks for watching and happy programming.
